Tractate Soferim



Chapter 1

It is not permitted to write [the sacred] books,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The scrolls of the Pentateuch used for the lections in public worship.</i> <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These two nouns are added by GRA and M. For Hebrew and other terms, see the Glossary.</i> on skins of ritually unclean cattle or on skins of ritually unclean wild animals. Nor may they<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The parchment sheets of which the scrolls are made up.</i> be sewn with their sinews, or [the parchment rolls of <i>tefillin</i>] be wound with their hair. It is an oral prescription delivered to Moses at Sinai that [all these] shall be written on the skins of ritually clean cattle or ritually clean wild animals, be sewn together with their sinews and [the <i>tefillin</i> rolls] wound with their hair. It is also an oral prescription delivered to Moses at Sinai that [the parchments] shall be ruled with a reed<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">To guide the hand of the scribe, the letters being written under the ruled lines.</i> and the writing on the skin be done with ink. [This latter requirement] has support in Scripture, <i>And I wrote them with ink in the book</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 36, 18.</i>
R. Joshua the grits-dealer was asked: ‘Why is it permitted to write [sacred scrolls] on the skins of <i>nebeloth</i> and <i>ṭerefoth?</i>’ ‘I will give you an analogy,’ R. Joshua replied. ‘This is similar to the case of two men who were condemned to death by the State, one being executed by the king and the other by the executioner. Which of them is superior? He who was executed by the king.’<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nebeloth</i> and <i>ṭerefoth</i> are the effect of an act of God.</i> ‘If so,’ he retorted, ‘they should be permitted to be eaten!’ ‘It is stated,’ replied R. Joshua, ‘<i>Ye shall not eat any</i> nebelah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 14, 21. E.V. <i>of anything that dieth of itself</i>.</i> Scripture has forbidden it, so what can I do for you?’ (From here it is inferred that it is permitted to write on skins of <i>nebelah</i> and on skins of <i>ṭerefah</i> and there is no need to consider the possibility of their having been pierced at the animal’s heart.)<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is enclosed in brackets in some eds. It was a heathen practice to remove the heart from a live animal to be offered as a sacrifice; this, in Rabbinic law, rendered the entire animal, including its skin, forbidden as an idolatrous offering (‘A.Z. 29b, Sonc. ed., p. 145).</i>
Skins<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Before they were dressed; but dressed skins may be bought only from trust-worthy persons who can testify that the dressing had been done in accordance with the law.</i> for the [sacred] books, <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i> may be bought anywhere, and one need not consider the possibility that they might be skins which were pierced at the animal’s heart.
The writing on <i>ḳelaf</i> must be on the side of the flesh, and on <i>gewil</i> it must be on the side of the hair, no deviation from this being permitted. It is an oral prescription delivered to Moses at Sinai that if one changed one for the other, [the scroll] may not be used for the lection [in the statutory services].
[The sacred texts]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in Rule 1.</i> may not be written on <i>difthera</i> or erased paper;<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Others, ‘palimpsest’. GRA omits ‘erased’.</i> nor with coal,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘black pigment’.</i> shoe-black, ink containing gum, vitriol or fruit-juice.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Others, ‘rain-water’, ‘wine-lees’.</i> Vitriol may not be added to the ink.
[The sacred texts] may not be written in [obsolete] Hebrew characters,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The script must be ‘Assyrian’ (the modern Hebrew square characters) and the language ‘the holy tongue’.</i> or in the Aramaic, Median or Greek languages.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">According to Meg. 9a (Sonc. ed., p. 48) it is permitted to write the Torah in Greek although this is not allowed in the case of <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i>.</i> If they were written in any other language or in any other characters they may not be used for the lection [in the statutory services], since they must be written in the Assyrian [script].
It once happened that five elders wrote the Torah for King Ptolemy<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Presumed to be Ptolemy Philadelphus who reigned over Egypt 285-247 <small>B.C.E.</small> He arranged for the Greek Version, the Septuagint, to be made, as narrated in the next paragraph. [The Tractate gives two accounts of the translation, the present one being the later which reflects the harmful effects which the Septuagint had in arresting the development of exegesis among the Hellenistic Jews (cf. Weiss, <i>Dor</i>, I, p. 90).]</i> in Greek, and that day was as ominous for Israel as the day on which the golden calf was made, since the Torah could not be accurately translated.
It also happened that King Ptolemy assembled seventy-two elders and placed them in seventy-two [separate] rooms without telling them the reason for which he had assembled them. He then went to each one of them and said to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘to them’.</i> ‘Write for me [a translation of] the Torah of Moses your master’. The Omnipresent inspired them<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘put counsel in the heart of each one of them’.</i> and the mind of all of them was identical, so that each on his own<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA.</i> wrote the [same translation of the] Torah, introducing [the same] thirteen alterations<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Deviations from the traditional text. In the extant Versions of the Septuagint only some of these are found.</i> as follows: ‘God created in the beginning’.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>In the beginning God created</i> (Gen. 1, 1) the Heb. of which might be misinterpreted to mean that a power named ‘Bereshith’ (in the beginning) created God.</i> ‘And God said I shall make a man in image and likeness.’<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. text reads: <i>Let us make man in our image, after our likeness</i> (Gen. 1, 26). The plural <i>us</i> and <i>our</i> might erroneously suggest a plurality of deities.</i> ‘And He finished on the sixth [day] and rested on the seventh [day].’<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For the Heb.: <i>And He finished on the seventh day</i> (Gen. 2, 2) which could be understood to imply that God did some work on the seventh day.</i> ‘Male and female He created him.’<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>created them</i> (<i>ibid.</i> V, 2) from which it might be inferred that man and woman were, from the first, two separate beings, contrary to <i>ibid.</i> II, 21.</i> ‘Come let Me go down and there confound their language.’<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>let us go down</i> (<i>ibid.</i> XI, 7).</i> ‘And Sarah laughed among her relatives,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. she laughed in the presence of people and therefore incurred censure (<i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 12). The Heb. means ‘within herself’ as Abraham had done when he <i>laughed … in his heart</i> (<i>ibid.</i> XVII, 17) and had not been rebuked.</i> saying.’ ‘For in their anger they slew oxen<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA, H and M. V incorrectly ‘a man’.</i> and in their self-will they digged up a stall.’<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Changing <i>men</i> (<i>ibid.</i> XLIX, 6) to ‘oxen’ and <i>oxen</i> to ‘stall’ in order to remove the stigma of murder from Jacob’s sons.</i> ‘And Moses took his wife and his children and set them upon a carrier<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V and H read ‘carriers of men’.</i> of men.’<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. an animal conformable with the dignity of Moses, instead of <i>ass</i> (Ex. 4, 20).</i> ‘Now the time that the children of Israel dwelt in Egypt and in the land of Canaan and in other lands<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V omits ‘and in other lands’. [The reading of the Septuagint is ‘in the land of Canaan and in the land of Egypt’.]</i> was four hundred and thirty years.’<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of the Heb. which omits ‘in the land of Canaan and in other lands’ (Ex. 12, 40), and implies that all the 430 years were spent in Egypt when, in fact, they could not have dwelt there more than 210 years (cf. Rashi to Meg. 9a).</i> ‘And he sent the elect of the children of Israel.’<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and Meg. 9a but omitted in V. ‘Elect’ is substituted for <i>young men</i> (<i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 5), the former being regarded as more suitable persons for the service.</i> ‘And upon the elect of the children of Israel He laid not His hand.’<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Here also ‘elect’ was substituted for the Heb. for <i>nobles</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 11). The alteration was not essential, but ‘elect’ which was mentioned earlier was preferred. It should be noted that in the total of thirteen alterations, this and the preceding are counted as one.</i> ‘I have not taken one desirable thing<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A ‘desirable thing’ [which occurs in the Septuagint] for the Heb. <i>ass</i> (Num. 16, 15) to avoid the suggestion that Moses did not take an ass but more precious objects.</i> from them.’ ‘The [beast] with small legs.’<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of the Heb. for <i>the hare</i> (Lev. 11, 6) which is <i>arnebeth</i>, so as not to give offence to Ptolemy the name of whose queen was Arnebeth.</i> ‘Which the Lord thy God hath allotted to give light unto all the peoples under the whole heaven.’<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The insertion ‘to give light’ (Deut. 4, 19) removes the possible misunderstanding that the heavenly bodies enumerated in the verse were intended by God to be objects of worship.</i> ‘Which I commanded should not be served.’<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 17, 3 reads <i>which I have commanded not</i>. This might be taken to mean that God did not desire their existence and their creation was consequently due to a power beyond His control. It should be noted that the last two alterations are regarded as one in the enumeration because both deal with heavenly bodies as objects of worship.</i>
[The sacred scrolls] may not be written in gold. It is related that in an Alexandrian<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V and H read ‘a scroll of the Torah of Alexander’ [i.e. Alexander Jannaeus (103-76 <small>B.C.E.</small>) according to Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>, II, p. 55].</i> scroll of the Torah all the divine names were written in gold, and when the matter came before the Sages they decided that it should be stored away.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘it shall be hidden’; not to be used for the lection.</i>
All the Torah is in Hebrew but it contains some Aramaic words.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘translation’ (<i>Targum</i>).</i> What is in Hebrew may not be written in Aramaic and the Aramaic words may not be altered; for example, <i>Jegar-sahadutha</i> and <i>Galeed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 31, 47, ‘the heap of witness’ in Aramaic and Hebrew.</i> Others hold that the rule applies only to <i>Jegar-sahadutha</i> and <i>Galeed</i> because they are two specifically Aramaic and Hebrew expressions respectively.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the other parts, however, although one language should not be changed for another, the scroll is not disqualified if a change was made (cf. N.Y. <i>ad loc.</i>).</i> If Hebrew words were written in Aramaic or Aramaic words in Hebrew, the scroll may not be used for the lection. The Aramaic [and Hebrew] words referred to are <i>Jegar-sahadutha, Galeed</i> and the like.<br>If <i>ha’azinu hashshamayim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Give ear ye heavens</i>, the opening words of Deut. 32, 1-43, which is arranged in the scroll as follows:<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________</i> was arranged like the Song of Moses<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 1-18 written as:<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>or, according to others:<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________<br>_________________</i> or if the Song of Moses was arranged like <i>ha’azinu;</i> if continuous lines were written in broken ones or vice versa; [35<i>b</i>] or if the breaking up was not done according to the rule, the scroll may not be used for the lection.
If one finished [writing nearly] all the Torah and less than a column<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. V reads: ‘If one finished all the column and left over less, one extends the wording so as to make’, etc. A reading in M is ‘if one finished all the book and left less than a column, one extends the writing so as to make’, etc.</i> remained of the text, one need not hesitate to make of this a small column on its own.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The extension of a short passage over a full column is done either by enlarging the characters or, according to others, by making each successive line shorter than the one preceding it.</i>
A [parchment] sheet [of the Torah scroll] containing six columns may be divided into two sheets;<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Each containing three columns.</i> but one of five columns may not be divided, in order that it should not<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and N. Y. H and M read: ‘perhaps one might’.</i> be separated into one of two<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Not less than three columns may be contained in each sheet.</i> and one of three columns. No one is allowed to write [a scroll of the Torah] unless one is able to read.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">M inserts: ‘one is not allowed to write unless [the parchment] was ruled; and if an unruled sheet was written on, it is disqualified’.</i>
A scroll of the Torah that was written by a Sadducee,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">An adherent of a sect which was opposed to the teachings and traditions of the Pharisees. M adds <i>min</i>, a Jewish heretic or Jewish-Christian.</i> an informer,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Against his fellow-Jews to the ruling Roman authorities. M adds <span dir="rtl">משומד</span>, an apostate.</i> a proselyte,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a ‘proselyte settler’ (<i>ger toshab</i>), one who does not observe the commandments but, on account of domicile in the land of Israel, has renounced idolatry.</i> a slave, a woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and M; not in V and H.</i> a madman or a minor may not be used for the lection.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For the unsuitability of the persons enumerated, cf. Giṭ. 45b (Sonc. ed., p. 199).</i> This is the rule: Whosoever cannot act in religious matters on behalf of the public is not permitted to write a scroll of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V reads: ‘everyone who writes it [must be a person who] may act on behalf of the public [in discharging a religious obligation]’.</i>
If an open section was written as closed or vice versa, the scroll must be stored away. What is an open section? One which does not begin at the beginning of a line. And what is a closed section? [One which is separated from what precedes by a vacant] space in the middle of the line. And how much space must be left at the beginning of a line so that a section may be called ‘open’? A space in which a name of three letters may be written.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The view of Maimonides which has been adopted is that space must be left for three words, each of three letters (cf. Y.D. 275, §2).</i> And how much space must be left in the middle of a line so that a section may be called ‘closed’? A space in which a name of three letters may be written. If an entire section was completed at the end of a column, one vacant line must be left at the top [of the next column]; but if a vacant space in which a name of three letters may be written was left at the foot [of the column], one begins [the next column] from the top.

Chapter 2

Sufficient space must be left between words<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘between name and name’.</i> so that they can be distinguished, and between letters so that they be not mixed. If the letters were mixed or a break was made in the middle of a word, the scroll must not be used for the lection in the statutory services.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In V Rule 1 is enclosed within brackets, probably because it is included in the fuller version in the next Rule.</i>
Between lines there must be left the space of a line, between words the space of a letter, between letters that of a hairbreadth, and between columns the space of the width of a thumb. If [a word at]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA.</i> the end of a column was copied<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘made’.</i> at the beginning [of the next column, the scroll] is disqualified. If the letters were mixed or if a break was made in the middle of a word, the lection may not be read from it.
It is permitted to insert<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘place’.</i> between columns [part of] a word of five<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">So N.Y. V, H and M read ‘four’.</i> letters. One may not write two letters within<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">So N.Y. V and H have ‘at the end’.</i> the column and three without;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Because the greater part of the word must appear within the column. So N.Y. V, H and M read ‘and two between the columns’. GRA emends to ‘one at the end of the column and three between the lines’.</i> but it is not so with a short word.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Of two letters (N.Y.). One letter may be written between columns (H).</i> If the [part of a longer word, however, forms] a short word of two<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, H and M, ‘three letters’.</i> letters on its own,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In a noun like <span dir="rtl">שלחן</span> (table), for instance, which consists of four letters, the last two can form the word <span dir="rtl">חן</span> (grace).</i> it is forbidden [to insert it between the columns].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of GRA and N.Y.</i>
In a Torah scroll the space<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">So M, H and N.Y. V repeats ‘space’ unnecessarily.</i> of two finger-breadths must be left between columns, but in scrolls of the Prophets and ordinary Pentateuchs<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Intended for study only and not for public lections.</i> a space of one thumb-breadth only. In the lower margin of a Torah scroll the space of a handbreadth is left, and in the upper margin three fingerbreadths;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">So H in agreement with Men. 30a (Sonc. ed., p. 195). V and M read ‘two parts of a handbreadth’.</i> but in scrolls of the Prophets and ordinary Pentateuchs three finger-breadths in the lower margin and two finger-breadths in the upper. In all these cases,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘here and there’.</i> if it is desired to extend [the margins] this may be done, provided they do not exceed [the space of] the written matter. Between the books<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘between a book and a book’. The Torah scroll consists of five books.</i> in a Torah scroll a vacant space of four lines must be left, and in a scroll of the Prophets three lines.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Both in the case of the major and minor Prophets (N.Y.).</i>
If one finished [writing nearly] all the book<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">So N.Y. V, H and M read ‘books’. The reference is either to one book of the Prophets or to several written in one scroll.</i> and one column of text remained, one need not hesitate to make a small sheet of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. I, Rule 11.</i> At the conclusion of the [last] column [sufficient parchment] must be left to wind [round the scroll]. It is proper<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in the beginning’, ‘at the outset’.</i> to attach a roller to the end of a scroll for winding, but there is no need to attach one at the beginning of a scroll. In a Torah scroll, however, rollers should be attached at both ends. A scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Of the Prophets, having its cylinder at the end of the scroll.</i> is therefore rolled up towards the beginning but a Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Which has cylinders at both ends.</i> towards its<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">So H, M and GRA. V omits the pronoun.</i> centre. A Torah scroll may not be opened by [holding on to] a sheet;<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Owing to its sanctity. It must be opened by manipulating the cylinders. Another reading for <span dir="rtl">פותחין</span> is <span dir="rtl">פוחתין</span> (H, cf. N.Y.) which gives the clause the meaning that when a Torah scroll is used for reading the lection not less than a full sheet, which contains not fewer than three columns, must be kept unrolled throughout the reading.</i> and the height<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘length from top to bottom’.</i> of such a scroll must be six handbreadths.
A sheet of a Torah scroll must contain not less than three columns and not more than eight. As regards the lines [in a scroll] a reason was given [for four different numbers]: corresponding to the journeys,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of H and GRA. V and M have ‘in the journeys’.</i> forty-two; corresponding to the myriads of Israel, sixty; corresponding to the elders of Israel, seventy-two; and corresponding to the reproofs in Deuteronomy, [36<i>a</i>] ninety-eight; all depending on [the size of] the script.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The larger the characters the fewer the number of lines in a column.</i><br>‘Corresponding to the journeys,’ as it is said, <i>And Moses wrote their goings forth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 33, 2. As the expression of writing (<i>wrote</i>) occurs in the same context as the journeys (<i>goings forth</i>), the number of lines in a <i>written</i> column may be the same as the number of the journeys.</i> ‘Corresponding to the myriads of Israel,’ as it is said, <i>Write thou these words … and with Israel;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 34, 27. <i>Write</i> and <i>Israel</i> (numbering sixty myriads) occur in the same context.</i> as Israel numbered sixty myriads so may the lines of the Torah be sixty [in a column]. ‘Corresponding to the elders, seventy-two,’ as it is said, <i>Gather unto Me seventy men</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 11, 16.</i> … <i>there remained … in the camp … of them that were recorded</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 26. The number of the elders is found in the same passage as the expression of writing (<i>recorded</i>, lit. ‘written’).</i> ‘Corresponding to the reproofs, ninety-eight,’ as it is said, <i>If thou wilt not observe to do all the words of this law that are written</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 28, 58, <i>the words of this law</i> refers to the reproofs numbering 98 and occurs with <i>written</i> in the same passage.</i><br>In the case of <i>ḳelaf</i> parchments no limit was prescribed, and one may add [as many lines]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">N.Y. explains, ‘sheets’.</i> as desired, provided only there are not less than three columns [to the sheet].<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the lines in a column, or according to N.Y. the sheets, should not be so many as to leave less than three columns for the last sheet of the scroll.</i>
If a sheet [of a Torah scroll] is worn out, one may not remove two [columns only]<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">So M. N.Y. (cf. Ṭur Y.D. 280) explains, ‘sheets’ instead of ‘columns’.</i> and replace them by two new columns; but three columns should be removed and replaced by three new ones, and the new columns must be written in the same size as the original script.
The width of a column [in a Torah scroll] must be such as to be recognizable at one glance,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. not more than three Heb. words, each of ten letters, could be written in a line. If the column is wider, the reader experiences difficulty in passing from line to line.</i> and in a small scroll not less than a handbreadth. R. Jose b. Judah says: It must not be less than the width of three fingers.
Half the length [of a column]<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Of text. This is exclusive of the upper and lower margins. Others explain, ‘sheet’, instead of ‘column’.</i> must not be greater than its width, nor its width greater than half its length, but they must be exactly alike,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. The reading of V is: ‘he takes the middle course and so does it in’, etc.</i> and this is the most correct procedure.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Some versions omit ‘half’ twice in this paragraph which agrees with B.B. 14a (Sonc. ed., p. 67): ‘A scroll of the Torah should be such that its length does not exceed its circumference nor its circumference its length [when rolled up]’. On this reading, the paragraph deals with the size of the scroll in its entirety.</i>
A Torah scroll may not consist of half <i>gewil</i> and half <i>ḳelaf</i>, but it may consist of half <i>gewil</i> and half <i>ḳelaf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">So Asheri (cf. N.Y.). V and H have the plural of <i>ḳelaf</i>.</i> of deers, although<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">GRA and others. V reads ‘because’.</i> this is not the best procedure.
[A tear in a parchment sheet of a Torah scroll] may not be joined with glue, nor is it permitted to write on a patch, nor may [the sheets] be sewn together on the written side. R. Simeon b. Eleazar said in the name of R. Meir: [A torn sheet] may be joined with glue and it is permitted to write on a patch. It is forbidden, however, to do the sewing<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">GRA, H and M. According to V ‘the writing’.</i> on the written side within but [it must be done] on the outside.<br>A space should be left [unstitched] at the top and at the foot [of the sheets] in order that the scroll be not torn [in use].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">By leaving a part unstitched, the liability to tear when the scroll is rolled up would be reduced. GRA reads this paragraph as follows: ‘It is an oral prescription delivered to Moses at Sinai that a space should be left [unstitched] at the top and at the foot [of the sheets]. This, however, was at once qualified [by the Sages who questioned whether this was such an oral prescription or merely a rule] so that the scroll should not be torn [cf. Meg. 19b, Sonc. ed., pp. 118f]. If a Torah scroll was torn a patch may be placed on its outside’.</i> This, however, was at once qualified by adding: from the foot towards the top and from the top towards the foot. It is an oral prescription delivered to Moses at Sinai that, if a Torah scroll was torn, a patch may be placed on its outside.<br>In the case of the double letters of the alphabet,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The five letters <span dir="rtl">כ׳, מ׳, נ׳, פ׳, צ׳</span> which have a different form as finals.</i> the first ones<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the letters in the non-final form.</i> are written at the beginning and in the middle of a word, and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The final letters.</i> at the end. If this is reversed,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A final letter in the middle or at the beginning or vice versa.</i> the scroll is disqualified. If a scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Other than a Torah scroll.</i> is torn, a patch may be placed on the inside and<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V reads ‘but’.</i> it is not [obligatory to attach it] on the outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The sentence is omitted in H, his reading being ‘if a scroll was torn’ instead of ‘if a Torah scroll was torn’.</i>

Chapter 3

It is permissible to join the Torah and the Prophets in one scroll, so R. Judah; but the Sages say: The Torah and the Prophets must be written in separate scrolls.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">So V, H, M. GRA adopts the reading of B.B. 13b (Sonc. ed., p. 66): ‘It is permissible to fasten the Torah, the Prophets and the Hagiographa [in one scroll]’.</i>
Between a book of the Prophets and another, one should not leave the same empty space as between two books of the Torah, but in each case the space must be that which has been prescribed for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">viz. four lines between books of the Torah, and three lines between books of the Prophets.</i> Furthermore, [a book of the Prophets]<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the preceding book ended at the foot of a column.</i> must begin at the top.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Of the next column, the reason being that, if the books of the Prophets were separated one from the other, the beginning of each book would commence at the top of a column. This does not apply to Torah scrolls which may never be cut up into separate books.</i>
A Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Which contains the five books of Moses.</i> may not be made into the separate books of the Pentateuch, neither into single books nor into two or three joined together, because it is forbidden to reduce a major sanctity<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah scroll in its entirety.</i> to a minor sanctity.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A section of the Pentateuch.</i> From scrolls containing sections of the Pentateuch, however, it is permitted to make up a Torah scroll. When may this be done? When the measurements<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Of all the scrolls that are to be joined together.</i> and the numbers of the lines<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In each column.</i> are alike.
It is not permissible to join together two sections of the Pentateuch or one and a half of them to form a single scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Because it might be mistaken for a proper Torah scroll.</i> But if it was intended<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">When these were written.</i> that additional scrolls be attached to them,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">To complete a Torah scroll.</i> it is permissible.
It is related that Boethus b. Zonin<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">[A pious Jew at the end of the first cent. <small>C.E.</small>, not to be identified with the Boethus named in ARN V.]</i> had eight books of the Prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Joshua, Judges, Samuel, Kings, Isaiah, Jeremiah, Ezekiel and the Twelve Prophets reckoned as one book.</i> joined together in one scroll in accordance with a ruling of R. Eleazar b. ‘Azariah. Others maintain that he had them all in separate scrolls. It is also related that a certain person brought before the Sages a single scroll containing the Torah, the Prophets and the Hagiographa.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">And, as recorded in B.B. 13b (Sonc. ed., p. 66), the Sages ruled that it was a proper scroll.</i>
This<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">That the Torah, Prophets and Hagiographa may be included in one scroll.</i> applies only to a codex,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as a book for study, no one could attribute to it the sanctity of a Torah scroll.</i> but in the case of a scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In that form only the contents could show whether it was a Torah scroll only.</i> the Torah and the Prophets must each consist of a separate parchment.
If a Torah scroll has spaces or stops [to mark] the beginning of verses, it may not be used for the lections.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Because the Torah scroll must conform to the original form without any alterations.</i> So also if a letter has been engraved or joined to another, the scroll may not be used for the lections.
A scroll [some of whose letters] are faded may not be used for the lections.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">So H and GRA. V reads ‘one should not rewrite them’.</i> R. Simeon b. Eleazar says: If the outline [of the effaced letters] is distinguishable, it is permitted [to rewrite them].
A scroll of the Torah in which a whole line is faded may not be used for the lections. If the greater part of a line is faded and the smaller part intact, the use of the scroll is permitted. If a Torah scroll contains an error, it may not be used for the lections. How many [errors disqualify it]? One in a column, is the view of R. Judah. R. Simeon b. Gamaliel says: Even if there be one error in three columns<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of GRA. In V and M, ‘even three errors in a column’. The reading of H is: ‘Three in a column is the view of R. Judah. Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel says: Even if there be one error in one column’.</i> the scroll may not be used for the lections.
Not less than three columns of the Torah scroll [may be unrolled at one time during the reading], but there is no objection to the reading of ten [columns] in one [lection]. It is a religious precept that the most important member<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Of the congregation, or of those who have taken part in the reading.</i> shall roll up the scroll.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Thus showing honour to the Torah.</i> When one rolls up a Torah scroll he should roll it [so that it closes] at the seam<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">So that if the scroll is pulled, only the seam will be affected.</i> and not at [the middle of] a sheet.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Which might be torn by the pull.</i> A Torah scroll should [36<i>b</i>] be rolled up<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and M: ‘it shall not be fastened from within, but it must be fastened from without’.</i> from without,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the back of the written side, while the scroll is being held up by another person who is faced by the written side.</i> but the [band]<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Which is wound around the scroll to prevent it from unrolling.</i> should be tied from within.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The knot facing the written side. In this way, when the next lection is to be read, the knot can be easily undone without having to turn the scroll over.</i><br>When one hands a Torah scroll to another person it must be done with the right hand only, and he who receives it must also do so with the right hand only, because the Torah was delivered at Mount Sinai in this manner, as it is stated, <i>At His right hand was a fiery law unto them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 33, 2.</i><br>R. Shefaṭai<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In Meg. 32a (Sonc. ed., p. 194) the name is Shefaṭiah.</i> said in the name of R. Joḥanan: If one reads the Scripture without a melody<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Indicated in the printed Pentateuch by the traditional cantillation.</i> or learns the Mishnah without a tune,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Which aids the memory.</i> of him Scripture says, <i>Wherefore I gave them also statutes that were not good</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 20, 25. An accompanying melody enlivens one’s studies, and endears the <i>statutes</i> which one learns. If such are described as <i>not good</i> it is because the melody is lacking.</i>
A scribe may not put upon the written part [of a Torah scroll] a reed-pen with ink on it; nor may he take hold of the sheets of parchment<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">So H. V, ‘folds’.</i> and thereby unroll [the scroll]; nor may he grasp them to extract the written sheets;<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Which are rolled up; lit. ‘and bring forth the writing without’.</i> nor may he remove a scroll from its [case] by shaking it out. All these [restrictions] are relaxed with the official revisers of Scripture scrolls. A Torah scroll may not be released from its case while it is lying on one’s lap<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘one shall not slip a Torah scroll from one’s lap and release it’.</i> because this is disrespectful treatment. It is not permissible to place a Torah scroll on one’s knees or lean on it with one’s elbows while reading; so according to the ruling of R. Nehemiah.
A Torah scroll may not be placed over a chair [with its sheets] hanging down while it is being read, in the manner of ordinary documents, because [sacred] books must not be treated with disrespect. The proper procedure is<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘but’.</i> to hold it in the hands<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">By means of a cloth since, according to Rule 16 below, it is forbidden to touch a Torah scroll with the bare hands.</i> with reverence and so read it. [Sacred] books<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Not only Torah scrolls (Maimonides).</i> may not be thrown about<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘from place to place’.</i> nor may they be treated in [any other] disrespectful manner.
A scroll of the Torah may not be placed on a bed and [certainly]<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. N.Y. The addition of ‘certainly’ avoids an anticlimax.</i> not at its foot or under it; nor may one sit on a bed on which a [Torah] scroll [happens to] rest. It is related that R. Eleazar once sat [unwittingly] on a bed on which lay a [Torah] scroll and [when he realized it] he jumped up as if a snake had bitten him. [Respect is shown for the Torah] because Scripture states, <i>Ye shall keep My sabbaths, and reverence My sanctuary</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 19, 30.</i>—it is not the sabbaths that one reverences but Him who commanded [to keep them], nor is one to reverence the sanctuary but Him who commanded [to make] it.<br>It is obligatory to make beautiful <i>ẓiẓith</i>, beautiful <i>mezuzoth</i>, to write a beautiful scroll of the Torah with choice ink, with a fine reed-pen [written] by an expert penman<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">[Reading with Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 254n <span dir="rtl">לבלר אומן</span> for <span dir="rtl">לבלרין נאים</span> (fine pen-men).]</i> on well-finished parchments, on deer skins,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and H, ‘dyed skins’.</i> and to wrap it in precious silks; for Scripture states, <i>This is my God, and I will glorify Him</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 2, <i>I will glorify Him</i> (<span dir="rtl">ואנוהו</span>) is connected homiletically with <span dir="rtl">נאה</span>, ‘beautiful’, and understood as ‘I will serve Him in a beautiful manner’.</i> which means, perform the commandments in a beautiful manner to His glory.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘before Him’.</i> Such is the interpretation of R. Ishmael. R. ‘Aḳiba expounds the text as meaning that one should expatiate on His excellencies. Abba Saul explains: Be like Him;<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He explains <span dir="rtl">ואנוהו</span> as the equivalent of <span dir="rtl">אני והוא</span>, ‘I and He’.</i> as He is merciful and gracious so be thou merciful and gracious.
Foodstuffs may not be treated with disrespect, nor may they be thrown about. One may not sit on a basket containing dates or dried figs;<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Because they might be crushed and spoilt.</i> but he may sit on a basket which contains pulse or on a cake of pressed figs, because it is customary to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Pulse or dried figs are not spoilt by sitting on them.</i> Foodstuffs may not be used as a support<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For any object.</i> or as a covering, nor may foodstuffs be eaten with the aid of foodstuffs,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Using them, e.g., in place of a spoon.</i> unless [the latter] are fit to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Immediately after use and would not be thrown away.</i> R. Eliezer b. Jacob says: Any fruit which has a stem or stalk<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Whereby it can be lifted if it should happen to fall into the contents of the vessel which it covered.</i> may be used as a covering.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since it would not be necessary to throw it away after use.</i> R. Simeon b. Gamaliel says: Any foodstuff which requires processing may be used as a covering.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In its raw state, since its use causes no deterioration.</i> R. Simeon b. Gamaliel also says: Any foodstuff which has a protection<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">e.g. a shell.</i> may be used as a covering. One who writes [Torah] scrolls may not turn over a sheet on its face<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘scroll’, with the writing downwards to protect it from falling dust while it is drying.</i> because this is disrespectful treatment.
If one finished [writing nearly] all the [Torah] scroll and left over one column, there is no objection to his arranging it on a small sheet [on its own].<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. I Rule 11 and II Rule 5.</i><br>Foodstuffs may not be used as a covering.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following are repeated from Rule 14.</i> R. Meir says: Any fruit which has a husk may be used as a covering. R. Judah says: Any fruit which has a stem or a stalk may be used as a covering.
Foodstuffs may not be eaten with the aid of foodstuffs unless they are consumed simultaneously. If a scroll cannot be covered with a cloth,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘on which there is no’. To protect the wet ink from dust while it is drying.</i> it may be turned over on its writing.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This qualifies what was stated in Rule 14 in accordance with ‘Erub. 98a.</i> R. Joḥanan said: Whoever holds a Torah scroll without a covering<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘naked’; this is regarded as disrespectful.</i> will be buried without a covering.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. a shroud.</i> Buried without a covering! Is this thinkable? It means, Without the covering protection of that religious performance.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">(65). The reverential treatment of the Torah scroll is accounted an act of piety.</i>

Chapter 4

The following divine names may not be erased,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he who writes’.</i> viz. <i>Alef-lamed</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Pronounced <i>’El</i> (God).</i> <i>Alef-daleth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The first two letters of the divine name <i>Alef-daleth-nun-yod</i> (Lord).</i> <i>Yod-he</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The first half of the Tetragrammaton and also constituting a name of God, <i>Yah</i>.</i> <i>Shaddai</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Almighty.</i> <i>Ẓeba’oth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">[Lord of] hosts.</i> <i>’Eheyeh ’asher ’Eheyeh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">‘I am that I am’ (cf. Ex. 3, 14).</i> R. Jose says: <i>Ẓeba’oth</i> is a secular noun.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">And may be erased.</i> R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: The Aguddah<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">[H reads <span dir="rtl">חגירה</span> in agreement with j. Meg. 71d.]</i> family, who were scribes in Jerusalem, used to erase <i>Ẓeba’oth</i>, because they treated it as a secular noun, as it is stated, <i>Captains of</i> hosts <i>shall be appointed at the head of the people</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 20, 9. Here <span dir="rtl">צבאית</span> refers to the army of Israel.</i>
If one writes <i>Alef-lamed</i> of the four letters [of <i>Alef-lamed-waw-he</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘God of’.</i> or <i>Yod-he</i> of the four letters [of <i>Yod-he-waw-he</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Tetragrammaton.</i> they may not be erased, because there are divine names corresponding to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="rtl">אל</span> and <span dir="rtl">יה</span>.</i> <i>Shin-daleth</i> of <i>Shin-daleth-yod</i> (<i>Shaddai</i>) or <i>Ẓadi-beth-alef</i> of <i>Ẓadi-beth-alef-waw-taw</i> (<i>Ẓeba’oth</i>) or <i>Alef-he</i> of <i>Alef-he-yod-he</i> (<i>’Eheyeh</i>) may be erased, because there are no divine names corresponding to them.
All prefixes and suffixes connected with a divine name may be erased. [Prefixes such as] <i>in</i> the Lord, <i>like</i> the Lord, <i>to</i> the Lord, <i>and</i> the Lord [represented respectively by the letters] <i>beth, kaf, lamed, waw</i> and suffixes such as <i>our</i> God, <i>your</i> God, <i>their</i> God [represented respectively by the letters] <i>nun-waw, kaf-mem, he-mem</i> and others of the same kind, may be erased. Some Sages say: The <i>kaf-mem</i> of ‘your [God]’<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">And so also all other suffixes (cf. Y.D. 276, §9), because the divine name which precedes the suffix imparts sanctity to it (N.Y.).</i> must not be erased.
All ordinary nouns the letters of which are the same as those of a divine name may be erased.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since they possess no sanctity</i> [For instance in] <i>For God doth know that in the day ye eat thereof, your eyes shall be opened, and ye shall be as God</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 3, 5.</i> the first [divine name] is sacred and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>as God;</i> according to the Targum it means ‘as angels.’</i> is secular; in <i>For the Lord your God, He is God of gods</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 10, 17.</i> the first<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the first two, <i>your God, He is God</i>.</i> is sacred and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>of gods</i>, signifying judges or princes.</i> is secular.
In <i>Lord of lords</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 10, 17.</i> the first is sacred and the second is secular; in <i>the God of Abraham</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 31, 53.</i> it is sacred; in <i>the God of Nahor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Nahor did not worship the true God.</i> it is secular; in <i>the God of their father</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Terah (their father) was an idolater.</i> it is secular. In <i>Thou shalt not revile God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 22, 27.</i> [the noun may bear] a sacred or secular meaning.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It may denote God or judges.</i> R. Simeon maintains that the noun is sacred.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">V inserts ‘as it says’ within brackets, the words being redundant. [H omits and reads ‘Ishmael’ instead of ‘Simeon’.]</i> In <i>Forasmuch as I have seen thy face, as one seeth the face of God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 33, 10.</i> the noun is secular;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Because it refers to an angel or prince.</i> in <i>God’s camp</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXII, 3.</i> the noun is sacred.
All the names [signifying God] mentioned in connection with Abraham are sacred, except one which is secular, viz. where it is stated, <i>And he said: My Lord, if now I have found favour in thy sight</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30a</sup><i class="footnote">(30a) <i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 3. <i>My lord</i> was addressed to the leader of the three angels.</i> Others say that it is sacred.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>My Lord</i> was addressed to God.</i> In <i>And it came to pass, when God caused me to wander</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XX, 13.</i> the name is secular.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. By ‘God’ is meant princes or rulers who were the cause of Abraham’s wanderings.</i> R. Ḥanina the brother<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">[He reads ‘nephew’ in agreement with Shebu. 33b (Sonc. ed., p. 205). The correct form of the name is Ḥananiah, Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 385.]</i> of R. Joshua maintains that it is sacred [since the verse means] that, had it not been for God,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Who protected him.</i> they<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The wicked rulers.</i> would long ago have <i>caused me to wander</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The verse will accordingly bear the following interpretation: <i>And it came to pass, when they</i> (the wicked men) <i>caused me to wander, God of my father’s house was with me</i> (to help me). [For various interpretations of the verse, cf. Midrash Rabbah, Genesis, LII, §11 (Sonc. ed., I, p. 457).]</i>
All [divine] names mentioned in connection with Lot are secular<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">There is only one secular name in the Lot passages. The Tetragrammaton, which is of course sacred, occurs twice. The word ‘all’ is not intended to be taken literally (cf. N.Y.).</i> except the last, viz. <i>And Lot said unto them: Oh, not so my Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 19, 18. Lot’s address to the angels is taken to end at <i>not so</i>, and then his petition to God begins, <i>My Lord, behold</i>, etc. [So the Targum and Shebu. 35b (Sonc. ed., pp. 205f).]</i> All [divine] names mentioned in the story of Micah<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Judg. 17f.</i> are secular. R. Jose says: When they begin with <i>Yod-he</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Tetragrammaton.</i> they are sacred, but when with <i>Alef-lamed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The word for ‘God’.</i> they are secular except in the phrase <i>the house of God was in Shiloh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 31.</i> All the [divine] names which occur in the story of Naboth are sacred except in the sentence <i>Naboth did curse god</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to one of the strange gods introduced by Jezebel. E.V. <i>God</i>.</i> <i>and king</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">1 Kings 21, 13.</i> All the [divine] names which occur in the narrative of Gibeah of Benjamin<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Judg. 20f.</i> are secular according to R. Eliezer, but R. Joshua says: They are sacred. R. Eliezer said to him, ‘Is it possible that the Omnipresent would promise [victory]<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">According to Judg. 20, 18, 23 God told Israel to wage war against Benjamin, which implied that they would be victorious.</i> and not fulfil?’<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Israel was defeated on both occasions (<i>ibid.</i> 21, 25).</i> R. Joshua replied, ‘The Omnipresent promises and fulfils’.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Israelites did not appreciate that on the first two occasions God only told them that they may go to war but promised no victory. Only on the third occasion were they assured that Benjamin would be delivered into their hands (<i>ibid.</i> XX, 28).</i> [37<i>a</i>]
In <i>I will tell</i> [<i>’El</i>] <i>of the decree</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 2, 7.</i> the word <i>’El</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Heb. <span dir="rtl">אל</span> with a difference of vowels may mean ‘God’ or ‘to’.</i> is sacred;<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The sense of the text being ‘I will tell God of the decree’.</i> but the Sages maintain that it is secular.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The text means, as in E.V., <i>I will tell of the decree</i>.</i> In <i>’el God in Ẓion</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 84, 8.</i> the first is secular<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>’El</i> meaning <i>before</i> (E.V.).</i> and the second is sacred. In <i>When he should go ’el God in judgment</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Job 34, 23.</i> the first is secular and the second [<i>’Elohim</i>] is sacred. In the case of <i>Immanuel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 7, 14.</i> and <i>Immanu’el</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VIII, 8.</i> the first is secular<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Being the name of a child.</i> and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘God is with us’.</i> is sacred. The first must not be divided<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Into two words, since it forms a single name.</i> but the second is divided.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Because it consists of two separate words, <span dir="rtl">עמנו</span> (with us) <span dir="rtl">אל</span> (God). The reading adopted here is that of H and N.Y.</i>
[In the verses] <i>And it came to pass when they</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Foreign princes or rulers. E.V. <i>God</i>.</i> <i>caused me to wander, God</i>, etc.,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 20, 13. Cf. Rule 6 above.</i> and <i>Which god</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>who is a God</i>.</i> <i>is like unto Thee</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Micah 7, 18.</i> the first<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The pronoun ‘they’ in Gen. 20, 13 and <i>god</i> in Micah 7, 18.</i> are sacred and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>God</i> in Gen. and <i>Thee</i> in Micah.</i> are secular. [The divine name in] <i>Samaria shall bear her guilt, for she hath rebelled against her God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 14, 1.</i> is sacred. R. Nathan says: [In the phrase] <i>in the house of his God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IX, 8.</i> it is sacred. In <i>therefore turn thou to thy God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XII, 7.</i> it is sacred. In <i>God standeth in the congregation of God; in the midst of the judges</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. has the divine name.</i> <i>He judgeth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 82, 1.</i> it is used as sacred<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>God standeth</i>.</i> and secular.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the sense of ‘judges’.</i> In <i>God delivereth me to</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Heb. <span dir="rtl">אל</span> (to) which has the same consonants as the word for ‘God’.</i> <i>the ungodly</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Job 16, 11.</i> the first is sacred and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>’el</i> in the sense of ‘to’.</i> is secular. In <i>For hath any said unto God: I have borne</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXIV, 31.</i> the first [<i>’el</i>] is secular and the second sacred.<br>R. Eleazar the son of R. Jose the Galilean said:<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V and H read ‘and R. Eleazar … said’.</i> [In the verses] <i>And let my cry have no</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Heb. <i>’al</i>, the same consonants as for the name of God.</i> <i>resting-place;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Job 16, 18.</i> <i>It is in the power</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 31, 29. The Heb. for <i>power</i> (<i>’el</i>) equals the divine name.</i> <i>of my hand; And there shall be nought in the power of thy hand;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 28, 32.</i> <i>When it is in the power of thy hand;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 3, 27.</i> <i>Unto death</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> II, 18; <i>unto</i> is <i>’el</i> in Heb.</i> all [the words whose consonants are the same as those for God] are secular. In <i>God hath given command to speed me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">2 Chron. 35, 21.</i> it is sacred. In <i>Forbear thee from meddling with God, who is with me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> it is sacred according to R. Jose b. Judah. In <i>O God, the proud are risen up against me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 86, 14.</i> it is sacred but the reader must pause in reading [after <i>God</i>]:<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of the pause, the adjective <i>proud</i> might erroneously be taken as qualifying God.</i> <i>God, the proud are risen up against me</i>.<br>[The terms] merciful and gracious, long-suffering and abounding in lovingkindness, king, kings, exalted, great, Most High, righteous and upright, pious, perfect, mighty, may be erased.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Although attributed to God, they do not possess the sanctity of the divine name.</i> He who curses himself or his neighbour by [any of] these<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Using any of these attributes instead of the divine name.</i> incurs guilt. [If he curses] heathens or the dead no guilt<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">So M and N.Y. V and H read ‘guilty of one [offence]’.</i> is incurred. [If he curses] a judge or a prince he incurs twofold guilt;<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since a judge or prince must not be cursed as any other person, and as men holding offices of responsibility.</i> according to others he incurs threefold guilt for cursing a prince.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The two mentioned in the preceding note, and a third because the Heb. word for ‘judge’ in the text is identical with the divine name.</i> If a person curses his father or mother with the Tetragrammaton<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the distinguished name’.</i> he is liable to the penalty of stoning,<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Lev. 24, 14. Stoning is one of the major penalties imposed by a court.</i> but if only with the attributes he is liable to a warning.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. he is guilty of transgressing a negative commandment, the penalty of which is scourging with thirty-nine stripes.</i>

Chapter 5

If one wrote a sacred name twice,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he who writes a name of two sacred names’. H omits ‘a name of’.</i> he retains the first and erases the latter.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The first is retained because it was properly written; the second is erased because it was written in error.</i> R. Judah says: If the first occurs at the end of a column, he erases the first and retains the second.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">It spoils the appearance to leave a blank at the beginning of the top line of a column.</i> [If he wrote] a secular noun twice,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘two secular nouns’.</i> he retains the first and wipes away the latter.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While the ink is still wet.</i> R. Judah says: If the first was at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and H, ‘at the top’.</i> of a column, he wipes away the first and retains the latter.
If one writes <i>Yehudah</i> and omits the <i>daleth</i>, he inserts<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘suspends’ between the <i>waw</i> and the <i>he</i>.</i> it above the line. If he intended to write the Tetragrammaton<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The four letters <i>Yod-he-waw-he</i>, lit. ‘the name’.</i> and wrote <i>Yehudah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="rtl">יהודה</span>.</i> instead,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of GRA and H. V omits ‘if he intended to write’,</i> he alters the <i>daleth</i> into a <i>he</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is done by adding a short vertical line to the <i>daleth</i>.</i> and wipes away the final letter <i>he</i>. If it was required to write <i>Yehudah</i> and he intended to write the Tetragrammaton, although he [inadvertently] inserted the <i>daleth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">So H. V reads: ‘he did not write in it’.</i> he erases it<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Following GRA. V and H ‘detains it’.</i> and writes another <i>Yehudah</i>.
If it was required to write the Tetragrammaton but he intended to write <i>Yehudah</i>, although he did not insert the <i>daleth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">So that the Tetragrammaton was written correctly but unintentionally so.</i> he must wipe away [the entire word] and re-write the Tetragrammaton. R. Judah says: He should go over it with the reed-pen and so sanctify it. [The Sages] said to him: This is not the best procedure.
He who writes the divine name and made an error [by omitting] one letter should suspend it above [the line].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Neither erasure nor wiping away of the written words is necessary.</i> R. Simeon of Shezur says: All the name may be suspended [if omitted] but a single letter of it may not be suspended. If one erred [by omitting] all the name, he should suspend it between the lines, according to the view of R. Judah. R. Meir says: He wipes away the writing that precedes and writes the name on the part which he wiped away. R. Simeon b. Eleazar said in the name of R. Meir: The name only may be suspended but the Tetragrammaton must not be written on an erasure. R. Ḥanina b. ‘Aḳabia says: The whole sheet<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In which the omission of the Tetragrammaton had occurred.</i> must be stored away.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As with all sacred objects that are worn out or decaying, or are for any other reason unfit for further use. GRA revised the wording of this Rule to conform in the main with the text in Men. 30b (Sonc. ed., p. 196): ‘If the Tetragrammaton was omitted in error [and a number of the words following it already written], what had been written is erased, the erased words are suspended [above the line] and the Tetragrammaton is inserted in the space of the erasure; so R. Judah. R. Meir said: He wipes the writing away and writes [the Tetragrammaton] in the space. R. Jose said: Even the Tetragrammaton may be suspended [above the line]. R. Simeon of Shezur said: It is permissible to suspend all the Tetragrammaton [above the line] but not a part of it. R. Simeon b. Eleazar said in the name of R. Meir: The Tetragrammaton may not be written in a space which has been erased or wiped away, nor may it be suspended [above the line]; but the entire sheet is removed and stored away’.</i>
If one wrote three<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V ‘twice’.</i> times <i>Alef-lamed</i> by mistake in the phrase <i>God delivereth me to the ungodly</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Job 16, 11. The text has the word twice, the first being the divine name and the second the preposition; inadvertently he wrote it three times, intending the first two to stand for ‘God’ (thus sanctifying both) and the third for ‘to’.</i> he retains the first and erases the second.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. The first occurrences of <i>’el</i> were meant to be the divine name and Rule 1 above applies. By quoting an illustration from Job, the inference to be drawn is that the regulation with respect to a Torah scroll holds good also of the Prophets and Hagiographa.</i>
When one is writing the Tetragrammaton he may not respond even if the king greets him.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">All his attention must be concentrated on the sanctity of the divine name.</i> If he was about to write two or three divine names successively,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">E.g. <i>The Lord, the Lord, God</i> in Ex. 34, 6.</i> he may pause between them and respond. After dipping the reed-pen in ink, he may not start with the letters of a divine name<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Because a pen full of ink may cause a blot.</i> but with the preceding letter.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Which, if blotted, can be erased or wiped away.</i> One who erases a single letter of a divine name transgresses a negative commandment. R. Simeon said:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Citing the Scriptural basis of the regulation.</i> On account of this it is stated, <i>Ye shall destroy their name out of that place. Ye shall not do so unto the Lord your God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 12, 3f, i.e. the name of God must not be erased.</i>
It is permissible, however, to wipe away ink that dropped upon the writing,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Even of a divine name.</i> since one’s intention is only to rectify it.
If one omits a line in error, he may not suspend it between the lines, but inserts it in close proximity<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘opposite’.</i> to the place<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and H omit ‘inserts in … place of’.</i> of the error by erasing one [adjacent line] and inserting two<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The one omitted and the one erased.</i> in its place, or [by erasing] two lines and writing three;<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The two erased and the one omitted.</i> provided only that he does not erase three lines.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In order to insert more than one missing line. The erasure of one or two lines is sufficient for the insertion of the omitted line without undue pressure on the space between the lines; but the treatment of more than one line in this manner would disfigure the entire column (N.Y.).</i>
It is not permissible to make a scroll of the <i>Shema‘</i> or of the <i>Hallel</i> for children,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Extracts from Scripture may not be made for study by children.</i> but if the scribe intends to add to it, it is permissible.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘but if’ to ‘permissible’ is an addition by GRA.</i> R. Judah permits the writing of a scroll from <i>In the beginning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 1.</i> to the story of the flood,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. to Gen. 6, 8. In each instance the extract is a connected theme.</i> and from the beginning of Leviticus to <i>And it came to pass on the eighth day;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 9, 1.</i> but in the case of all the other books [of Scripture the writing of extracts is] forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Only in the case of the Pentateuch were the two extracts mentioned allowed to be written for the instruction of children. V adds here, ‘if he intends to add to it, it is permitted’.</i>
The following names are to be divided:<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Although each represents one name, it is to be written as two words.</i> Beth-el,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">e.g. Gen. 12, 8.</i> Beth-’awen,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 4, 15, X, 15.</i> Beer-sheba,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">E.g. Gen. 21, 31.</i> Ẓofenath-pa’neaḥ, [37<i>b</i>] Poṭi-fera,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XLI, 45. E.V. <i>Zaphenathpaneah</i> and <i>Poti-phera</i>.</i> Ben-’oni,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXV, 18.</i> Yedid-yah,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 12, 25. E.V. <i>Jedidiah</i>.</i> Halelu-yah.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">E.g. Ps. 111, 1, ‘praise ye the Lord’. E.V. prints it as one word. V and H add: <span dir="rtl">וחרה אף וחרה אפי</span>, <i>and the anger</i> [<i>of the Lord</i>] <i>was kindled, My wrath shall wax hot</i> (Ex. 22, 23; Num. 11, 10).</i> R. Jose says: These must not be divided.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">But written as one word.</i> All, however, agree that there must be no breaking up of ‘Ammi’el,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A name meaning ‘God is with me’ (e.g. Num. 13, 12). Another reading is <span dir="rtl">עזיאל</span>, ‘God is my strength’ (e.g. Ex. 6, 18).</i> ‘Amminadab,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A name meaning ‘my kinsman is noble’ (e.g. <i>ibid.</i> 23).</i> Ẓuri’el,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A name meaning ‘my Rock is God’ (Num. 3, 35).</i> Ẓurishaddai.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">A name meaning ‘my Rock is Shaddai’ (cf. Num. 1, 6).</i>
The following names may not be broken up: Yisra’el, Ga’tham,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A name occurring in Gen. 36, 11, 16, 1 Chron. 1, 36. E.V. <i>Gatam</i>.</i> Poṭifar, Nebukadneẓẓar.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">V and H unnecessarily repeat <span dir="rtl">צמיאל, עוריאל, צורישדי</span>.</i>
If a person writes a divine name on his body, he must neither bathe nor anoint himself<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">So that the name be not washed away.</i> (nor stand in an unclean place.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Out of respect.</i> If he must perform an obligatory immersion,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As, e.g., after a period of ritual impurity.</i> he should wind a reed about it,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">To prevent the name from coming into contact with the water which might wash it away.</i> descend and perform an immersion. R. Jose said: He may at all times descend and perform an immersion in the ordinary way)<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The words in brackets are inserted by GRA who follows Shab. 120b (Sonc. ed., p. 597). V and H read: R. Jose permits, provided, etc.</i> provided he does not rub it off.
[If one wrote a divine name] on the horn of a cow or on the legs of a bed,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">For magical purposes.</i> he scrapes it off<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">[The correct reading is perhaps <span dir="rtl">גודדד</span> for <span dir="rtl">גוררד</span>, ‘he chops off (the horn or leg)’.]</i> and stores it away. [If he wrote it] on a stone, he detaches it and stores it away.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">From respect for the divine name.</i>
If the seam of a sacred scroll was broken for the first time,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘one’.</i> it may be sewn up again;<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">On the undamaged part of the sheet, near the writing, although the margins between the columns are thereby reduced.</i> if a second time, it may be sewn up again; if a third time, it must not be sewn up again.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Because the margins would become too small.</i> If one cuts off a strip of a new scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘scrolls’.</i> it may [be used for ordinary purposes],<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Being new, the scroll had not yet been consecrated.</i> but if it is cut off an old scroll,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Which has been consecrated by the script.</i> [its use is] forbidden. Worn-out scrolls, phylacteries and wraps of scrolls must be stored away.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Having once been consecrated, they may no longer be used for ordinary purposes.</i>
It is related of R. Ḥalafta that, when he visited Rabban Gamaliel, he found him sitting at his table with the Targum of Job<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This must have been an earlier work than the extant Targum. [The anecdote is related in Shab. 115a (Sonc. ed., p. 565) with some variants.]</i> in his hand and reading it. He said to him, ‘Master, will you permit me to tell you what my eyes saw?’ ‘Tell me,’ he replied. ‘I was watching Rabban Gamaliel the Elder, your father’s<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">V rightly corrects ‘mother’s’ to ‘father’s’.</i> father, while he was sitting at the side of a building [in course of construction] on the Temple Mount, when a Targum of Job was brought to him. “Remove a course of stones,” he ordered the builder,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">V incorrectly ‘his children’.</i> “and bury this under it.” ’ R. Jose b. R. Judah said: They overturned a tub of mortar upon it.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is added by GRA from Shab. 115a.</i> Rabbi said: There are two objections to the statement—firstly, how could mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Which is composed of earth and water.</i> be available on the Temple Mount?<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Where only a mixture of sand and lime was used.</i> Further, it is not permissible to destroy such writings<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Books like the Targum of Job whose use was forbidden on account of their exclusion from the Canon.</i> with one’s own hands! [They should not be deposited in an unclean place] but in a deserted spot where they decay of their own accord.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The objection to a written, as against an oral, Targum at the time was the fear that the same sanctity might be attached to it as to the Hebrew text.</i>
If one lends a Torah scroll to his friend, the latter may not lend it to another person.
