If one deposits a Torah scroll with his friend, the latter must unroll it once in twelve months.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is inserted by GRA following B.M. 29b (Sonc. ed., p. 182).</i> If he is capable of reading it, he may do so,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">While the scroll is being unrolled in accordance with this Rule.</i> but if he unrolled it for his own benefit,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">So corrected by GRA.</i> reading it is forbidden. Symmachos said: He unrolls a new scroll once in thirty days and an old one once in twelve months. R. Eliezer b. Jacob says:<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘and an old’ to ‘says’ is added by GRA from B.M. 29b.</i> Both a new and an old scroll [should be unrolled] once in twelve months.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In the course of the discussion in B.M. 29b this is amended to ‘thirty days’ to differentiate the opinion of R. Eliezer from that of the first authority who is quoted above.</i>
All occurrences of the name Solomon in the Song of Songs are sacred<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to God in the Rabbinical interpretation of the Book.</i> except one which is secular. Which one is it? <i>Behold, it is the litter of Solomon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 3, 7.</i> Others say: <i>Thou, O Solomon, shalt have the thousand</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VIII, 12.</i>
All references to ‘kings’ in Daniel are secular except one which is sacred. Which one is it? <i>O king unto whom the King</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Understood as referring to God.</i> <i>of kings, the God of heaven, hath given the kingdom, the power, and the strength, and the glory</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Dan. 2, 37. E.V. <i>O king, king of kings, unto whom</i>, etc.</i> Others say: <i>My Lord</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Interpreted as an invocation to God in Shebu. 35b (Sonc. ed., p. 207).</i> <i>the dream be to them that hate thee, and the interpretation thereof to thine adversaries</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IV, 16.</i>

Chapter 6

A scribe must provide a distinguishing mark<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The traditional mark is an inverted <i>nun</i>.</i> for the section<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V reads ‘a prescribed space at the opening’.</i> beginning <i>And it came to pass when the ark set forward</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 10, 35. The section consists of the two verses, <i>ibid.</i> 35f.</i> both at its beginning and at its end, because it is a book on its own. Others maintain that its proper place is in the section of the setting forward of the standards.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 2.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This Rule is partly a repetition of the preceding, since the end of the section, <i>ibid.</i> X, 35f, is followed by the beginning of <i>And the people were as murmurers</i> (<i>ibid.</i> XI, 1); and partly incorrect since no mark is inserted at the end of the latter section. The entire Rule is to be deleted according to GRA.</i> The scribe must leave a prescribed space at the opening of the section which begins <i>And the people were as murmurers;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XI, 1.</i> [and not only] at its beginning [but also] at its end, because it is a book on its own. Others hold that its proper place is in the section of the setting forward of the standards.
Ten [letters or groups of letters] in the Torah are marked by dots:<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The dots are placed above the letters. Cf. above, ARN XXXIV, p. 165 where the Rabbinical explanations are given.</i> In <i>the Lord judge between me and thee</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 16, 5.</i> there is a dot on the <i>beth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and H ‘the <i>yod</i>’.</i> of <i>ubeneka</i> (and between thee). In <i>and they said unto him</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 9.</i> there are dots on <i>alef-yod-waw</i> of <i>’elaw</i> (unto him). In <i>and he knew not when she lay down, nor when she arose</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIX, 33.</i> the [second] <i>waw</i> in <i>ubekumah</i> (nor when she arose) is marked with a dot. In <i>and kissed him</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXIII, 4.</i> the entire word <i>wayyishshaḳehu</i> is marked by dots. In <i>and his brethren went to feed their father’s flock</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXVII, 12.</i> the letters of <i>’eth</i> (the mark of the defined accusative) are dotted. In <i>whom Moses and Aaron numbered</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 3, 39.</i> the word <i>Aaron</i> is marked with dots. In <i>or be in a journey afar off</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IX, 10.</i> the <i>he</i> in <i>reḥoḳah</i> (afar off) has a dot. In <i>and we have laid waste even unto Nopha, which</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXI, 30.</i> the <i>resh</i> in <i>’asher</i> (which) has a dot. In <i>and a several tenth part</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIX, 15.</i> in the section dealing with the first day of the festival of Tabernacles, the second <i>waw</i> in <i>we’issaron</i> (and a tenth part) has a dot. In <i>the secret things belong unto the Lord our God; but the things that are revealed belong unto us and to our children for</i> ever,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 29, 28. [H more correctly, ‘unto us and to our children and the <i>‘ayin</i> in <span dir="rtl">עד</span>’. Cf. Sanh. 43b (Sonc. ed., p. 285, n. 4) and ARN above, p. 166.]</i> the <i>‘ayin</i> in <i>‘ad</i> (for) has a dot.
R. Simeon b. Laḳish said: Three scrolls of the Torah were found in the Temple court:<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Presumably when the text of Scripture was about to be fixed.</i> the <i>Ma‘on</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V has <span dir="rtl">מענה</span>. This and the following two names were derived from the peculiar word which distinguished each scroll.</i> scroll, the <i>Za’aṭuṭë</i> scroll, and the <i>Hu’</i> scroll. In one of these they found the expression of <i>ma‘on</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘dwelling place’, the noun being in the masculine form.</i> and in the other two it was written, <i>The eternal God is me‘onah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 33, 27, the same noun in the feminine.</i> (a dwelling place), so they adopted the reading of the two scrolls and discarded that of the one scroll. In another of the scrolls they found it written, <i>And he sent the za‘aṭuṭë</i> (nobles) <i>of the children of Israel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 24, 5.</i> and in the other two they found written <i>And he sent na‘arë</i> (the young men of) <i>the children of Israel</i>, so they retained the reading of the two and abandoned that of the one. In one of the scrolls <i>hu</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The third person sing. masc. which is used in the Pentateuch for <i>hi’</i>, third person fem. sing. pronoun.</i> was written eleven times, but in the other two <i>hi</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The correct feminine form, being exceptions to the usual written form. A list of these is given in ARN XXXIV, above p. 167.</i> was written eleven times, so they adopted the reading of the two and discarded that of the one.
Three expressions of <i>lo’</i> are written <i>lamed-alef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘not’.</i> but read as <i>lamed-waw</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Which means ‘to him, has’.</i> They are: <i>’asher lo’</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="rtl">לא</span>, lit. ‘which not’ but read <span dir="rtl">לו</span> ‘which has’.</i> <i>jointed legs above their feet;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 11, 21.</i> <i>’asher lo’ ḥomah;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXV, 30, <i>lamed-alef</i>, meaning ‘which has no wall’ but read as <i>lamed-waw</i> ‘which has a wall’. E.V. <i>in a walled city</i>.</i> and <i>’asher lo’ ye‘adah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 21, 8. The written form with <i>lamed-alef</i> means ‘who hath not espoused her’, but the reading with <i>lamed-waw</i> means, as E.V., <i>who hath espoused her to himself</i>.</i>
The following [occurrences of <i>lamed-alef</i> (not)] which have to be read as <i>lamed-waw</i> (to, by, has) are found in the Prophets and Hagiographa: <i>multiply;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The introductory word of 1 Sam. 2, 3 in which occurs the phrase <i>and by Him are actions weighed</i> according to the <i>ḳerë</i>, but the <i>kethib</i> reads ‘and actions are not weighed’.</i> <i>Hushai;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. 2 Sam. 16, 18, where the <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>his will I be</i> and the <i>kethib</i> ‘I will not be’.</i> <i>said;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. 2 Kings 8, 10. Here the <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>say unto him, thou shalt surely recover</i>, and the <i>kethib</i> ‘say, thou shalt not surely recover’.</i> <i>thou hast multiplied;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Isa. 9, 2.The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>Thou hast increased their joy</i>, and the <i>kethib</i> ‘Thou hast not increased joy’.</i> <i>their affliction;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Isa. 63, 9. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>In all their affliction He was afflicted</i> (lit. there was affliction to Him) and the <i>kethib</i> ‘in all their affliction He was not an adversary’.</i> <i>He slay me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Job 13, 15. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>yet will I trust Him</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘I have nothing to hope for’.</i> <i>I keep silence;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Job 41, 4. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>would I keep silence</i> (to him), the <i>kethib</i> ‘I would not keep silence’.</i> <i>as the</i> (<i>wandering</i>) <i>sparrow;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Prov. 26, 2. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>shall come home</i> (to him), the <i>kethib</i> ‘shall not come home’.</i> <i>his friends;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Prov. 19, 7. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>they turn against him</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘they do not turn’.</i> <i>Zerubbabel;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Ezra 4, 2, The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>we do sacrifice unto Him</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘we do not sacrifice’.</i> <i>were fashioned;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Ps. 139, 16. The <i>ḳerë</i> is ‘and for it there was one among them’, the <i>kethib when as yet there was none of them</i>. In this instance E.V. follows the <i>kethib</i>.</i> <i>know ye;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Ps. 100, 3. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>we are His</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘and not we (have made) ourselves’.</i> <i>be gathered;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Isa. 49, 5. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>be gathered unto Him</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘be not gathered’.</i> <i>name</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. 1 Chron. 11, 20. The <i>ḳerë</i> is <i>and had a name among the three</i>, the <i>kethib</i> ‘and not a name among the three’.</i> [this last also] in the corresponding passage.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">viz. in 2 Sam. 23, 18. This reading is suggested by N.Y. to replace that of V ‘and his word’ which is meaningless. In this verse, however, <i>lamed-waw</i> is both written and read. [More probably <span dir="rtl">ודבר</span> in V is a misreading of <i>daleth</i> signifying ‘four’ which should be attached to the beginning of the next Rule.]</i> [38<i>a</i>]
<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">[V is unintelligible and the reading of H has been adopted.]</i> Four [occurrences of the name] Jeuel are to be read Jeiel; viz. <i>and in Gibeon</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. in 1 Chron. 9, 35.</i> <i>the Ashterathite</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XI, 44.</i> <i>the scribe</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">2 Chron. 26, 11.</i> [<i>and of the sons of</i>] <i>Elizaphon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIX, 13.</i>
The following words are to be read but are not written [in the text]: <i>the children of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In Judg. 20, 13.</i> <i>Euphrates;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In 2 Sam. 8, 3.</i> <i>man;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In 2 Sam. 16, 23.</i> <i>as;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 20. V incorrectly <span dir="rtl">בן</span> for <span dir="rtl">כן</span>.</i> <i>his sons;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 19, 37.</i> <i>of hosts;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 31.</i> <i>come;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In Jer. 31, 38 (some edd. 37).</i> <i>thereof;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In Jer. 50, 29. V incorrectly <i>lo’</i> for <i>lah</i>.</i> <i>unto me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In Ruth 3, 5.</i> <i>to me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 17.</i>
[With the following] it is the reverse;<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Of those mentioned in the preceding Rule. The former are to be read but are not written; the latter are written but are not to be read.</i> they are written [in the text] but are not to be read: <i>Amnon;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In 2 Sam. 13, 33 the word <i>’im</i> is written but not to be read.</i> <i>as he shall say;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In Jer. 39, 12 where similarly, <i>’im</i> is written but not read. V incorrectly indicates the verse with <i>ba’asher</i> for <i>ka’asher</i>.</i> <i>in</i> (<i>what</i>) <i>place;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In 2 Sam. 15, 21 the word <span dir="rtl">אם</span> is written but not read.</i> <i>kinsman;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In Ruth 3, 12, the word <i>’im</i> is written but not read.</i> <i>let … bend;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In Jer. 51, 3 the verb is repeated in the text.</i> <i>five</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In Ezek. 48, 16 <i>the south side … five hundred</i> the number <i>five</i> is written twice but read only once. [H adds two further instances: <span dir="rtl">נא</span> in 2 Kings 5, 18 and <i>’eth</i> in Jer. 38, 16.]</i>

Chapter 7

The following words are written without a <i>waw</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">At the end of the word.</i> but are to be read with the <i>waw: and offered it;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 7, 9.</i> <i>said;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 19. Here and in the other verses which are cited the <i>kethib</i> is the singular and the <i>ḳerë</i> the plural.</i> <i>and</i> [<i>they</i>] <i>said;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XII, 10.</i> <i>and they spoke;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">1 Kings 12, 7.</i> <i>shall they take away;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 20, 18.</i> <i>and let them deliver;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXII, 5.</i> <i>and eat;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 37, 30.</i> <i>together;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 48, 7, <i>yaḥdaw</i> to be read for <i>yaḥad</i>.</i> <i>and they shall profane it;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 7, 21.</i> <i>and they offered;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Ezra 3, 3.</i> <i>was made;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Dan. 5, 21.</i> <i>and took upon them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 9, 27.</i> <i>Hananiah;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Added by GRA and H. The name <i>Hananiah</i> indicates Neh. 3, 30 where the text omits the <i>waw</i> at the end of <i>’aḥaraw</i> (<i>after him</i>).</i> <i>Malchijah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Indicating <i>ibid.</i> 31 where the <i>waw</i> at the end of <i>’aḥaraw</i> is also omitted.</i> The reverse of this [occurs in the following words]:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A superfluous <i>waw</i> is written but not read.</i> <i>my feet;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 22, 34; the <i>kethib</i> is ‘his feet’. [Although not included here, in the preceding verse <i>my way is</i> ‘his way’ in the <i>kethib</i>.]</i> <i>throw her down;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 9, 33. [The added <i>waw</i> is due to dittography and superfluous.]</i> <i>and … commanded;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVI, 15. The <i>kethib</i> means ‘commanded him’.</i> <i>straight before him;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In Ezek. 46, 9 where the verb <i>shall go forth</i> is written as plural but to be read as singular.</i> <i>the fountain gate;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In Neh. 3, 15 where the <i>kethib</i> is ‘they set up’ and the <i>ḳerë</i> (he) <i>set up</i>.</i> <i>and called him;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In 1 Kings 12, 3. The reference is to the word which follows which is plural in the <i>kethib</i> and singular in the <i>ḳerë</i>.</i> <i>and he assembled;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 21. Here the reference is to the first verb <i>was come</i> which is written as plural but to be read as singular.</i> <i>took</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In 2 Kings 14, 13. The reference is again to the verb <i>came</i> with the singular to be read for the plural in the text.</i>
The following are written with a <i>he</i> which is not pronounced: <i>when I saw;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 7, 21, written <i>wa’er’eh</i> and read <i>wa’erë’</i>.</i> <i>and I brought;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 8, written <i>wa’abih</i> and read <i>wa’abi</i>.</i> <i>the lion</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 23, 20, written <i>ha’aryeh</i> and read <i>ha’ari</i>.</i> in the Book of Samuel;<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes the parallel passage in 1 Chron. 11, 22 where <i>ha’ari</i> is both written and read.</i> <i>was he found;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 48, 27, written <i>nimẓa’ah</i> but the reading is <i>nimẓa’</i>.</i> <i>and … saw;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> III, 7, written <i>wattir’eh</i> and read <i>watter’ë’</i>.</i> <i>this</i> [<i>city</i>];<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXVI, 6, written <i>hazzothah</i> and read <i>hazzoth</i>.</i> <i>evil;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Micah 3, 2, written <i>ra‘ah</i> and read <i>ra‘</i>.</i> [<i>the</i>] <i>evil;</i><sup
