V incorrectly reads <i>‘Abarim</i>.</i> the second<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes the first <i>ready</i> in Esth. 3, 14.</i> <i>ready;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 8, 13, written <i>‘athudim</i> and read <i>‘athidim</i>.</i> <i>their lads;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 14, 3, written <i>ẓe‘orehem</i> and read <i>ẓe‘irehem</i>.</i> <i>her little ones;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XLVIII, 4, written <i>ẓe‘oreha</i> and read <i>ẓe‘ireha</i>.</i> <i>dung</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 4, 15, written <i>ẓefu‘ë</i> and read <i>ẓefi‘ë</i>.</i> [38<i>b</i>]

Chapter 8

Variants<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In words, letters or vowels.</i> between the poem<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">So H. V has the plural.</i> of Psalm [XVIII] and Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 22.</i> are the following: <i>and … spoke;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 1; in Ps. 1 this is replaced by <i>For the Leader</i>, etc.</i> <i>and my deliverer;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 2 represents <i>my</i> by a suffix and pronoun; Ps. 3 omits the pronoun.</i> <i>my God, my rock;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 3; Sam. 3, <i>the God who is my rock</i>.</i> <i>my high tower, and my refuge;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 3; Ps. 3 omits <i>and my refuge</i>.</i> <i>from mine enemies;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 4 the Heb. <i>for from</i> is the prefix <i>mem</i>, in Ps. 4 the preposition <i>min</i>.</i> <i>for … compassed me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 5; in Ps. 5 <i>for</i> is wanting.</i> <i>the waves of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 5; Ps. 5 <i>the cords of</i>.</i> <i>the floods of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 5; Ps. 5 <i>and the floods of</i>.</i> <i>compassed me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 6 the Heb. is <i>sabbuni</i>, in Ps. 6 <i>sebabuni</i>. The meaning is the same.</i> <i>I called;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 7; Ps. 7 <i>I cried</i>.</i> <i>and … He heard;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 7; Ps. 7 omits <i>and</i>.</i> <i>then … did shake;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. in Sam. 8 is <i>wayyithga‘ash,</i> in Ps. 8 <i>wattig‘ash</i>. The meaning is practically the same.</i> <i>the foundations of heaven;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 8; Ps. 8 <i>the foundations of the mountains</i>.</i> <i>yea, He was seen;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 11; Ps. 11 <i>yea, He did swoop</i>.</i> <i>and He made;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 12; Ps. 12 omits <i>and</i>.</i> <i>pavilions;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 12; Ps. 12 <i>His pavilion</i>.</i> <i>gathering of:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 12; Ps. 12 <i>darkness of</i>.</i> <i>coals of fire flamed forth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 13; Ps. 13 <i>hailstones and coals of fire</i>.</i> <i>thundered from heaven;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 14; Ps. 14 <i>thundered in the heavens</i>.</i> <i>arrows;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 15; Ps. 15 <i>His arrows</i>.</i> <i>and discomfited them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 15 the Heb. is <i>wayyahom</i> (<i>ḳerë</i>), but the <i>kethib</i> is the same as in Ps. 15 <i>wayyehummem</i>.</i> <i>the channels of waters;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 16; Sam. 16 <i>the channels of the sea</i>.</i> <i>and … were laid bare;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 16; in Sam. 16 <i>and</i> is wanting.</i> <i>by the rebuke of the Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 16; Ps. 16 <i>at Thy rebuke, O Lord</i>.</i> <i>His nostrils;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 16; Ps. 16 <i>Thy nostrils</i>.</i> <i>from them that hated me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 18; Ps. 18 adds <i>and</i>.</i> <i>a stay;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 19 <i>mish‘an;</i> in Ps. 19 <i>lemish‘an</i>. The sense is the same.</i> <i>He brought me forth also;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. in Sam. 20 has a verb and pronoun; in Ps. 20 a verb with a pronominal suffix. There is no difference in meaning.</i> <i>according</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">V prints ‘in’ for ‘according’ in error.</i> <i>to my righteousness;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 21 the Heb. is <i>keẓidḳathi</i>, in Ps. 21 <i>keẓidḳi</i>. The nouns are practically synonymous.</i> <i>I did not depart from them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 23; Ps. 23 <i>I put not away … from me</i>.</i> <i>and I was;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 24 the Heb. is <i>wa’ehyeh;</i> Ps. 24 <i>wa’ehi</i> is a shortened form of the same verb.</i> <i>toward Him;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 24; Ps. 24 <i>with Him</i>.</i> <i>and I kept myself;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Both Sam. 24 and Ps. 24 have the same verb, but the former adds a <i>he</i> at the end.</i> <i>according to my righteousness;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 25 <i>keẓidḳathi;</i> Ps. 25 <i>keẓidḳi</i>.</i> <i>according to my cleanness;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 25; Ps. 25 <i>according to the cleanness of my hands</i>.</i> <i>man;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 26 the noun is <i>gibbor</i> (lit. mighty man), in Ps. 26 <i>gebar</i> (<i>man</i>).</i> <i>Thou dost show Thyself pure;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference in the form of the verb; in Sam. 27 <i>tittabar</i>, in Ps. 27 <i>tithbarar</i>.</i> <i>Thou dost show Thyself subtle;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Another difference in the form of the verb; in Sam. 27 <i>tittappal,</i> in Ps. 27 <i>tithpattal</i>.</i> <i>and … Thou dost save;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 28; Ps. 28 <i>for Thou dost save</i>.</i> <i>but Thine eyes are upon the haughty, that Thou mayest humble them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 28; Ps. 28 <i>but the haughty eyes Thou dost humble</i>.</i> <i>and the Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 29; Ps. 29 <i>the Lord my God</i>.</i> <i>by my God;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 30; Ps. 30 <i>and by my God</i>.</i> <i>who is God;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 32 reads <i>’el</i>, Ps. 32 <i>’elo’ah</i>.</i> <i>save;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 32; Ps. 32 <i>except</i>. A different preposition is used. V <i>ba‘adi</i> is an error for <i>mibbal‘adë</i>.</i> <i>my … fortress;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 33; Ps. 33 <i>that girdeth me</i>.</i> <i>who letteth … go forth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 33; Ps. 33 <i>and maketh</i>. The former reads <i>wayyater</i>, the latter <i>wayyitten</i>.</i> <i>do bend;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 35 <i>weniḥath</i>, Ps. 35 <i>weniḥathah</i>, with the same sense.</i> <i>and Thy condescension;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 36 <i>wa‘anotheka,</i> Ps. 36 <i>we‘anwatheka</i>.</i> <i>under me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 37 <i>taḥteni</i>, Ps. 37 <i>taḥtai</i>.</i> <i>I have pursued;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 38 <i>’erdefah</i>, Ps. 38 <i>’erdof</i>.</i> <i>and destroyed them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 38; Ps. 38 <i>and overtaken them</i>.</i> <i>and I have consumed them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 39; wanting in Ps. 39.</i> <i>and smitten them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 39; Ps. 39 omits <i>and</i>.</i> <i>that they cannot arise;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 39, lit. ‘that they will not arise’; Ps. 39 <i>that they are not able to rise</i>.</i> <i>yea, they are fallen;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 39; Ps. 39 <i>they are fallen</i>.</i> <i>for Thou hast girded me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 40 omits the <i>alef</i> from the verb.</i> <i>under me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 40 <i>taḥteni</i>, Ps. 40 <i>taḥtai</i>.</i> <i>yea, them that hate me, that I might cut them off;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 41; Ps. 41 <i>and I did cut off them that hate me</i>.</i> <i>they looked;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 42; Ps. 42 <i>they cried</i>.</i> <i>even unto the Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 42 the Heb. for <i>unto</i> is <i>’el</i>, in Ps. 42 <i>‘al</i>.</i> <i>as the dust of the earth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 43; Ps. 43 <i>as the dust before the wind</i>.</i> <i>I did stamp them … I did tread them down;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 43; Ps. 43 <i>I did cast them out</i>.</i> <i>Thou also hast delivered me … my people. Thou hast kept me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 44; Ps. 44 <i>Thou hast delivered me … the people; Thou hast made me</i>.</i> <i>dwindle away … as soon as they hear of me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 45; Ps. 45 <i>as soon as they hear of me … dwindle away</i>. In Sam. the Heb. for <i>hear</i> and <i>dwindle away</i> is <i>lishmoa‘</i> and <i>yithkaḥashu</i>, in Ps. <i>leshema‘</i> and <i>yekaḥashu</i>.</i> <i>and come halting out of their close places;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 46; Ps. 46 <i>and come trembling out of their close places</i>. The Heb. for <i>out of their close places</i> is <i>mimmisgerotham</i> in Sam. and <i>mimmisgerothehem</i> in Ps. There is no difference in the Heb. verb despite the different translation.</i> <i>my Rock of salvation;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 47 Ps. 47 lacks <i>my rock</i> in the second part of the verse.</i> <i>and bringeth down … under me;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 48; Ps. 48 <i>and subdueth … under me</i>. There is also a difference in the Heb. for <i>under me</i> as in verses 37, 40.</i> <i>and that bringeth me forth … yea … above them that rise up against me … violent;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 49; Ps. 49 <i>he delivereth me … yea … above them that rise up against me … violent</i>. The Heb. for <i>above me</i> and <i>violent</i> in Sam. is <i>umiḳḳamai</i> and <i>ḥamasim;</i> in Ps. <i>min ḳammai</i> and <i>ḥamas</i>.</i> the Tetragrammaton is placed first;<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In Sam. 50 it precedes <i>among the nations</i>, in Ps. 50 it follows it.</i> <i>I will sing praises;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. in Sam. 50 is <i>’azammer</i>, in Ps. 50 <i>’azammerah</i>. ‘Omer’ in V is a printer’s error.</i> <i>a tower of salvation</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Sam. 51; Ps. 51 <i>great salvation giveth He</i>. The Heb. in the former is <i>migdol</i>, in the latter <i>magdil</i>.</i>
The following are variants between Isaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Chapters XXXVI-XXXIX.</i> and the text of Kings:<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 18-XX.</i> <i>Now in the four</i>[<i>teenth</i>];<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Kings XVIII, 13; Isa. 36, 1 <i>now it came to pass in the four</i>[<i>teenth</i>].</i> <i>Hezekiah;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In Kings <i>ibid.</i> 14-16 the name is spelt <i>Ḥizḳiyyah</i> instead of <i>Ḥizḳiyyahu</i>.</i> <i>Rab-saris … to king Hezehiah … unto Jerusalem. And they went up and came to Jerusalem. And when they were come up, they came and stood by</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 17. In Isa. 36, 2 <i>Rab-saris</i> is lacking, the order of the words is <i>to Jerusalem unto the king</i>. The clause <i>and they went up … they came</i> is lacking, and <i>stood</i> is in the singular but the plural in Kings.</i> <i>And when they had called to the king … unto them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 18; <i>and when they … king</i> is lacking in Isa. 3 which has <i>unto them</i> for <i>unto him</i>.</i> <i>Sayest thou</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 20; Isa. 5 <i>I said</i>.</i> <i>now … thou trustest;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">In Isa. 6 <i>now</i> is lacking, as is also <i>leka</i> (lit. thou trusteth for thyself), compared with Kings 21.</i> <i>ye say … in Jerusalem;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>ye say</i> in Kings 22, Isa. 7 has <i>thou say</i> and omits <i>in Jerusalem</i>.</i> <i>with … king;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 23, the Heb. for <i>with … king</i> is <i>’eth melek;</i> in Isa. 8 it is <i>’eth hammelek</i>.</i> <i>now … against this place … against this land and destroy it;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>now</i> in Kings 25, Isa. 10 reads <i>and … now</i>. In <i>against this land</i> the preposition is <i>‘al</i> for <i>’el</i>.</i> <i>the son of Hilkiah … with us;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 26; in Isa. 11 <i>the son of Hilkiah</i> is lacking, and <i>to us</i> occurs instead of <i>with us</i>.</i> <i>unto them … hath … to;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 27; in Isa. 12 <i>unto them</i> is omitted, and <i>hath … to</i> is <i>ha‘al</i> for <i>ha’el</i>.</i> <i>and spoke … the word of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 28; in Isa. 13 <i>and spoke</i> is omitted and <i>word</i> is in the plural.</i> <i>and hearken not … when he persuadeth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 32; in Isa. 18 <i>and hearken not</i> is lacking, and <i>when</i> is replaced by <i>beware lest</i>.</i> <i>hath … ever delivered;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 33; Isa. 18 <i>hath … delivered</i>.</i> <i>Hena, and Ivvah have they delivered;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 34; in Isa. 19 <i>Hena and Ivva</i> is lacking, and before <i>have</i> the conjunction <i>and</i> is inserted.</i> <i>the countries;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 35; Isa. 20 <i>these countries</i>.</i> <i>but the people held their peace;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 36; in Isa. 21 <i>the people</i> is lacking and <i>wayyaḥarishu</i> is read for <i>weheḥerishu</i>.</i> <i>the words of Rab-shakeh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 27 and Isa. 22 are identical in the Heb. The reference is probably to 2 Kings 19, 4 and Isa. 37, 4 where the former reads <i>all the words of Rab-shakeh</i> while the latter omits <i>all</i>.</i><br><i>And Shebna;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 19, 2;in Isa. 37, 2 <i>we’eth</i> precedes the name.</i> <i>the prophet</i> precedes <i>the son of Amoz;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> in Isa. <i>ibid. the prophet</i> follows. The order in E.V. is the same in both.</i> <i>all the words of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 4; in Isa. 4 <i>all</i> is omitted.</i> <i>unto them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 6 <i>lahem;</i> Isa. 6 <i>’alehem</i>.</i> <i>to his own land;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 7 has the preposition <i>l</i>, Isa. 7 <i>’el</i>.</i> <i>of Tirhakah;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 9 has the preposition <i>’el</i>, Isa. 9 <i>‘al</i>.</i> <i>behold he is come out … again;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. 9 omits <i>behold</i> and for <i>again</i> (<i>wayyashab</i>) reads <i>and when he heard</i> (<i>wayyishma‘</i>).</i> <i>what;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 11 has <i>’eth ’asher</i>, Isa. 11 <i>’asher</i>.</i> <i>O Lord, the God of;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 15. Isa. 16 inserts <i>of hosts</i> after <i>Lord</i>.</i> <i>I have heard thee;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 20, wanting in Isa. 21.</i> <i>against the Holy One;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 22 reads <i>‘al</i>, Isa. 23 <i>’el</i>.</i> <i>thy messengers;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 23; Isa. 24 <i>thy servants</i>.</i> <i>the choice;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 23 reads <i>mibḥor;</i> Isa. 24 <i>mibḥar</i>.</i> <i>and I have entered into his farthest lodge;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> in Isa 24 <i>and I come up to the height of the mountains</i>.</i> <i>strange;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 24; lacking in Isa. 25.</i> <i>in … times</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 25 reads <i>lemimë</i>, Isa. 26 <i>mimë</i>.</i> <i>… should be laid waste;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In Kings 25 the reading is <i>lahshoth</i>, in Isa. 26 <i>lehash’oth</i>.</i> <i>and confounded;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 26 has <i>wayyeboshu</i>, Isa. 27 <i>waboshu</i>.</i> <i>and as corn blasted;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. 27 <i>and as a field of corn</i>.</i> <i>that which springeth of the same;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 29 reads <i>saḥish</i>, Isa. 30 <i>shaḥis</i>.</i> <i>he came;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 33 reads <i>yabo’</i>, Isa. 34 <i>ba’</i>.</i> <i>will defend this city;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 34 has the preposition <i>’el</i>, Isa. 35 <i>‘al</i>.</i> <i>and it came to pass that night;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 35; lacking in Isa. 36.</i> <i>and smote;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> the reading is <i>wayyak</i>, in Isa. 36 <i>wayyakeh</i>.</i> <i>fourscore</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 35; Isa. 36 <i>and fourscore</i>.</i><br><i>His face;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 20, 2 reads <i>’eth panaw;</i> Isa. 38, 2 omits <i>’eth</i>.</i> <i>saying;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> lacking in Isa. 2.</i> <i>I beseech Thee;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 3; in Isa. 3 <i>and said</i> is inserted before <i>I</i>.</i> <i>heart;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid. ubelebab</i>; Isa. <i>ubeleb</i>.</i> <i>return … the princes of My people … I will heal thee;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 5; lacking in Isa. 5.</i> <i>and I will add;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 6 <i>wehosafti</i>; Isa. 5 <i>behold, I will add</i> (<i>hinneni yosif</i>).</i> <i>for Mine own sake and for My servant David’s sake;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i>; lacking in Isa. 6.</i> <i>take … and he recovered;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 7. The whole sentence is lacking in Isa.</i> <i>and Hezekiah said unto Isaiah … the third day;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 8. The whole sentence is lacking in Isa.</i> <i>this;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 9; Isa. 7 <i>and this</i>.</i> <i>that the Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> the Heb. particle is <i>ki</i>, in Isa. 7 <i>’asher</i>.</i> <i>will do the thing;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. 7 <i>this thing</i>.</i> <i>go forward</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. 8 <i>behold, I will cause … to return</i>.</i> <i>Berodach</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 12; Isa. 39, 1 <i>Merodach</i>.</i> <i>… for he had heard;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> the reading is <i>ki shama‘;</i> Isa. <i>ibid. wayyishma‘</i>.</i> <i>Hezekiah had been sick;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. <i>ibid. that he had been sick, and was recovered</i>.</i> <i>hearkened unto them;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 13; Isa. 2 <i>was glad of them</i>.</i> <i>all his treasure house;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. <i>ibid.</i> omits <i>all</i>.</i> <i>oil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. <i>ibid. the … oil</i>.</i> <i>… the house of his armour</i><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.;</i> Isa. <i>ibid.</i> inserts <i>all</i> before <i>the house</i>.</i> <i>… they are come;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 14; Isa. 3 adds <i>unto me</i></i> <i>the word of the Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 16; in Isa. 5 <i>of hosts</i> follows <i>Lord</i>.</i> <i>to Babylon;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 17 the reading is <i>Babelah</i>, in Isa. 6 <i>Babel</i>.</i> <i>is it not so, if</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Kings 19 reads <i>halo ’im</i>, Isa. 8 <i>if but</i> (<i>ki</i>). The variants here listed are not complete and there are others which are not included (cf. GRA).</i>

Chapter 9

On the <i>beth</i> of <i>bereshith</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 1 the first word of the Pentateuch.</i> there must be four crownlets,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Short upward strokes on the top of the letter.</i> and the letters<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Some eds. read ‘letter’ in the singular, and the general practice is to enlarge only the <i>beth</i>.</i> of the word must be extended above all the other letters, because by its means<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This idea is also expressed in <i>’Othioth deRabbi ‘Aḳiba</i>, quoted by M (p. 131): The world was created by means of the letter <i>beth</i>.</i> the world came into existence.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">So M. V reads, ‘for it (the world) will exist for ever’, the <i>beth</i> representing <i>berakah</i> (blessing). [Cf. Midrash Rabbah, Genesis, I, §9 (Sonc. ed., p. 9).]</i>
The <i>waw</i> of <i>gaḥon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 11, 42, <i>belly</i>.</i> must be enlarged,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘upright, erect’.</i> because it is the middle of the letters of the Torah;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The number of letters from the beginning of the Pentateuch to the <i>waw</i> of <i>gaḥon</i> equals the number from the <i>waw</i> to the end.</i> <i>darosh darash</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> X, 16, <i>diligently inquired</i>.</i> is the middle of the words of the Torah. <i>Darosh</i> is written at the end of the line<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Indicating the end of the first half of the number of words.</i> and <i>darash</i> at the beginning of the next line.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Marking the beginning of the second half of the number of words.</i>
<i>Wayyishḥṭ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>And when it was slain;</i> it is uncertain whether this refers to Lev. 8, 15 (N.Y.) or <i>ibid.</i> 23 (M) both of which begin with the same word.</i> must be enlarged,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">According to M and H only the <i>ḥeth</i> of the word, it being also the initial of <i>ḥaẓi</i> (half), is to be enlarged.</i> because it is half of the verses of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In Ḳid. 30a (Sonc. ed., p. 144) <i>wehithgallaḥ</i> (Lev. 13, 33, <i>then he shall be shaven</i>) is given as the middle of the verses and, according to the Masorah, its <i>gimel</i> is to be enlarged. On the number of verses opinions differ. It is variously computed as 5,844, 5,845, 5,885 and 5,888.</i>
<i>Shema‘ Yisra’el</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 6, 4, <i>Hear, O Israel</i>.</i> must be written at the beginning of a line, and all its letters are to be enlarged,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Masorah only the <i>‘ayin</i> of <i>Shema‘</i> is enlarged.</i> while <i>’eḥad</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid., one.</i></i> must come at the end of [the same] line.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The final letter of the word is also enlarged according to the Masorah.</i>
The <i>lamed</i> of <i>wayyashlikem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 29, 27, <i>and cast them</i>.</i> must be long.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. longer than the other letters. By its elongation, the <i>lamed</i> is treated as the beginning of a word consisting of it and the following two letters, <i>LaKem</i> which means ‘to you’, implying homiletically God’s promise, ‘I will be a God <i>to you</i> even after you have been <i>cast into another land</i>’.</i>
The <i>he</i> of <i>haladonai</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXII, 6, <i>do … the Lord</i>.</i> must be longer than any other <i>he</i>, because it is<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V reads, ‘so that it shall form’. [N.Y. gives the meaning, ‘as though it were’, viz. the initial of <span dir="rtl">הוי</span>, ‘alas’; cf. Midrash Rabbah, Exodus, Sonc. ed., p. 294, n. 1.]</i> a word on its own.
The <i>yod o</i>f <i>teshi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 32, 18, <i>thou wast unmindful</i>.</i> must be smaller than any <i>yod</i> in the Scriptures. The <i>yod</i> of <i>yigdal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 14, 17, <i>let … be great</i>.</i> must be bigger than any <i>yod</i> in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and N.Y. V erroneously ‘big, because they are letters which are in Scripture’. H lacks the sentence.</i> <i>Yisra’el</i> at the end of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 34, 12, <i>Israel</i>.</i> must be enlarged, while the <i>lamed</i> in it must be higher than any other <i>lamed</i>.
The following words<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Which have an indelicate nuance.</i> are written but not read [in their written form]:<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A more polite word is substituted.</i> It is written <i>ba‘folim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘posteriors’.</i> but we read <i>baṭṭeḥorim;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 5, 6, <i>emerods</i>.</i> it is written <i>yishgalennah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘rape’. This is the reading of N.Y. and M. V has <i>thishgalennah</i>.</i> but we read <i>yishkabennah;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 28, 30, <i>shall lie with her</i>.</i> it is written <i>weroba‘ haḳḳab ḥiryonim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 6, 25, <i>and the fourth part of a kab of dove’s dung</i>.</i> but we read <i>weroba‘ haḳḳab dibyonim;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘and the fourth … of decayed leaves’.</i> it is written <i>memë shinehem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVIII, 27, <i>their own water</i>.</i> but we read <i>memë raglehem;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the water of their feet’.</i> it is written <i>ḥorehem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid. their own dung.</i></i> but we read <i>ẓo’atham;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘their evacuation’.</i> it is written <i>wayyesimehu lemaḥara’oth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> X, 27, <i>and made it a draught-house</i>.</i> but we read <i>lemoẓa’oth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘retreats’.</i><br>If a person uses euphemisms in the section of forbidden marriages<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 18, 6-20.</i> he is silenced.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">[The text in V is corrupt and emended in agreement with the Mishnah in Meg. 25a (Sonc. ed., p. 149).]</i> R. Jonah explained:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">What is meant here by ‘euphemisms’.</i> [39<i>a</i>] [When one says]<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">By trying to be more considerate than Scripture.</i> ‘the nakedness of his father’ or ‘the nakedness of his<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>thy father</i> and <i>thy mother</i> (Lev. 18, 7). R. Joseph (Meg. <i>loc. cit.</i>) gives a different interpretation, viz. the alteration of <i>nakedness</i> to ‘shame’ (cf. Sonc. ed., p. 151, n. 2).</i> mother’. If a person says<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">[Or, according to J. Rabbinowitz, <i>Mishnah Megillah</i>, p. 133. ‘And thou shalt not give any of thy seed (to a heathen woman) to become with child in idolatry,] instead of <i>and thou shalt not give of thy seed to set them apart to Molech</i> (Lev. 18, 21).</i> ‘and thou shalt not cause any of thy seed to be conceived by a Gentile woman’,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">So altering the true meaning of the text which deals with the worship of Molech (cf. Sonc. ed., Meg. p. 149, n. 3).</i> he is silenced with a rebuke.
The incident of Reuben<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 35, 22.</i> is read<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In the public lection of the Scriptures.</i> but not translated.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Out of respect for the memory of Reuben. In the ancient Synagogues, the readings from the Scriptures were rendered into Aramaic by an official for the benefit of those who were unable to follow the Hebrew.</i> The story of Tamar<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 38.</i> is both read and translated.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H in agreement with Meg. 25a (Sonc. ed., p. 151). V inserts the negative before both verbs. The reflection on Judah’s behaviour is mitigated by his frank confession.</i> The first [account of] the golden calf<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 33, 1-20.</i> is read and translated,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sin was forgiven (Meg. 25b, Sonc. ed., p. 152).</i> the second<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 21-25 according to Meg. <i>loc. cit.</i> (Sonc. ed., p. 153).</i> is read but not translated.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the statement <i>and there came out this calf</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 24) which seems to indicate that the calf had abnormal powers.</i>
Where [does the translator] begin? At <i>Oh this people have sinned</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 31-35. This disagrees with Meg. quoted above.</i> The blessing of the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 6, 24-27 which includes the phrase <i>lift up … countenance</i> which in the Heb. could be misunderstood in its lit. meaning of ‘favouritism’.</i> and the stories of David<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 11, 2-17 which is discreditable to David.</i> and Amnon<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIII, 1-14 which records Amnon’s disgrace.</i> are neither read nor translated.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">So V and H in agreement with Meg. <i>loc. cit.;</i> but GRA and others omit the negative before ‘read’</i>
The section dealing with the heavenly chariot<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 1 and X which are too abstruse for the ordinary layman.</i> must not be read as a <i>hafṭarah</i>. R. Judah permits it.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The custom is for Ezek. 1 to be read as the <i>hafṭarah</i> on the first day of Pentecost. This is in accordance with R. Judah’s view.</i> R. Eleazar says: The section beginning <i>Cause Jerusalem to know</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 15<i>I</i></i> must not be read as a <i>hafṭarah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the denunciation of Jerusalem.</i> It once happened that a person read the section beginning <i>Cause Jerusalem to know</i> [<i>hear bominations</i>] as a <i>hafṭarah</i> and R. Eleazar exclaimed, ‘Let that man go and investigate the abominations of his mother’.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In. Meg. 25b (Sonc. ed., p. 153) the reading is: R. Eleazar said to him, ‘While you are investigating the abominations of Jerusalem, go and investigate the abominations of your mother’.</i> Enquiries were made about him and he was found to be illegitimate.

Chapter 10

Moses<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In V and H a passage has been interpolated after ‘Moses’ which has no relation to the subject-matter. It seems to have been originally a marginal note explanatory of the meaning of <i>hiḳni</i>’, an unusual verb to be employed in the sense of ‘warn’. It reads as follows: <i>Hiḳni</i>’, i.e. ‘he warned’, as we have learned in a Mishnah (Soṭ. I, 1), <i>hammeḳannë’</i> (of the same root as <i>hiḳni’</i> which means), If [a husband] ‘warns’ his wife [not to associate with a certain man], R. Eliezer said: His warning is valid if it was given in the presence of two witnesses, though he makes her drink [the water of bitterness] on the evidence of one witness [who saw that she had secluded herself with the man after she had been given due warning], because a warning is not valid if given in the presence of less than two witnesses; for [only where there are two witnesses] would she be able to plead, ‘I was not warned’, but when [there was only] one witness she might dare to plead, ‘I was not warned’.</i> warned<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>hiḳni’</i> some eds. read the normal verb <i>hithḳin</i>, ‘he established a custom, ordained’.</i> Israel that they should read out of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In the service of the Synagogue.</i> on Sabbaths, Festivals, New Moons and the intermediate days of the Festivals;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the secular [part] of the Festival’, the days intervening between the holy days which begin and end the Festivals of Passover and Tabernacles.</i> as it is stated, <i>And Moses declared unto the children of Israel the appointed seasons of the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 23, 44.</i>
Ezra ordained for Israel that they should read in the Torah on Mondays and Thursdays and in the afternoon service on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">[For this and other ordinances of Ezra, cf. B.Ḳ. 82a (Sonc. ed., p. 466) where the reason is given for these additional lections.]</i>
There is no difference between a Festival and the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In regard to forbidden work.</i> save only in the matter of preparing food.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘food of a person’. On a Festival, but not when it falls on the Sabbath, the preparation of food for the day by cooking is permitted.</i> There is no difference between the Sabbath and the Day of Atonement save only that a wilful violation of the former [is punishable] by men<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. a court of law.</i> while a wilful violation of the latter [is punished] by <i>kareth</i>.
R. Judah said: At the place [in the Torah] where we leave off on the Sabbath morning we begin to read at the afternoon service, and on the following Monday, Thursday and Sabbath.
On Mondays and Thursdays<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">During the morning service.</i> and at the Sabbath afternoon service three men read [from the Torah].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The first of the seven portions into which the weekly <i>sidra’</i> or section is divided for the Sabbath lection. On the specified occasions it is subdivided between three worshippers.</i> Their number must be neither reduced nor increased; nor is a <i>hafṭarah</i> read from [a book of] a Prophet. He who begins as well as he who concludes [the reading of the prescribed section of] the Torah recites the appropriate benedictions before and after his reading.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For the benedictions, cf. P.B., p. 68.</i><br>On the New Moon and during the intermediate days of a Festival, four [persons read from the Torah]. Their number must be neither reduced nor increased. This is the general rule: On any day which has a <i>musaf</i> and is not a Festival, four persons read [from the Torah].
On a Festival five [persons read from the prescribed portion of the Torah]; on the Day of Atonement, six; and on the Sabbath, seven. The number must not be reduced but may be increased. On a Festival, for the violation of which only a negative precept is infringed, five persons [read in the Torah]; on the Day of Atonement, for the violation of which <i>kareth</i> is incurred, six; on the Sabbath, the violation of which is punishable by stoning, seven; and one concludes with [a reading from a book of] a Prophet.<br>The one who begins as well as the one who concludes [the lection] recites the benediction before and after his reading.
The accompanying benedictions of the <i>Shema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">These are the <i>ḳaddish, bareku</i> and <i>yoẓer ’or</i> (P.B., pp. 37ff) which precede the <i>Shema‘</i>, and the three benedictions of the <i>‘Amidah</i> which follow it (P.B., pp. 44ff) including the <i>ḳedushshah</i>.</i> may not be repeated<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘they must not break off in connection with the <i>Shema‘ ’</i>. The ruling applies to persons who had read the morning prayers in private and now wish to join in congregational worship. They do not repeat all the statutory morning prayers, but may repeat ‘broken off’ parts, viz. those specified in the preceding note (cf. N.Y. and Rashi to Meg. 23b). For other interpretations, cf. Meg. (Sonc. ed., p. 141, n. 1) [and J. Rabbinowitz, <i>Mishnah Megillah</i>, pp. 114ff].</i> in congregational prayer, irrespective of whether they are said standing<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the benedictions which follow the <i>Shema‘</i> said while the worshippers are standing.</i> or sitting;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The benedictions which precede the <i>Shema‘</i> said while the worshippers are sitting.</i> nor may one act as reader in public worship;<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In connection with any part of the prayers. Lit. ‘they do not pass before the [reading] chest’, to lead the congregation in the conduct of the service.</i> nor may [the <i>kohanim</i>] lift their hands;<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">To bless the people during the service; cf. P.B., p. 238a.</i> nor may the <i>hafṭarah</i> be read from the Prophet;<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As is the law in regard to the Torah itself; cf. Meg. 23b (Sonc. ed., p. 141).</i> nor may halts<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘a standing and a sitting’.</i> be made [at funerals]<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It was customary, when returning from a burial, to halt and sit seven times to praise and lament the dead.</i> for men, how much less for women since no halts are made for women; nor may <i>ḳaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 37.</i> and <i>bareku</i> be said<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Even without the other statutory prayers.</i>—if less than ten adult males<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Who have attained their thirteenth birthday.</i> are present.<br>Our teachers in the West<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The land of Israel which was west of Babylon.</i> rule that seven [adult males<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Who have not heard one of the benedictions indicated above.</i> are sufficient]<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For the purpose of repeating the benedictions.</i> and, as a reason for their ruling, they quote, <i>When men let grow their hair in Israel, when the people offer themselves willingly, bless ye the Lord</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Judg. 5, 2.</i> [which implies that the number present must be the same] as that of the [Hebrew] words.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Because the verse contains the word <i>bareku</i> (<i>bless ye</i>).</i> Others hold that even six [adult males are sufficient for the purpose, since up to] <i>bareku</i> [the number of words] is six.<br>Furthermore, where there are nine or ten persons who have heard either <i>bareku</i> or <i>ḳaddish</i> and, after the service, one of them [who missed one of the benedictions] said <i>bareku</i> or <i>ḳaddish</i>, he has thereby performed [the duty of public worship].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Contrary to the ruling of the Rabbis of the West and the others who hold that seven or even six are enough.</i><br>The Sages long ago issued an ordinance to Synagogue precentors to say after the prayer for redemption,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">‘O Rock of Israel,’ etc. (P.B., p. 44).</i> <i>Blessed be the name of the Lord from this time forth and for ever</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 113, 2.</i> and after that, ‘Bless ye the Lord Who is to be blessed’,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bareku</i>, etc. (P.B., p. 37).</i> so as to enable those who had not heard the benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Owing to their late arrival.</i> to perform the duty of public worship; for R. Joḥanan said: Would that a man read his prayers all the day long.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore it is permissible for the precentor to repeat the benedictions under discussion more than once for the benefit of those who did not hear him when he first read them.</i><br>The congregations of the West and the congregations of the East<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The land of Israel and Babylon respectively.</i> have a custom to say it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The verse from Psalms followed by the benedictions mentioned.</i> after ‘He Who maketh peace’<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 54, forming the conclusion of the <i>‘Amidah</i>.</i> in the three statutory prayers<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For the evening, morning and afternoon. This custom is contrary to the previous opinion which permits an interruption between the prayer for redemption and the <i>‘Amidah</i> which immediately follows it. The originators of the custom forbid such an interruption.</i> of the Eighteen Benedictions,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The actual number is nineteen, but it was eighteen when originally instituted.</i> as a provision for the benefit of those who arrived late and for those who leave early.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">It is for their benefit that the repetition is not made at the end of the entire service.</i> [The verse from Psalms with the benedictions is also said] even after the public reading from the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If persons came in as late as that.</i><br>As regards the redemption of land,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Which had been dedicated to the Temple.</i> however, [the presence is required of] nine male adults and a <i>kohen</i>; and the same law applies to the redemption of a human being.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Lev. 27, 2ff, 16ff.</i>

Chapter 11

One who reads the Torah [in public worship] should read not less than three verses,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The number corresponds to the three main divisions of the Bible: the Pentateuch, Prophets and Hagiographa; Meg. 24a (Sonc. ed., p. 143).</i> and he should not read for the interpreter<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who translated and expounded the Heb. text into Aramaic for the congregation.</i> more than one verse [at a time].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">So that the interpreter, who had no book before him, should not become confused.</i> In the case of [the lection from] a Prophet, however, three [verses at a time may be read];<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If desired. Since no instruction in law is involved in the lection from the Prophets as in the Torah, a mistake would not be serious.</i> but if the three verses constituted three different paragraphs, he may read one verse at a time<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For the interpreter.</i>—for instance, <i>For thus saith the Lord; Ye were sold for nought</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 52, 3.</i> … [39<i>b</i>] <i>For thus saith the Lord God: My people went down aforetime into Egypt</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 4.</i> <i>… Now, therefore, what do I here, saith the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 5.</i><br>In a city where only one person is able to read [from the Torah scroll] he reads all<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">‘All’ is added by GRA, M and H.</i> the prescribed sections, provided he sits down between the reading of one section and the next.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘between reading and reading’. The number of times he sits down corresponds to the number of persons who are to be called up for the reading. This ruling applies to the time when only the first and last who were called up recited the benedictions before and after the reading. At the present time, when each person called up recites the benedictions before and after his portion, there is no need for the sitting down.</i>
It is permitted to skip from place to place in [a scroll of] a Prophet,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Omitting the intermediate portions.</i> but not in a Torah scroll, because [the scroll of] a Prophet may be unrolled in public but not of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The unrolling of which might disturb the congregation.</i> Similarly ruled R. Jeremiah in the name of R. Simeon b. Laḳish: A Torah scroll may not be unrolled in public.<br>R. Jose enquired: Suppose<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">GRA in agreement with j.Meg. IV, 5, 75b. V and H read ‘interpreted it, as for instance’.</i> there was only a small section [which one wished to skip]!<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">And consequently no unrolling is required. Why, then, should skipping in such a case be forbidden?</i> The real reason<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘but’ added by GRA. V and H omit.</i> is that Israel shall hear the Torah in its proper order. But have we not learned: [The High Priest] reads [in public] <i>aḥarë moth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">lit. <i>after the death</i>, designating the section Lev. 16.</i> and <i>’ak be‘asor?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. <i>howbeit on the tenth</i>, designating the section beginning Lev. 23, 27. These sections are far removed from one another and the Torah scroll must consequently be unrolled in public. Why is this permitted?</i> [With these two sections] it is different,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore unrolling in public is permitted.</i> because they deal with the statutory order [of the reading] of the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the Day of Atonement. As they form one subject, skipping from place to place is allowed.</i> You can see the reason<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and you may know’.</i> for yourself, because R. Simeon b. Laḳish ruled, ‘No part of the Torah may be<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in every place one may not’.</i> read by heart’, and yet here<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The section beginning <i>And on the tenth day</i> (Num. 29, 7).</i> [the High Priest] reads by heart.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For the reason stated above.</i>
R. Jose gave the following instruction to Bar<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">So j.Meg. IV, 5, 75b, the text of which is followed here. V has ‘Mar’.</i> ‘Ula, the precentor of the Babylonian Synagogue [in Sepphoris]:<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In Upper Galilee.</i> When only one Torah scroll is available the unrolling<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">When skipping is necessary from one passage to another.</i> should be done in front of the ark curtain,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">There is no need to return the scroll to the ark after the first reading had concluded and then take it out again for the second section.</i> but when two scrolls are available you should carry the first [back to the ark] and then take out the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The present custom is to take both scrolls from the ark at the same time and to keep the second rolled and wrapped until the completion of the reading from the first.</i> To what extent [is skipping in the Torah permitted]? R. Jose said: As, for instance, the sections<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Which are not so far from one another as to cause the interpreter to stop and keep the congregation waiting while the scroll is being unrolled to the second section, and deal with the same subject, thus causing no confusion to the hearers.</i> [read on the Day of Atonement] by the High Priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H in agreement with j.Meg. <i>loc. cit.</i> The reading of V is ‘Jehoiada the priest’.</i>
If the precentor read in the Torah on a Sabbath for less than seven persons,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘readings or callings’, i.e. the lection was allocated to less than the minimum number.</i> having forgotten and thought that he had already read for<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘had called up’.</i> seven persons, he should read again completing the number of seven, and call up an eighth as <i>mafṭir;</i> provided always that the lection<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the order’, the prescribed section for the reading of the day.</i> consists of [a minimum of] twenty-one verses and allows three verses for each of the seven persons who are called up. And if a section consists of less [than three verses for each of the seven persons]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This apparently refers to ancient Synagogues where only short portions of the Torah were read or where it was completed once in three years, not annually as is the present custom. Then the section for a Sabbath may sometimes have been very short.</i> an addition should be made to the section from the one following it.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘from another portion’.</i>
If a section consisted of four or five verses, he reads the whole of it, but with a section of six verses he may read and make a break<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">At the end of the third verse.</i> because it has the [minimum] length for two readings. If on a Monday or Thursday, or the Sabbath afternoon service or the afternoon service of Festivals<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">On which occasion three persons are called to the reading of the Torah.</i> less than ten verses<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">According to some authorities, if nine verses were read no repetition of the reading is necessary (cf. <i>’Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> CXXXVII, §1 and commentaries <i>ad loc.</i>). Others maintain that in general ten is the minimum number.</i> were read, he should repeat the reading to complete ten verses. From where [in the Torah] does one read in the afternoon service of a Festival [when it occurs on a Sabbath]?<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">On a Festival which occurs on a week-day there is no reading from the Torah in the afternoon service.</i> In the regular order where [the Torah reading] was interrupted before the Festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. from the beginning of the lection of the next Sabbath.</i>
If in the course of public reading he skipped a verse and omitted to read it, should he have read ten verses exclusive<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. V has ‘from’.</i> of the verse skipped, he need not go back; but if less than<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘within’.</i> ten, he must go back and read ten verses in correct order so that none is left out. This is the case with [the reading of] a week-day,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Monday and Thursday.</i> Sabbath afternoon and Festival afternoon;<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the omitted verses will be read on the following Sabbath morning in the course of the lection.</i> but on a Sabbath [morning] if he forgot and passed over a verse and then realized [his mistake], he must stop<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Added by GRA.</i> and re-read [the passage in full]. Even after one had read the <i>hafṭarah</i> in [a Book of] a Prophet and recited the <i>musaf</i>, he must stop forthwith and read [the Scriptural passage]. The <i>musaf</i>, however,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V inserts ‘because’ which cannot be justified.</i> need not be repeated.

Chapter 12

It is permitted to make a break<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the passage may be divided and two persons called up to the reading.</i> in the blessings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">e.g. Lev. 26, 3-13.</i> but not in the curses.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14-46.</i> R. Ḥiyya b. Gamda said: What is the reason?<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Why may not more than one person be called to the section of the curses?</i> Because it is written [<i>My son, despise not the chastening of the Lord</i>,]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is the first part of the verse which is not quoted in the text of the Tractate.</i> <i>neither cut thou up His correction</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 3, 11, E.V. <i>neither spurn</i>.</i>—do not cut them<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘make it’, a section in the Torah which deals with God’s chastening of Israel.</i> up into pieces.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. root may bear both meanings: ‘spurn’ and ‘cut up’.</i>
R. Levi said:<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Explaining the reason that no more than one person may be called to the reading of the curses in Lev. 26, 14ff.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, said: It is not proper that My children shall be cursed while I am being blessed.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">By the recital of the benedictions in the middle of reading the curses.</i> R. Jose b. Abin said: It is not for this reason, but because one who stands up to read in the Torah must begin and conclude with something auspicious.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If two persons were to be called to the reading, the first would finish and the second begin with a verse which threatens a curse.</i>
Levi b. Paṭi enquired of R. Hanina, ‘What is the law in regard to the curses each of which begins with <i>’arur?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 27, 15-26, each verse commencing <i>Cursed be</i>.</i> Must they be read by one person only,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">As with the curses of Lev. 26, 14ff which must not be read by more than one person.</i> and must a benediction be recited before and after them?’ He replied, ‘There is no [passage] that requires a benediction before and after its reading but the curses of Leviticus<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Torath Kohanim</i>, lit. ‘the law of the priests’, the name of the Book of Leviticus. The passage is XXVI, 14-46.</i> and Deuteronomy’.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mishneh Torah</i>, lit. ‘the repetition of the law’, the name of the Book of Deuteronomy. The passage is XXVIII, 15-68. Only with these passages, which are not restricted to a particular time, are the benedictions recited before and after they have been read; but with the <i>’arur</i> curses, which were spoken by the Levites only (cf. Deut. 27, 14) and at the time when the Jordan was crossed (<i>ibid.</i> 2), no benedictions are to be recited.</i>
When Jonathan, a children’s teacher from Gufta,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In Galilee. V incorrectly ‘Gufthera’.</i> came down here,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Tiberias where R. Abuna re-sided. The city is a on lower level, hence the verb ‘came down’.</i> he saw a children’s teacher, R. Abuna, reading the Song of the Well<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 21, 17-20.</i> and reciting the benedictions before and after it. ‘Is it proper,’ he asked him, ‘to act in this manner?’<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The rule is that the benedictions are recited only at the beginning and end of the entire section to be read. The Song of the Well, appearing in the middle of a section, should consequently require no benedictions before and after its reading.</i> ‘Do you,’ the other retorted, ‘still require [a ruling] on this subject? All poems<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Such as the Song of the Sea (Ex. 15, 1-19), the Song of the Well and probably also Deut. 32, 1-43.</i> must be preceded and followed by benedictions.’
A question<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In connection with the preceding Rule.</i> was asked of<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Another reading is ‘he came and asked’.</i> R. Simon. ‘There is no section’,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘you have not’.</i> he told them in the name of R. Joshua b. Levi, ‘which requires a benediction before and after its reading other than the Song at the Sea, the Ten Words,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Decalogue (Ex. 20, 2-17).</i> and the curses in Deuteronomy’.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 28, 15-68. Others add ‘the curses in Leviticus’.</i>
R. Abahu<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. V reads ‘Abi’.</i> said: I have not heard [such a ruling],<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">That in the case of the passages specified benedictions are to be recited before and after the reading.</i> but it is feasible to apply it to the Ten Words. R. Jose b. Abin said in the name of Rabbi:<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">[The reading should be ‘R. Berekiah’ or ‘R. Simon’; cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. pal. Amoräer</i>, III, p. 727, n. 7.]</i> The last ten verses in Deuteronomy<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and N.Y. The text in V is difficult.</i> require a benediction before and after they have been read.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">These verses, which describe the death of Moses, form a section of their own, because they were written by Joshua (according to R. Judah or R. Nehemiah) or, if written by Moses, they were dictated to him by God while he ‘wrote them down with tears’ (B.B. 15a, Sonc. ed., p. 72).</i>
R. Isaac Seḥorah asked R. Isaac Nappaḥa, ‘What do we read [first]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the second and third scrolls.</i> when the New Moon of <i>Ṭebeth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Which always occurs in the week of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> falls on a Sabbath?’<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Three scrolls are then taken from the ark. The usual weekly portion is read in the first, and the passages for New Moon and <i>Ḥanukkah</i> in the second and third.</i> [He replied,] ‘In the section of <i>kalloth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>made an end</i> (Num. 7, 1). This designates the entire section, descriptive of the dedication offerings brought by the princes of Israel, which is prescribed for the reading of the day.</i> while the <i>mafṭir</i> reads<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the third scroll.</i> the passages relating to the Sabbath and the New Moon.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 9-15. The present custom is always to read the section for the Sabbath and New Moon first, and then the passage for <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> If it falls on a week-day we call up on the first day<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Of the two days of the New Moon.</i> three persons for the reading of the New Moon, the fourth reading that of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, because that which is more frequent takes precedence.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The New Moon occurs monthly, <i>Ḥanukkah</i> once a year.</i> But since the reading of the New Moon was read first,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">On the first day of the New Moon.</i> [40<i>a</i>] three persons are to be called up on the second day for the reading of the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> passage,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In the first scroll. Only two scrolls are taken out on a week-day during <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> while the fourth reads<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In the second scroll.</i> the section of the New Moon, viz. <i>uberashë ḥodshekem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>and in your new moons</i> (Num. 28, 11).</i> to the end of the passage.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 15.</i> Why is this so?<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. why is the fourth person called to the reading of the New Moon passage and not, as on the first day, to the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> passage?</i> Because it was only on account of the New Moon <i>musaf</i> that a fourth person is added’.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">On the other week-days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> there is no <i>musaf</i>. This argument cannot be applied to the first day of the New Moon because it is the second day which begins the month. The first of the two days of the New Moon is the last day of the preceding month. V and H insert here a ruling which has no connection with this chapter and is practically identical with XIV, 2 below.</i>
R. Jose b. Abin said: For the Song of <i>Ha’azinu</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>give ear</i>, designating the section Deut. 32, 1-43. For <i>Ha’azinu</i> (the reading of GRA) V and H read ‘the Levites’, because the poem was sung by the Levites in the Temple during the Sabbath <i>musaf</i> in six parts on successive Sabbaths.</i> not less than six persons<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Because the Levites concluded it in cycles of six Sabbath divisions.</i> are to be called up, and your mnemonic sign<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For the words beginning each of the six parts. The divisions, according to Maimonides, emphasize the exhortations and arouse the people to repentance.</i> is <i>h z y w l k</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">pronounced <span dir="rtl">.הזי״ו ל״ך</span></i> which stands for <i>ha’azinu</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>give ear</i> (Deut. 32, 1).</i> <i>zekor</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>remember</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 7).</i> <i>yarkibehu</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>He made him ride</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 13).</i> <i>wayyishman</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>but … waxed fat</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 15).</i> <i>lu</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>if</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 29).</i> <i>ki yadin … ‘ammo</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>for the Lord will judge His people</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 36).</i> It must also be provided with<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and it has’.</i> the space of a full line above it and of a full line below it. Corresponding to it the custom has been adopted to write in the Torah its number of lines.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The number is 72 according to GRA who apparently includes the two blank lines. Maimonides and others give the number as 70.</i>
A mnemonic sign for the beginnings of the lines<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Of the song <i>Ha’azinu</i>. The following renderings are from E.V. While the Heb. words stand at the beginning of the respective lines, the translations, owing to English idiom, do not always occupy the same position in the sentence.</i> is the following: <i>ha’azinu</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Give ear</i> (Deut. 32, 1).</i> <i>ya‘arof</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shall drop</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 2).</i> <i>kise‘irim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>As the small rain</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> <i>ki</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>For</i> (3).</i> <i>haẓẓur</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>The Rock</i> (4).</i> <i>’el</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>A God</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> <i>shiḥeth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is corruption</i> (5).</i> <i>halaH</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>The Lord</i> (6).</i> <i>halo’</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is not</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> <i>zekor</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Remember</i> (7).</i> <i>she’al</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ask</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> <i>behanḥel</i>,<sup R. Ze‘ira, quoting R. Jeremiah, in the name of Rab, said; The Song at the Sea<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 1-18.</i> and the Song of Deborah<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Judg. 5, 1-31.</i> are written in the form of a half-brick over a whole brick,<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Or ‘blank space’; the Heb. <span dir="rtl">לבינה</span> may bear both meanings.</i> and a whole brick over a half-brick.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. above. p. 215, n. 45.</i>

Chapter 13

In regard, however,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the poems dealt with in the previous Rule.</i> to the Song of David in Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 22.</i> and in the Psalms,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 18.</i> the Sages have not prescribed a limit;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For the number of lines in a column.</i> but a skilled scribe spaces them out symmetrically<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the form of stichoi.</i> according to the beginnings, the middle pauses, and the endings of the verses.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This rendering follows the text in M and H.</i> A similar arrangement is also followed throughout the Psalms and in Job and Proverbs.
The scroll of Esther<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Which is read on <i>Purim</i>.</i> requires ruled lines<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">To guide the scribe in writing the letters which are suspended from them.</i> as does the real<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘its truth of’.</i> Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Here denotes the Pentateuch.</i> because it is called ‘a scroll’.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The text corrected by GRA. V has ‘scroll called’. ‘Scroll’ is lit. ‘book’, the word used in Esth. 9, 32. For another reason, cf. Meg. 16b (Sonc. ed., p. 100).</i>
The ten sons of Haman<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 9, 7-9.</i> and the kings of Canaan<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 12, 9-24.</i> are written in the form of a half-brick over a half-brick and a brick over a brick.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Like the song of <i>Ha’azinu</i> (cf. above XII, 8).</i> Such a structure<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In which bricks and half-bricks rest one above the other and do not overlap.</i> does not endure.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And so is symbolic of the fate of Haman’s sons.</i> What is implied by ‘such’?<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The statement ‘such a structure does not last’.</i> Does it indicate a recommendation<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As the arrangement is only a symbol, a deviation from it would not disqualify the scroll.</i> or an indispensable condition?<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A deviation would then lead to disqualification. The answer follows in the next Rule.</i>
R. Jose<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows GRA and H in agreement with j.Meg. III, 7, 74b. V reads: ‘R. Jose b. Ḥanina, the brother of R. Hoshaiah, R. Abba b. Zabda surrounded thee, and we asked him this, and he said in the name of Rab that the condition was indispensable’.</i> said to R. Ḥanina, the nephew of R. Hoshaiah, ‘Surely you remember that when we were standing in front of the shop of your uncle, R. Hoshaiah, R. Ba’ b. Zabda passed by and we put the question to him, and he replied that the condition was indispensable’.
R. Ḥiyya b. R. Adda of Jaffa, quoting R. Jeremiah in the name of R. Ze‘ira, said: The names of the ten sons of Haman and the word <i>ten</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 9, 10.</i> must be recited in one breath.
R. Jose b. Abin said: When they<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The names of Haman’s sons.</i> are written down, the word <i>’ish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Man</i>, the last word of Esth. 9, 6.</i> must be at the top of the column<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">And at the beginning of the first line.</i> and each <i>we’eth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Sign of the defined accusative which occurs ten times, each preceding the names of the ten sons.</i> at the end [of the lines, a total] of eleven lines,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The first line consisting of <i>’ish</i> on the right and <i>we’eth</i> on the left, the next nine lines having the name on the right and <i>we’eth</i> on the left, while the eleventh line has the name <i>Wayezatha</i> on the right and <i>‘asereth</i> (<i>ten</i>) on the left.</i> and <i>‘asereth</i> at the end of the column. Thus, in reading transversely, the columns<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">On the right (consisting of <i>’ish</i> and the ten names), and the left (ten times <i>we’eth</i> and <i>‘asereth</i>).</i> appear laced and closely corded like transverse lacing [of a web].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of V is corrupt and the translation follows GRA, N.Y. and Jastrow.</i> It symbolizes that when [the ten sons] fall there will be no rising for them.<br>The phrase <i>’aḥar haddebarim … Hammedatha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 3, 1, E.V. <i>After these things did king Ahasuerus promote Haman the son of Hammedatha</i>.</i> was translated by R. Joseph as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joseph is the reputed author of a later Aramaic translation of the Prophets and Hagiographa.</i> ‘After these things did king Ahasuerus promote Haman the son of Hammedatha the Aggagite, son of Seraḥ,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">A name derived from a root ‘to stink’. The object of the genealogy is to trace Haman, the symbol of the enemies of Jews throughout their history, through a chain of despicable men to Esau, the brother but enemy of the patriarch Jacob.</i> son of Buza, son of Aflutos,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Buza means ‘contempt’, and Aflutos is probably Plutos, a heathen god.</i> son of Deosis,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Dionysus (?).</i> son of Padom,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Or, Pados (putrid?).</i> son of Ma‘adi,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘sickness’.</i> son of Be‘alkon,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning is ‘cunning’.</i> son of Antamarus,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Signifying ‘crushed stones’.</i> son of Ḥarum,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Or, Ḥuram, possibly the heathen god Ares.</i> son of Hadoram,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Or, Heridos, i.e. Herod who is known in history as a tyrant.</i> son of Shazar,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning ‘hip disease’</i> son of Nagar,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Or, possibly, Negad (a whip).</i> son of Parshandatha,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following were also names of two of Haman’s sons.</i> son of Wayezatha, son of ‘Amaleḳ,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The ancestor of the people who were the first to attack Israel after their exodus from Egypt.</i> son of the concubine of Eliphaz the firstborn of Esau; he advanced him and set his seat above all his princes and his servants, because pride goeth before destruction’.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The last four words come from Prov. 16, 18. The translation of the paragraph embodies most of GRA’s emendations.</i>
R. Ze‘ira said: The <i>waw</i> of <i>Wayezatha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 9, 9.</i> must project like a boat-pole on the river Libruth.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the <i>waw</i> must be longer than the other letters. The ruling is quoted from Meg. 16b (Sonc. ed., p. 99). The river has not been identified.</i>
