If a person holds a scroll of the Torah [in order to read it], there is a difference of opinion between two <i>Tanna’im</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">On the question whether the prescribed benedictions should be said while the scroll is open or rolled.</i> One declared that he should open it, look [for the passage which is to be read], roll it up and then say the benediction;<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Were one to say it while the scroll is open, people might erroneously assume that the benediction is written in the Torah (Meg. 32a, Sonc. ed., pp. 192f).</i> but the other declared that he should open it, look [for the required passage] and say the benediction.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Because nobody could be so ignorant as to be unaware that the benedictions do not form a part of the text of the Torah (Meg. <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> What is the reason?<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">That the scroll must be open when the benediction is said.</i> Because it is written, <i>And when he opened it, all the people stood up;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Neh. 8, 5 referring to the scroll of the Torah.</i> and what follows? <i>And Ezra blessed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. he said the prescribed benedictions.</i> <i>the Lord, the great God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 6. As Ezra <i>opened</i> the scroll and <i>blessed the Lord</i>, so everyone who reads the Torah must have the scroll open when the benedictions are said.</i> Whereby did he proclaim His greatness?<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">As implied in the words <i>the great God</i>.</i> Rab said: He proclaimed His greatness by pronouncing the Ineffable Name.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Tetragrammaton.</i> Raba said: He proclaimed His greatness by the recital of the prescribed benediction.<br>What is the form of the benedictions?<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Before and after the reading in the Torah.</i> In an assembly of ten<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A minimum of ten males over the age of thirteen is required for congregational worship.</i> one says, ‘Bless ye the Lord Who is to be blessed’.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">And then follow the benedictions given later for an individual in private reading.</i> In the case of an individual, when he rises early to read in the Torah, he says, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast given us a Torah from heaven, everlasting life from the heights. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who givest the Torah’. Then<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">At the conclusion of the reading.</i> he rolls up the scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This is a unanimous ruling. It is upheld even by the Rabbi who maintained that the scroll must be open when the first benediction is said.</i> and says, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast given us a Torah of truth and hast planted everlasting life in our midst. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who givest the Torah’.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The benedictions now in use are shorter (P.B., p. 147).</i>
Similarly when one reads from the Prophets,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>hafṭarah</i> which is read after the conclusion of the prescribed section of the Torah.</i> a benediction is said as follows: ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast chosen good prophets, and hast found pleasure in their words which were spoken in truth. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who hast chosen the Torah and Moses Thy servant, and Israel Thy people, and the prophets of truth and righteousness’.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Wehaẓẓedeḳ</i>, the noun being preceded by <i>he</i>, the definite article. This is the version adopted by the Sefaradim, while the Ashkenazim omit the article (cf. P.B., p. 149).</i>
And at the end [of the reading of the portion from the Prophets], one says the following benedictions: ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Rock of all worlds, righteous through all generations, O faithful God, Who sayest and doest, Who speakest<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">V reads <i>medabber</i>, without the article, as in the current version (P.B., <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> and fulfillest, all Whose words are truth and righteousness’. The people forthwith stand and say:<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the present usage when the reader proceeds to say the benedictions mentioned in Rule 11.</i> ‘Faithful art Thou, O Lord our God, faithful are Thy words, ever faithful, living and enduring are Thy name and remembrance; and do Thou reign<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V reads ‘He will reign’, H ‘Thou wilt reign’.</i> continually over us for ever and ever’. This point<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Whether the benediction is to be said while sitting or standing.</i> is one of the disagreements between the scholars of the East and West; the former give the response<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">To the reader, with the benediction.</i> while sitting and the latter while standing. But wherever two <i>Tanna’im</i> or two <i>’Amora’im</i> differ, and we are not certain with whom the <i>halakah</i> agrees, we follow him who maintains the stricter view. How much more then must we, in regard to the praise<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The benedictions.</i> of the Life of all the worlds,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">[On this designation of God, cf. Marmorstein, <i>The Old Rabbinic Doctrine of God</i>, I, p. 85.]</i> which is a matter of the heart,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">V reads <i>galuï</i> (revealed), M and H <i>taluï</i> (dependent).</i> teach our disciples as the rule to be practised that the <i>halakah</i> is in agreement with him who upholds the stricter view;<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Following the emended text of GRA.</i> as Scripture states, <i>The Lord hath made every thing for His own purpose</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 16, 4. There is consequently all the more reason why the stricter view should be followed, viz. that the blessings and praises of God be said while standing.</i>
After that<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">After the people have concluded their benedictions (see the preceding Rule).</i> the <i>mafṭir</i> resumes his recital<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘subject’.</i> and says, ‘Faithful art Thou, O Lord our God, and faithful are Thy words, and not one of Thy words shall return void, for Thou art a faithful<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The current version adds ‘and merciful’ (P.B., p. 149).</i> God and King. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, the God Who art faithful in all Thy words’.
‘Comfort,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This is the continuation of the benedictions after the <i>hafṭarah</i>. The version now in use (P.B., <i>loc. cit.</i>) reads ‘have mercy’ instead of ‘comfort’ (GRA emends accordingly), and ‘O Lord our God’ and ‘Thy city’ are omitted.</i> O Lord our God, Zion Thy city, for it is the home of our life, and save her that is grieved in spirit speedily, even in our days. [40<i>b</i>] Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who makest Zion joyful through her children.’
‘Gladden us, O Lord our God,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘with Elijah’ to ‘swear unto him’ is represented in V by ‘etc.’ M and H cite the benediction in full.</i> with Elijah the prophet, Thy servant, and with the kingdom of the house of David, Thine anointed. Soon may he come and rejoice our hearts. Suffer not a stranger to sit upon his throne, nor let others any longer inherit his glory; for by Thy holy name Thou didst swear unto him, that his light should not be quenched for ever. In his days Judah shall be saved, and Israel shall dwell safely; and this is his name whereby he shall be called: the Lord is our righteousness.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">‘In his days … righteousness’ is a quotation from Jer. 23, 6 and is now omitted from the benediction.</i> Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who causest the horn of salvation to flourish for Thy people Israel.’<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The present custom (in accordance with Pes. 117b, Sonc. ed., p. 603) is to conclude, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord, the Shield of David’.</i>
‘For the Torah, [for the divine service],<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">M and H insert this phrase which is included in P.B.</i> for the prophets and for this holy<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The ‘Sabbath’ or the name of the Festival is inserted here; and when a Festival coincides with the Sabbath both are mentioned.</i> … which Thou, O Lord our God, hast given us for holiness and for rest, for honour and for glory. For all these things, O Lord our God, we thank Thee, and continually bless<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The P.B. version reads, ‘Thee, blessed be Thy name by the mouth of every living being continually and for ever’.</i> Thy name, O God of our salvation. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who sanctifiest Israel and such and such a day.’<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For ‘such and such a day’ the name of the Festival is inserted.</i><br>An exception is made on the Sabbath when ‘Israel’ is not mentioned in the conclusion but only ‘Who sanctifiest the Sabbath’; because the Sabbath preceded Israel, as it is written, <i>For in six days the Lord made heaven and earth, and on the seventh day He ceased from work and rested</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 31, 17.</i> and it is further said, <i>See that the Lord hath given you the sabbath</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVI, 29. The Sabbath was instituted at the creation of the world, and the use of the definite article in the second quotation indicates that it was a well-known institution at the time. The Festivals, on the other hand, were ordained after the people of Israel came into existence.</i> which implies that the Sabbath had already been in existence.

Chapter 14

And why did [the Rabbis] enact all these [benedictions]?<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>hafṭarah</i> as specified in the previous chapter.</i> In order to establish a correspondence between the seven benedictions [of the <i>hafṭarah</i>] and the seven readers.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum number who must be called to the reading of the Torah every Sabbath.</i> You may also similarly explain the reason for the twenty-two verses read in the [Book of a] Prophet: twenty-two corresponding to the seven readers, each of whom must read [a minimum of] three verses, and one corresponding to the superintendent of the Synagogue services.
All that had been said<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding Rule concerning the minimum number of verses to be read in the Prophet.</i> applies only where the text was neither translated nor expounded; but where it was translated or expounded<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">So that much of the time of the worshippers was taken up by the translation or exposition.</i> the <i>mafṭir</i> reads three, five or six verses in the Prophet, and this suffices.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The reading is curtailed for the convenience of the congregation.</i>
In the case of Ruth, the Song of Songs, Ecclesiastes,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA and H.</i> Lamentations and Esther,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">These books are read respectively on Pentecost, Passover, Tabernacles, the Fast of Ab and <i>Purim</i>.</i> it is necessary to say the benediction, ‘Concerning the reading of the <i>Megillah</i>’,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The full benediction is: ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast sanctified us by Thy commandments, and hast given us command concerning the reading of the <i>Megillah’</i> (P.B., p. 276). The present custom is to say the benediction for the scroll of Esther only. The others are read usually from a printed book without a benediction.</i> although it is included in the Hagiographa.
One who reads in the Hagiographa must say, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast sanctified us by Thy commandments and hast given us command to read in the Holy Writings’.
[The Rabbis] enacted that, as regards [the reading of] the scroll of Esther, the benediction on the ‘season’ must be said;<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Like the benediction in the preceding note it begins with ‘Blessed art Thou … universe’ and concludes with ‘Who hast kept us in life, and hast preserved us, and enabled us to reach this season’.</i> and after the conclusion of the reading the reader must say, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, O God Who dost plead our cause, judge our suit and avenge our wrong, Who redeemest and savest us from the hand of all tyrants. Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, Helper and Saviour’.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the version in P.B., p. 276.</i> The benediction is said<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The reason for the benediction is given to explain why it must begin with ‘Blessed art Thou’, although it would appear to be a continuation of the benediction for the <i>Megillah</i> which includes this beginning.</i> on account of the humiliation of the Torah, while others maintain that it is said on account of the humiliation of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Whose vindication is celebrated. As it is independent of the <i>Megillah</i>, it must consequently begin again with the usual formula. For ‘Torah’, M and H read ‘the Divine Presence’ affronted by Haman’s plot against God’s people.</i>
[The Rabbis] further laid down that it is also necessary to offer praise and thanksgiving [in the benediction] for the redemption and release.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">From threatened destruction.</i> Then one concludes: ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord, God of vengeance, Who dost requite enemies according to their deeds, art a Shield to the righteous, and savest Thy people from the hands of their adversaries’. After that the righteous are lauded: ‘Blessed be Mordecai, blessed be Esther, blessed be all Israel’. Rab, however, declared that it is necessary to say, ‘Accursed be Haman and accursed be his sons’. R. Phinehas said: It is necessary to say, ‘Harbonah be remembered for good’.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">He contributed to Haman’s downfall (cf. Esth. 7, 9). For the version now in use, cf. P.B., p. 277.</i>
R. Berekiah, quoting R. Jeremiah who had it from R. Ḥiyya, who reported it in the name of R. Joḥanan, said: When R. Jonathan came to the verse, <i>Whom Nebuchadnezzar … had carried away</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Esth 2, 6.</i> he used to exclaim, ‘O the wicked Nebuchadnezzar, may his bones be crushed! the good is rewarded<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘to mix’ (drinks), hence ‘to serve drinks’ and, metaphorically, ‘retribution, recompense’.</i> according to his goodness and the wicked according to his wickedness, as it is stated, <i>The memory of the righteous shall be for a blessing; but the name of the wicked shall rot</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 10, 7. This verse justifies the execration of Nebuchadnezzar and (in Rule 6) of Haman.</i>
The <i>mafṭir</i> recites also the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained below. The recitation of the <i>Shema‘</i> is deemed to be a great honour which compensates for the lesser dignity conferred by the reading of the <i>hafṭarah</i>.</i> Of what Shema‘ has this been said?<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shema‘</i> is recited several times during the service.</i> Of the one [recited when] the scroll of the Torah [is taken from the ark].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the next Rule.</i> How does one begin?<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The collection of Biblical verses recited when the Torah scroll is taken from the ark.</i> <i>Happy are they that dwell in Thy house</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 84, 5.</i> Then the <i>mafṭir</i> stands and recites, <i>There is none like unto Thee among the gods, O Lord; and there are no works like Thine</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXXXVI, 8.</i> <i>Who is like unto Thee, O Lord, among the mighty? Who is like unto Thee, glorious in holiness, fearful in praises, doing wonders?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 11.</i> <i>Thy kingdom is a kingdom for all ages, and Thy dominion endureth throughout all generations</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 145, 13.</i> <i>The Lord is King, the Lord hath reigned, the Lord shall reign for ever and ever</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Compounded from Ps. 10, 16, XCIII, 1 (E.V. <i>reigneth</i>) and Ex. 15, 18.</i> <i>The Lord was pleased, for His righteousness’ sake, to make the teaching great and glorious</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 42, 21.</i> <i>The Lord will give strength unto His people; the Lord will bless His people with peace</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 29, 11.</i> <i>Thou art the Lord, even Thou alone; Thou hast made heaven, the heaven of heavens, with all their host, the earth and all things that are therein, the seas and all that is in them and Thou preservest them all; and the host of heaven worshippeth Thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Neh. 9, 6.</i>
Thereupon<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘immediately’.</i> the <i>mafṭir</i> steps forward,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘enters’.</i> takes the scroll of the Torah and says, <i>Hear, O Israel</i>, chanting the first verse,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the whole of Deut. 6, <i>4</i>.</i> and the people say it after him in response.
[The <i>mafṭir</i>] then elevates the Torah scroll and says: ‘One is our God, great is our Lord, holy and revered is His name for ever and ever’.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of GRA. V, M and H omit the sentence.</i> Then he repeats: ‘One is our God, great is our Lord, holy is He. One is our God, great is our Lord, holy and revered is His name’. [This is said three times] corresponding in number<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The three repetitions of ‘one is our God’, etc.</i> to the three patriarchs; others hold that it corresponds to the three <i>kedushshoth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘sanctifications’, i.e. ‘Holy, holy, holy’ with suitable introductions said three times in the morning service (cf. P.B., pp. 39, 45 and 73).</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of the verses begun in Rule 8.</i> <i>‘Thy righteousness also, O God, reacheth unto high heaven; Thou Who hast done great things, O God, who is like unto Thee?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 71, 19.</i> <i>O Lord, Thy name endureth for ever; Thy memorial, O Lord, throughout all generations</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXXXV, 13.</i> Let all ascribe strength to God and give honour to the Torah. <i>O magnify the Lord with me, and let us exalt His name together</i>.’<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid</i> XXXIV, 4.</i> The Torah scroll must be elevated at <i>Hear, O Israel</i>, at the threefold declaration of the unity of God, and at <i>O magnify the Lord with me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For the modern ritual, cf. P.B., p. 145.</i>
One must also say:<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In concluding the verses and prayers cited in the preceding Rules.</i> ‘Magnified and hallowed,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This word is included by GRA and H.</i> praised and glorified, exalted and extolled, uplifted, honoured and adored<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">V adds in parenthesis ‘honoured and revered’ (so also M).</i> above all be the name of the supreme King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, in the worlds which He hath created, this world and the world to come, in accordance with His desire and the desire of all His people, the house of Israel. May His kingdom be revealed and made visible unto us speedily and at a near time; and may He build His house in our days, and be gracious unto our remnant and unto the remnant of His people Israel in His mercy and in the abundance of His lovingkindness, in peace and grace, kindness and compassion. May the Omnipresent do all this for us for the sake of His great name; and say ye, Amen’.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the version in P.B., pp. 145f.</i>
After that he elevates the Torah scroll and says: ‘One is our God, great is our Lord, holy and revered is His name for ever and ever’. Then he begins to chant, ‘The Lord is God, the Lord is His name’. The congregation says it after him in response; then he repeats it twice and the congregation responds twice.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This Rule is enclosed in round brackets in V. It appears to be a duplicate of Rule 10, but both M and H include it.</i>
The scroll of the Torah is immediately unrolled a space of three columns and is elevated so as to show the face of the script to the people standing on the right and on the left. Then it is turned round towards the front and towards the rear; for it is a precept for all men and women to see the script,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Before it is read. The ritual here described is followed by the Sefaradim. The Ashkenazim elevate the scroll after the conclusion of the reading (cf. P.B., p. 148).</i> to bend their knees and exclaim, ‘<i>And this is the Torah which Moses set before the children of Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 4, 44. For other verses which are now added, cf. P.B., <i>loc. cit.</i></i> <i>The Torah of the Lord is perfect, restoring the soul</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 19, 8. This verse is not included now.</i> The <i>mafṭir</i> then hands it over to the superintendent of the Synagogue services who returns it to the first<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the head’ (so GRA). V has ‘to cover the heads’, which is obscure.</i> of those who are to be called to the reading, because it is not an honour for the Torah to be left alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘single’. It is therefore necessary for the <i>mafṭir</i>, the superintendent and one of the congregation who is to read a portion to accompany the scroll.</i> Similarly it is not proper for the precentor to stand alone before the reading-desk; so [two persons] should stand with him, one on his right and the other on his left, [the number] corresponding to that of the patriarchs.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">V inserts in parenthesis a ruling which seems displaced although it is included by GRA, M and H. It reads: ‘Some say that [the <i>mafṭir</i>] recites the <i>Shema‘</i> with the benediction “Who formest light” (P.B., p. 128) and “Holy” (p. 131) because of the benediction which he says “For the Torah, for the divine service”, etc. (pp. 149f)’.</i> The pure-minded men of Jerusalem acted in this manner: When the Torah scroll was taken out of the ark and when it was returned they followed it as a mark of respect.
A minor<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The age is defined in Rule 17.</i> may read in the Torah and translate it, but he may not read the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. X, 7, p. 258 nn. 24ff.</i> by reciting ‘Who formest light’;<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The first of the benedictions before the <i>Shema‘</i> (cf. P.B., pp. 37f).</i> nor may he stand before the reading-desk,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘pass before’, etc., to act as reader of the prayers for the congregation.</i> nor lift up his hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">To pronounce the priestly benediction if he is a <i>Kohen</i> (cf. P.B., p. 238a).</i> A <i>poḥeaḥ</i>, that is one whose knees are exposed or whose clothes are torn, or one whose head is uncovered, may read the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i>. Others say that if a person’s knees [are exposed] or his clothes torn he may read the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i>, but one may not do so when his head is uncovered, since it is not permitted to utter the divine names [with uncovered head]. In either case he may translate, but not read in the Torah, nor stand before the reading-desk, nor lift up his hands.
A blind man may recite the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i> and translate. R. Judah said: One who has never seen the light may not read the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since it contains thanksgivings for the creation and enjoyment of light.</i>
The minor of whom the Rabbis spoke<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In Rule 15.</i> is one who is under twelve years of age. A person who is above that age may read the parts of the <i>Shema‘</i>, but may not stand before the reading-desk nor lift up his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read: ‘and he who stands before the reading-desk and he who lifts up his hands’.</i> until he is twenty years of age and bearded. If he has no beard, although he is twenty years of age, he is forbidden to do so because<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘although’ for ‘he is … because’.</i> he looks like an eunuch. Others say that even an actual eunuch is permitted [to perform these acts]. If, however, a person has grown a beard even from the age of eighteen, he may stand before the reading-desk and lift up his hands.
As regards the scroll of Esther, all are qualified to read it except a deaf person, an imbecile and a minor. R. Judah declared a minor to be qualified. R. Levi said: The scroll of Esther must be read [on <i>Purim</i>] at night and repeated in the daytime, as it is stated, <i>So that my glory may sing praise to Thee</i>—i.e. by night, <i>and not be silent</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 30, 13.</i>—i.e. by day. (Therefore it is customary to read it at the termination of the Sabbath of two [successive weeks].)<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is later explained that there was an ancient practice for the congregation to read the Book of Esther on the two Saturday evenings before <i>Purim</i>.</i> [41<i>a</i>] When R. Meir once passed by a Synagogue he heard the reading of<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the voices’.</i> a section [of the scroll of Esther].<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">On a Saturday night.</i> ‘How long,’ he said to them, ‘will you be cutting up the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Which, in its wider sense, includes the Book of Esther.</i> into sections?’<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘making the Torah callings up, callings up’.</i> [‘We do it’], they replied, ‘on account of <i>my glory may sing praise unto Thee</i>’, and he allowed them [to continue the practice]. People have adopted the practice of reading it on two<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA.</i> Saturday nights of <i>Adar</i> until fifteen days of the month have elapsed. In what manner is it read? At [the termination of] the first Sabbath of <i>Adar</i> the people read together up to <i>In that night</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 6, 1; half the book.</i> and on the next Saturday night they read from <i>In that night</i> up to <i>and speaking peace to all his seed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">To the end of the book.</i><br>In the case of the Song of Songs, it is read on the last [two] nights of the [Passover] Festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">So one MS. quoted by M. V and others add ‘of the exile’, which cannot be justified since only the last day of Passover is a ‘Festival of the exile’.</i> half of it on the first night and the other half on the second night.<br>Ruth [is read] on the termination of the first day of Pentecost to [the end of] its [first] half, and concluded on the termination of the last day of the Festival. Others hold that with all the Festivals, we begin to read [the respective scrolls] on the Saturday night preceding them. People have also adopted the following procedure, viz. that no ruling is authoritatively laid down unless a custom<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">On which it is based.</i> had been definitely established.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read: ‘unless it is a custom’.</i> As regards the saying of the Rabbis that ‘a custom cancels a ruling’,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. j.B.M. VII, 1, 11b.]</i> it applies to a custom of the pious men of old; but a custom which has no support from the Torah is only like an error of judgment.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘weighing of an opinion’.</i>
As we read [the weekly portion] from the Torah and the <i>hafṭarah</i> from the Prophet in daytime, so it is obligatory to read the scroll of Esther in daytime;<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This is a repetition of R. Levi’s teaching quoted in the preceding paragraph, except that it is more specific. The word <i>welishnothah</i> (translated ‘repeated’) might be mistranslated as ‘the study of the Mishnah’ on the <i>Megillah</i>. The wording here precludes such a rendering.</i> as it is stated, <i>In the day that the enemies of the Jews hoped</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 9, 1.</i> on which it was taught in the Mishnah:<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Meg. II, 5 (Sonc. ed., p. 124).</i> The whole of the day is a proper time for the reading of the scroll of Esther.
There is no difference between scrolls [of Scripture] and <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i> save that the scrolls,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The subject is continued in XV, 1.</i> etc. If a <i>tefillah</i> had worn out, no <i>mezuzah</i> may be made of it;<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>mezuzah</i> is deemed to be of a lesser degree of sanctity.</i> but if a <i>mezuzah</i> had worn out it may be made into <i>tefillin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If the margins are still in a good condition and fit for the purpose.</i> because in sacred matters ascent to a higher degree of sanctity is allowed but not descent to a lesser degree.

Chapter 15

There is no difference between scrolls [of Scripture] and <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i> save that the scrolls may be written in any language,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For use in the Synagogue. Two such official translations are known: one in Aramaic, the Targum Onḳelos, and another in Greek made by Aquila under Rabbinic supervision.</i> whereas <i>tefillin</i> and <i>mezuzoth</i> may only be written in the Assyrian script.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew square characters are described as ‘Assyrian’ because they came into common use for religious writings after the return from the Babylonian exile (cf. Sanh. 21b, 22a, Sonc. ed., pp. 119ff and notes).</i> Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel said: Even scrolls [of Scripture] were permitted by the Rabbis to be written only in Greek.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">[For the reason, cf. Meg. 9b (Sonc. ed., p. 50).]</i>
Although Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">An eminent authority.</i> said, ‘Even scrolls [of Scripture] were permitted by the Rabbis to be written only in Greek’, the Sages did not agree with him. For Rabbi related:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in V, 15 (p. 236). The following statements in the name of Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel and the Sages respectively prove that both maintained their views.</i> It once happened that Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel was standing at the side of a building [in course of construction] on the Temple Mount, when a Targum of the book of Job was brought before him. He ordered the builder, ‘Bury it under the course of stones’. But the Sages also maintained their ruling, because we have learned in the Mishnah:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Shab. XVI, 1 (Sonc. ed., p. 563).</i> All holy writings<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Pentateuch, Prophets and Hagiographa.</i> may be saved from a fire<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath by being removed from one domain to another, although this is not permitted with ordinary objects.</i> irrespective of whether they are used in public reading<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Such as the Prophets from which the <i>hafṭarah</i> is selected.</i> or not;<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">viz. the Hagiographa.</i> and although they are written in any language, they must be stored away.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">When they are worn out and no longer fit for use. Sacred writings must never be destroyed.</i> And why do we not read [some of these scrolls]? On account of [possible] neglect of the House of Study.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath when lectures are delivered in the Houses of Study. By engaging in the reading of the books mentioned the public would neglect the lectures.</i><br>The sheath of a scroll may be saved<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">From a fire on the Sabbath.</i> together with the scroll, and the <i>tefillin</i> bag together with the <i>tefillin</i>, although they contain money.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Which otherwise would not be permitted to be moved.</i> To what place may one rescue them? To a closed alley.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Where movement on the Sabbath is less restricted than in the public domain.</i> Ben Bathyra said: Even into an open alley.
Although the Sages have said that [some of] the Holy Writings may only be read from [the Sabbath] afternoon service onwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">When there are no public lectures in the Houses of Study (cf. Shab. 116b, Sonc. ed., p. 572).</i> yet they may be studied and expounded. If it is necesssary [to refer to] a word,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In Shab. <i>loc. cit.</i> the reading is ‘a verse’.</i> one may take a scroll and examine it. As regards secular documents, however, although they have ruled lines,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Like sacred scrolls, and one is less likely to forget the Sabbath and insert notes or corrections.</i> it is forbidden to read them [on the Sabbath].
Benedictions,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Such as those of the <i>‘Amidah</i> (P.B., pp. 44-53) and the like.</i> in which many passages of the Torah were quoted,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘written’.</i> must not be saved from a fire [on the Sabbath].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">They do not possess the same degree of sanctity as the scrolls of Scripture.</i> Hence it was declared: They who write down benedictions are like those who burn the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since these could not be saved if a fire broke out on the Sabbath.</i><br>It was related of a man who wrote benedictions that R. Ishmael went to examine him. As soon as he heard the sound of R. Ishmael’s steps, he seized a bundle of written sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Containing benedictions and selections from the Torah.</i> and threw it into a bowl of water. In the following words [did R. Ishmael] address him, ‘The punishment for the latter act<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Destruction of the sheets containing sacred texts by water.</i> is greater than for the former’.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Writing benedictions which included passages from Scripture.</i>
Why did he<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">viz. R. Ishmael. So N.Y.; V reads ‘they’.</i> so address him? On account of the passages from the Torah which were quoted in them.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the benedictions; and could not, therefore, be saved from a fire on the Sabbath.</i><br>For<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The following reason has no connection with the beginning of Rule 5. It refers back to Rule 2 above which lays down that one must not read certain books of the Scripture during the hours when lectures are delivered in the Houses of Study (N.Y.).</i> R. Simeon b. Yoḥai taught: He who occupies himself with [only the study of] Scripture indulges in an indifferent practice;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘a measure and no measure’.</i> with Mishnah it is a practice for which a reward is conferred; as for one who occupies himself with <i>Shas</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. letters <i>shin samek</i>, pronounced <i>Shas</i>, stand for <span dir="rtl">ששה סדרים</span> ‘the six orders’ of the Mishnah or of the combined Mishnah and Gemara. Here it evidently refers to the Gemara only. The corresponding passage in B.M. 33a (Sonc. ed., p. 206) reads ‘Gemara’. It has been suggested that the proper reading is ‘Talmud’ (cf. B.M. <i>loc. cit.</i>, n. 6).</i> there is no worthier practice, provided he had first studied Scripture and Mishnah.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">It is accordingly proved that the study of Mishnah and Gemara, which necessarily includes the Scriptures, is of far higher importance than of Scripture only.</i>
Always run to the Mishnah more than to the <i>Shas</i>. R. Jose b. Abin said: [The preceding Rule only applies to the time] before Rabbi had edited<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘sunk’, an unusual verb in such a connection. [Instead of <span dir="rtl">שקע</span>, H mentions a MS. variant <span dir="rtl">קבע</span> (fixed).]</i> the <i>Mishnayoth</i>, but now that Rabbi has edited the <i>Mishnayoth</i> run to the Mishnah more than to the <i>Shas</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">So N.Y. as required by the context. V, M and H read: ‘run to the <i>Shas</i> more than to the Mishnah.’</i>
[The Sages], however, said: Scripture has been compared to water, the Mishnah to wine, and the <i>Shas</i> to spiced-wine. The world cannot exist without water, it cannot exist without wine, and it cannot exist without spiced-wine; but a rich man enjoys all three of them. So, too, it is impossible for the world to exist without Scripture and without Mishnah, but it can never exist without the <i>Shas</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Which includes all.</i>
The Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The term here embraces the whole of the Scriptures.</i> has also been compared to salt, the Mishnah to pepper, and the <i>Shas</i> to spices. The world cannot exist without salt, without pepper and without spices; but the rich man enjoys all three. So, too, it is impossible for the world to exist without Scripture, without Mishnah and without the <i>Shas</i>.
Happy is the man whose labour is in [the study of] the <i>Shas</i>. Not that he should skip Scripture and the Mishnah and come [direct] to the <i>Shas;</i> but he should learn Scripture and Mishnah with the object of coming to the <i>Shas</i>. Of such a person it is stated, <i>The rich man’s wealth is his strong city, and as a high wall in his own conceit</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 18, 11.</i>
Abba Gurion of Ẓadian<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Bethsaida in Galilee.</i> said in the name of Abba Guria:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA in agreement with Ḳid. 82a (Sonc. ed., p. 423). V reads ‘said’ after the first ‘Gurion’ and a second ‘Gurion’ for ‘Guria’.</i> A man should not teach his son to be an ass-driver,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">These, when sent on a long journey, hire themselves to others and so make a profit by using the time which belongs to the original employer.</i> sailor, waggoner, shepherd<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He feeds his flock in other persons’ fields.</i> or shopkeeper,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He gives false measure or sells adulterated goods.</i> because their occupations are robbery.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The descriptions are no doubt a reflection of the standards of the time.</i> R. Judah,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Differing from Abba Gurion.</i> however, quoting him,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Abba Guria.</i> said: Ass-drivers are mostly wicked, but sailors are mostly pious.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The perils of the sea turn their thoughts to God.</i> The best of physicians are [destined] for Gehinnom<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Because they may be guilty of causing loss of life through neglect or over-confidence; or, by trusting too much in their own skill, they deny the power of God [with this passage, cf. ARN XXXVI, 5, and notes].</i> and the most worthy of butchers is Amalek’s partner.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In his desire to avoid financial loss, he sells unwholesome meat and thereby spreads disease. ‘Amalek’ is the type of Israel’s enemies.</i> Bastards are mostly keen-witted,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">They are like their cunning fathers who enticed their mothers.</i> slaves are mostly arrogant,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <span dir="rtl">גאים</span> with GRA. V, M and H have <span dir="rtl">נאים</span>, ‘beautiful’, an improbable reading.</i> the children of reputed parents are mostly bashful, and children usually resemble the mother’s brother.<br>R. Simeon b. Yoḥai taught: Kill the best of the heathens in time of war;<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">[For the various forms of this saying, cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> II, p. 86, n. 3. The qualifying phrase ‘in the time of war’ he regards as a later addition to soften the harshness of the utterance. It was called forth in a time of bitter oppression and is not to be taken as typical of Jewish ethics. On the subject, cf. J. S. Bloch, <i>Israel and the Nations</i>, pp. 204ff.]</i> crush the brain of the best of serpents. The most worthy of women indulges in witchcraft. Happy is he who does the will of the Omnipresent.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Because he is delivered from all worldly temptations.</i> [41<i>b</i>]

Chapter 16

R. Nehorai said: I abandon all professions in the world and teach my son only Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA in agreement with Ḳid. 82a (Sonc. ed., p. 423).</i> because a man enjoys part of its reward in this world while the principal remains intact for the world to come. The other professions, however, are not so. For should a person come to sickness, old age or suffering and is unable to carry on his work, he would die of starvation; whereas with the Torah it is not so, but it protects him from all evil in his youth and gives him a future and hope in his old age. What does it say in Scripture of his youth? <i>But they that wait for the Lord shall renew their strength</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Isa 40, 31.</i> And what does it say of his old age? <i>They shall still bring forth fruit in old age</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 92, 15. In Ḳid. <i>loc. cit.</i> the Mishnah adds: ‘And thus it is said of our father Abraham, <i>And Abraham was old … and the Lord blessed Abraham in all things</i> (Gen. 24, 1)’.</i>
R. Joshua b. Levi said: With regard to the <i>’aggadta</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">So M and H. V has <i>‘’aggadta</i> written in it’.</i> he who writes it down has no share in the world to come,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>’Aggadta</i> must only be taught and learned orally.</i> he who expounds it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From a written text; similarly with ‘he who listens to it’.</i> is cursed,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘blessed’, a euphemism.</i> while he who listens to it receives no reward.<br>R. Ḥinnena b. Papa taught:<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and M. V reads, ‘but did not … teach?’, but there is no answer in the text.</i> <i>The Lord spoke with you face to face</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 5, 4.</i>—<i>face</i> implies two,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <i>panim</i> (face) is a singular noun with a plural form and is homiletically treated as denoting not less than two.</i> <i>to face</i> also implies two, thus providing four expressions of ‘face’ [alluding to] Scripture,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following three words are inserted by GRA and omitted by V.</i> Mishnah, <i>halakoth</i> and <i>’aggadoth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <i>’aggadoth</i> which have not been committed to writing.</i> an awestruck face for Scripture, a neutral face for Mishnah, a friendly<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA, lit. ‘a making clear’; V reads ‘smiling’.</i> face for <i>Shas</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Here it stands for <i>halakah</i> which is its main contents.</i> and a smiling<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA; V ‘friendly’.</i> face for <i>’aggadta</i>.
R. Nehemiah, in the name of R. Jacob b. Jannai, remarked on the text, <i>As in water face answereth to face:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 27, 19.</i> It is customary to have a master who desires to teach and a student who does not want to learn, or a student who desires to learn and a master who does not want to teach him; but in this case<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">viz. of God and Moses.</i> the Master desires to teach and the student to learn.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">V adds in parenthesis ‘Scripture and Mishnah, Gemara and <i>’aggadah’</i>. The words are included in M and H.</i>
R. Isaac Nappaḥa began his discourse as follows: <i>Stay me with dainties</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 2, 5, Heb. <span dir="rtl">באשישות</span>.</i> refers to the well-founded<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="rtl">מאוששות</span>, meaning ‘strong, firm’.</i> <i>halakoth; refresh me with apples</i> refers to the <i>’aggadoth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA; not in V. H reads: ‘these are the words of Torah’.</i> whose fragrance is pleasant like that of apples. <i>For I am love-sick</i>—R. Isaac said: In the past when money was plentiful<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">And people enjoyed leisure and an easy existence.</i> one longed to hear a word of the <i>halakah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires time for concentration of thought and serious application of the mind.</i> but now that money is scarce<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">And there is a hard struggle to earn a livelihood.</i> one longs to hear<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V omits ‘but now … hear’. [The reading of H is: ‘but now that money is scarce, and especially when we are oppressed by (lit. sick from) the government, one longs to hear a word from Scripture and a word of <i>’aggadah’</i>.]</i> a word of <i>’aggadah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Which is easy, consoling and entertaining.</i>
R. Tanḥum b. Ḥanilai said: If the Torah had been given as a clear-cut code,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘cut’.</i> no judge would have a <i>locus standi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘standing of feet’, reading with M and H <span dir="rtl">עמידת</span> for <span dir="rtl">עומדות</span>.</i> in laying down a ruling;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could not use his discretion and reasoning power.</i> but now a judge has a <i>locus standi</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The clause ‘but … <i>standi’</i> is inserted by GRA. It is lacking in V, M and H.</i> for if he declares a thing to be clean there are [authorities] who declare a thing in a similar condition to be unclean, and if he decides that it is unclean there are [authorities] who declare a thing in a similar condition to be clean.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He can always find authority in support of his view.</i>
R. Jannai said: The Torah which the Holy One, blessed be He, gave to Moses was delivered to him in forty-nine aspects of uncleanness and forty-nine aspects of cleanness,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. of prohibition and permissibility respectively.</i> as it is stated, <i>And his banner</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 2, 4, the continuation of the verse being <i>over me is love</i>. The verse is interpreted of the Torah, which is God’s <i>banner</i>, entrusted to Israel as a mark of His love. Each Heb. letter denotes a number.</i> (<i>wediglo</i>), the numerical value of its letters being forty-nine.
R. Abahu said in the name of R. Joḥanan: R. ‘Aḳiba had a distinguished<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘religiously scrupulous and meek’ (Rashi).</i> disciple who knew how to interpret the Torah in forty-nine aspects of uncleanness and forty-nine aspects of cleanness, not one reason being the same as another.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the note of GRA.</i> Consequently<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since, according to R. ‘Aḳiba’s disciple and R. Tanḥum (in Rule 8), the interpretations are as many on one side as on the other.</i> if those who rule a thing to be unclean are in the majority it is unclean, and if those who rule a thing to be clean are in the majority it is clean.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Because, in addition to their majority, they also have as many ancient authorities on whom to rely as their opponents.</i> Whence [did he learn] all these? [From his extensive studies], because he was learned in the Scriptures, expert in the Mishnah, distinguished in Talmud and brilliant<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘bright, intelligent, a reasoner’.</i> in <i>’aggadah</i>.
It was related of R. Joḥanan b. Zakkai that he did not neglect the study of a single passage of the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the Pentateuch.</i> He also studied [all] Scripture,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Including the Prophets and the Hagiographa.</i> Targum, Mishnah, <i>halakoth</i> and <i>’aggadoth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">M has ‘proverbs about foxes’; V and H insert ‘and proverbs’.</i> He learned everything. It was also related of him that he declared, ‘If all the heavens were sheets, all the trees quills and all the seas ink, they would not suffice for recording my wisdom which I acquired from my masters; and yet I have gained<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘set apart’.</i> no more of the wisdom of the Sages than a fly [acquires] which dips in the great sea and deprives it of the tiniest drop’.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. ARN XXV, 3, p. 126.]</i>
From whom did he<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai. Cf. for the eulogy, Suk. 28a (Sonc. ed., p. 123).</i> receive the Torah? From Hillel and Shammai. It was said of Hillel that he had not omitted to study any of the words of the Sages, even all languages, even the speech of mountains, hills and valleys, the speech of trees and herbs, the speech of wild beasts and cattle, the speech of demons<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">[For <i>shedim</i> (demons), H quotes a MS. variant <span dir="rtl">שירים</span> (songs).]</i> and parables. Why [did he study] all these? Because it is stated, <i>The Lord was pleased, for His righteousness’ sake, to make the teaching great and glorious</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 42, 21.</i>
R. Joshua b. Levi said: I have never looked into a book of <i>’aggadta</i> except once when I looked and found written therein that the one hundred and seventy-five sections of the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the Pentateuch.</i> in which occurs any expression of speaking, saying or commanding, correspond to the number of years of our father Abraham;<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Abraham lived 175 years (Gen. 25, 7).</i> for it is written, <i>Thou hast ascended on high, Thou hast led captivity captive; Thou hast received gifts</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Explained as the Torah given to Moses as a gift from God.</i> <i>for the sake of the man</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">viz. Abraham. Ps. 68, 19, E.V. <i>among men</i>.</i> and it is also written, <i>The greatest man</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Also explained of Abraham.</i> <i>among the Anakim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 14, 15.</i> On this account the Rabbis instituted one hundred and seventy-five orders in the Torah [to be read in public worship] every Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Completing the entire Pentateuch in 175 weeks, or three and a half years: fifty orders or sections in one year. This was the Jerusalem practice. In Babylon and at the present time the Pentateuch is completed every year.</i> [as regularly as the] continual burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 28, 10.</i>
The hundred and forty-seven psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The number of Psalms in the Heb. Bible is 150; but, according to Rabbinic opinion, I and II, IX and X, CXXXIV and CXXXV are each reckoned as one Psalm.</i> included in the Book of Psalms correspond to the number of years of Jacob.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Gen. 47, 28.</i> This is intended to teach you that all the praises which Israel offer to the Holy One, blessed be He, correspond to the number of years of our father Jacob. What is the reason?<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">GRA inserts the question.</i> [Scripture states,] <i>Yet Thou art holy, O Thou that art enthroned upon the praises of Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 22, 4. Israel is synonymous with Jacob.</i>
The hundred and twenty-three times that Israel respond with <i>Halleluyah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Not all these are to be found in M.T. or Psalms, but many such and other responses and refrains were frequently made orally. Cf. my articles on ‘Antiphony in Ancient Heb. Poetry’ (<i>Jewish Quarterly Review</i>, New Series, XXVI, pp. 199-219), ‘The Text and Ancient form of Recital’, etc. (<i>Journal of Biblical Literature</i>, LI, pp. 214-226), ‘Long and Shorter Versions of Ancient Heb. Poems’ (<i>American Journal of Semitic Languages and Literatures</i>, XLIX, pp. 15-31).</i> correspond to the number of years of Aaron.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 33, 39.</i> What is the reason?<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The question is inserted by GRA.</i> [Scripture states,] <i>Halleluyah, praise God beḳodsho</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 150, 1, E.V. <i>in His sanctuary</i>.</i> which [is interpreted as] ‘with His holy man’, i.e. Aaron, the holy man of God.<br>Nevertheless<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Although R. Joshua b. Levi only once looked into a written book of <i>’aggadta</i>. This remark refers back to Rule 10.</i> I was afraid at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The lapse continued to worry him.</i><br><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In V and M this is preceded by a parenthesis which virtually is a repetition of part of Rule 7, ‘R. ‘Aḳiba had a distinguished disciple’, etc.</i>A minor who is not allowed to read portions of the <i>Shema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. XIV, 15, p. 280.</i> must not say ‘Holy’<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 39.</i> in the benediction <i>Yoẓer</i> privately;<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoẓer</i> (Who formed) designates the first benediction before the <i>Shema‘</i>, beginning ‘Blessed’ (P.B., p. 37) and ending with ‘Creator of the luminaries’ (p. 39). The first <i>ḳedushshah</i> of the morning service is included in it.</i> but with a congregation he may respond together with the others. An adult, however, who reads portions of the <i>Shema‘</i> may say ‘Holy’ because it is in the order [of the Biblical text];<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He may therefore read it, not as a part of the divine service but as a passage of Scripture.</i> but as for the ‘Holy’ of the <i>‘Amidah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 45.</i> since he would have to say ‘We will reverence and sanctify Thee’,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Which is a part of the congregational service (P.B., p. 160). This form, now used in the <i>ḳedushshah</i> for the Sabbath <i>musaf</i>, was used formerly, and in certain congregations to-day, on week-days.</i> it is not proper for him to say it if less than ten adults are present. A minor, furthermore, may not be counted in the quorum of ten<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Being the minimum number of adult males required for a congregational service.</i> for <i>Bareku</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">‘Bless ye’, etc. (P.B., p. 37).</i> the reading of the Torah and <i>ḳaddish</i> until he is thirteen years of age; but no examination<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">As to whether the boy has also grown two pubic hairs.</i> is carried out in such cases.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The age of 13 is sufficient to establish puberty. ‘No examination … cases’ (GRA) corrects <i>nidbaḳin</i> (cleaved) in V to <i>nibdaḳin</i>.</i>.

Chapter 17

<i>The words of the wise are as goads … they are given from one shepherd;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Eccles. 12, 11.</i> and one Shepherd<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">[i.e. God; cf. ARN XVIII, 3, p. 105.]</i> has uttered them. Therefore they must be saved from a fire.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even on the Sabbath.</i> A book of the <i>’aggadta</i>, although not allowed to be written, must nevertheless be saved from a fire. What is the reason? <i>It is a time to work for the Lord; they have made void Thy law</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 119, 126, E.V. <i>for the Lord to work</i>. This implies that when the welfare of the people or the preservation of the Faith requires it, a law may be temporarily suspended.</i>
[The sequence of the Sabbath readings] is interrupted<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">To make room for the special reading for the particular occasion which replaces it, or is added to it, as explained below.</i> for New Moons, <i>Ḥanukkah, Purim</i>, fasts, <i>ma‘amadoth</i> and the Day of Atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In the afternoon service, even when it falls on a Sabbath.</i>
In what manner are the interruptions made if the New Moon or the days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> fall on a Sabbath? [In the former case] seven persons read the regular Sabbath section while the eighth reads that of the New Moon, viz. the two portions<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and M read ‘breaks’.</i> <i>And on the sabbath day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the passage Num. 28, 9f which begins with these words.</i> and <i>And in your new moons;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The passage <i>ibid.</i> 11-15.</i> and if the first day of <i>Ḥanukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Or any of the other days. Unlike our present custom, they read the same portion on the Sabbath irrespective of which day of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> it was.</i> falls on a Sabbath, seven read [the section] of the Sabbath while the eighth reads, <i>And it came to pass on the day that Moses had made an end</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 7, 1.</i> up to <i>so he made the candlestick</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VIII, 5.</i>
[42<i>a</i>] What<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">From here to <i>Then came Amalek</i> is the reading of GRA.</i> is the procedure on <i>Purim?</i> If <i>Purim</i> falls on a Sabbath, seven read [the section] of the Sabbath while the eighth reads <i>Then came Amalek</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 17, 8-16.</i> The three fast days<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In commemoration of the three days of fasting ordained by Mordecai and Esther (Esth. 4, 16).</i> are not observed on consecutive days but are separated [and observed] on Monday, Thursday and Monday. Our Rabbis in the land of Israel had the custom of fasting after <i>Purim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Not, as others, before it.</i> on account of [the victory over] Nicanor and his associates,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This occurred on the thirteenth of Adar, the day before <i>Purim</i> [cf. 1 Macc. VII, 49; 2 Macc. XV, 36; Josephus, Antiq. XII, x, 5], on account of which fasting was prohibited. Nicanor was a Syrian commander who fell in the Maccabean war in the battle of Adasa in 161 <small>b.c.e.</small></i> and also because the commemoration of sad events is postponed rather than antedated.
[What was the procedure in the case of] <i>ma‘amadoth?</i> As it was taught:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. Ta‘an. 27b (Sonc. ed., p. 145).]</i> The men of the <i>ma‘amadoth</i> used to fast [as follows]: On every Monday for the sake of those who sail on the sea, as it is written, <i>And God said: Let there be a firmament in the midst of the waters;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 6. The mention of <i>water</i> is a call to prayer on behalf of those who sail over the sea.</i> on Tuesdays for the sake of wayfarers, as it is written, <i>And God said: Let the waters under the heaven be gathered together;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 9, which is followed by <i>And let the dry land appear</i>.</i> on Wednesdays for the sake of children that they should not be attacked by croup, as it is written, <i>And God said: Let there be lights</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14, children being to the home what the heavenly luminaries are to the world.</i> the written form being defective;<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>waw</i> is missing in the noun <span dir="rtl">מארת</span> (lights).</i> on Thursdays they fasted for the sake of pregnant women and nursing mothers that they should not lose their children, as it is said, <i>And God said: Let the waters swarm with swarms</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 20, followed by <i>living creatures</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> On the Sabbath eve and at the conclusion of the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Sunday, as explicitly mentioned in Ta‘an. 26a (Sonc. ed., pp. 136f).</i> they did not fast from respect for the Sabbath. Some declare<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As a reason why they did not fast on Sundays.</i> that at the twilight of the Sabbath eve an additional soul is given to every Israelite, and after the Sabbath it is taken from him.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The body is then weakened, and fasting is held to be too great a hardship.</i> Another reason<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Why there was no fasting on Sunday.</i> is that Christians<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">So M and H. V has ‘worshippers of stars’, an alteration made because of the censor.</i> should not say, ‘Because we rejoice on the Sunday they fast on it’. The Sages, however, said: In the period of the <i>ma‘amadoth</i> they were not concerned with the hatred of the Christians, but the reason is that it is written <i>He ceased from work wayyinnafash</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 31, 17, E.V., <i>and rested</i>.</i> [which is homiletically interpreted] <i>wai ‘al nefesh</i>, ‘Woe for [the loss of the additional] soul’.
On Passover we read from the section of the Festivals in Leviticus;<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 22, 26—XXIII, 44.</i> on Pentecost <i>Seven weeks;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 16, 9ff. The present practice is to read <i>ibid.</i> XV, 19—XVI, 17 on the second day of Pentecost; and if it falls on a Sabbath to begin from XIV, 22.</i> on New Year <i>In the seventh month on the first day of the month;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 23, 23ff.</i> on the Day of Atonement <i>After the death</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVI.</i> On the first day of Tabernacles we read from the section of the Festivals in Leviticus,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXII, 26—XXIII, 44.</i> and on the other days of Tabernacles the section of the offerings of the Festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 29, 12ff, on each day the corresponding offering.</i>
On <i>Ḥanukkah</i> we read the section of [the dedication of the altar by] the princes;<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 7. On the first day verses 1-17 are read; on the next six days the corresponding offerings; and on the eighth day VII, 54—VIII, 4.</i> on <i>Purim, Then came Amalek;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 17, 8-16.</i> on the New Moon, <i>And in your new moons;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 11-15.</i> at the <i>ma‘amadoth</i> the account of the creation.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 1—II, 3, because it is on account of the Temple service that the heaven and earth were created.</i> On the fast days of the Ninth of <i>Ab</i> and the last seven days in connection with droughts,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the shutting up of the rains’.</i> we read the section of the blessings and curses;<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 26, 3-46.</i> but on other fast days <i>And Moses besought</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 32, 11-14, XXXIV, 1-10.</i> and we read as the <i>hafṭarah Seek ye the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Isa. 55, 6—LVI, 8.</i> Some say that no <i>hafṭarah</i> is to be read, but the people have adopted the custom of reading the <i>hafṭarah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H omit ‘but’ and ‘of reading the <i>hafṭarah</i>’.</i> There must be no break in the section of curses because it is not proper to end with curses and to begin with curses.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. above XII, 1.</i>
On Mondays and Thursdays and at the Sabbath afternoon service the regular portion of the week is read, and this is not reckoned as a part of the reading [for the succeeding Sabbath].<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">When it must be repeated.</i> [The reason<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the various readings prescribed above.</i> is] because it is stated, <i>And Moses declared unto the children of Israel the appointed seasons of the Lord</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 23, 44.</i> which implies that it is an ordinance that each [appointed section] shall be read in its season. Hence [the Rabbis] said: [The sequence of the readings] is interrupted for them all—for New Moons, <i>Ḥanukkah, Purim</i>, fasts, <i>ma‘amadoth</i> and the Day of Atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Rule 2.</i> irrespective of whether these occur on a Sabbath, a Monday or Thursday, on which days there exists a regular sequence of readings.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘which they read according to order’. H (so N.Y.) has ‘on which they do not read’, etc., changing <i>shehem</i> to <i>she’ain</i>.</i> This sequence is interrupted<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘that they interrupt’.</i> and the [special sections] are read. You cannot, however, say of fasts or <i>ma‘amadoth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Which can never occur on the Sabbath.</i> that they would fall on a Sabbath in order [to lay down the rule that] the normal sequence of the Sabbath [readings] is interrupted on their account. [For these can interrupt the sequence] of a Monday or Thursday only.
When a New Moon falls on the Sabbath, the eighth person who has to read <i>And on the sabbath day</i> and <i>And in the beginnings of your months</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Rule 3.</i> also reads, as the <i>hafṭarah, Thus said the Lord God: The gate of the inner court that looketh toward the east</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 46, 1. The present custom is to read Isa. 66. The <i>hafṭarah</i> prescribed here is included in that of <i>parashath haḥodesh</i>, which is read only once a year, on the Sabbath of the New Moon of <i>Nisan</i> or, if that falls on a week-day, on the Sabbath preceding it.</i> If [the New Moon] falls on a Sunday, he<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The eighth person called to the reading.</i> reads on the Sabbath preceding the New Moon <i>And Jonathan said unto him: To-morrow is the new moon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 20, 18-42.</i> On the New Moon itself, three persons read the section of the continual offering. The first person reads, <i>And … spoke</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 1.</i> <i>Command</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 2.</i> <i>And thou shalt say</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 3.</i> The second person reads, <i>The one lamb</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 4.</i> <i>And the tenth part</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid. 5</i>.</i> <i>It is a continual burnt-offering</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid. 6</i>.</i> <i>And the drink-offering thereof</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 7.</i> <i>And the other lamb</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 8. This verse is included by GRA and M.</i> The third person repeats and reads, <i>It is a continual burnt-offering, And the drink-offering, And the other lamb, And on the sabbath day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 9. This and the following verses are added by GRA.</i> <i>This is the burnt-offering of every sabbath</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 10.</i> [The reason for this arrangement is] because [the Rabbis] ruled that it is not permitted to read less than three verses in a section nor<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘and some say’.</i> is it permissible to leave unfinished less than three verses at the end of a section.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The sections under discussion are the following: Num. 28, 1-8, 9f, 11-15.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The text according to GRA is followed.</i> If two verses were read,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As with the section, <i>And on the sabbath day</i> (Num. 28, 9f), which consists of only two verses.</i> and another section was begun,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">e.g., <i>And in the beginnings of your months</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 11-15).</i> there must be no stop before three verses of the latter were read. And since [the third person] reads, … <i>burnt-offering, And the drink-offering thereof, And the other lamb, And on the sabbath day, This is the burnt-offering of … sabbath</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 6-10.</i> it follows that the third person reads five verses,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H have ‘four’.</i> while [the next] one reads the whole section of the New Moon, <i>And in the beginnings of your months</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 11-15.</i>
On the New Moon it is necessary to say after <i>May the glory</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 104, 31. The reference is to the collection of verses beginning with these words (cf. P.B., pp. 28f) or to the whole of Ps. 104 which is added now to the daily Psalm at the end of the morning service of the New Moon.</i> the following: <i>The Lord reigneth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 93.</i> <i>O sing unto the Lord a new song;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 98. From ‘on the New Moon’ to ‘ a new song’ is the reading of GRA. V, M and H begin with ‘but’, omit ‘on the New Moon’, and conclude with ‘according to the words of the Scribes’.</i> and after that, <i>O give thanks unto the Lord, call upon His name</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">1 Chron. 16, 8-36.</i> and the six daily psalms;<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Pss. CXLV to CL.</i> for<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">So according to M. V and H read ‘and’.</i> R. Jose said, ‘May my lot be among those who include in their daily prayers<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘those who pray’.</i> those six psalms’. It has also been stated: Why did David mention Abraham, Isaac and Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘priests, Levites and Israelites’.</i> in the psalm beginning, <i>O give thanks unto the Lord, call upon His name?</i> Because it has to be said daily.

Chapter 18

Why has it been ordained that the psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">So H while M has the singular; it is lacking in V.</i> [prescribed for the Temple service] are to be said<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At the present time when no sacrifices are offered.</i> on every New Moon?<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As in Temple times when they were recited while the continual sacrifice was being offered.</i> [This is done in accordance with the view of] R. Simeon b. Laḳish. He enquired of R.
