Joḥanan whether it was permissible to recite the psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘song’.</i> in the absence of libations, and he replied, ‘We may deduce the answer from the following statement: On this and that [occasion]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The destruction of the first and second Temples.</i> the Levites stood on the platform<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sunday immediately following the respective destructions (‘Arak. 11b, Sonc. ed., p. 65).</i> and recited, <i>And He hath brought upon them their own iniquity</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 94, 23. As this Psalm was prescribed normally for Wednesdays (in connection with the sacrifices and libations of the day) but was said on these two occasions on the Sunday (for which a different Psalm had been arranged), it follows that the recital of a particular Psalm is independent of the sacrifices or libations of the day. It was ordained, therefore, that the Psalm for the New Moon, originally prescribed for the Temple service, should also be said at the present time (cf. N.Y.).</i> R. Simeon b. Laḳish asked him, ‘Which is first, the psalm<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Which was said in connection with the morning continual offering.</i> or the additional sacrifice?’<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Of the New Moon, if the Psalm had been delayed until the time of the additional sacrifice had arrived.</i> He replied,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA.</i> ‘It is obvious that the psalm is read first’.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since it belongs to the earlier part of the service.</i> For this reason<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the ruling above that the Temple Psalm of the New Moon should continue to be said even at the present time.</i> the people have adopted the custom of saying psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">As in the Temple, although no sacrifices are now offered.</i> at their proper times;<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed for the Temple service.</i> as we have learned in the Mishnah:<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Tamid VII, 4 (Sonc. ed., pp. 37f).</i> ‘These were the psalms which the Levites used to recite in the Temple: On Sunday<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the first day’. Monday is called ‘the second day’ and so on.</i> they used to recite, <i>The earth is the Lord’s, and the fulness thereof; the world, and they that dwell therein</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The quotation represents Ps. 24, and the following quotations denote the respective Psalms in which they occur.</i> On Monday, <i>Great is the Lord, and exceedingly to be praised</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 48.</i> etc. On Tuesday, <i>God standeth in the congregation of God</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 82.</i> etc. On Wednesday, <i>O Lord, Thou God to whom vengeance belongeth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 94.</i> etc. On Thursday, <i>Sing aloud unto God our strength</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 81.</i> etc. On Friday, <i>The Lord reigneth; He is clothed in majesty</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 93.</i> On the Sabbath, <i>A psalm, a Song. For the sabbath day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 92.</i> [which is the psalm and song also]<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">So in Tamid <i>loc. cit.</i>, but omitted in V.</i> for the hereafter, for the day which will be wholly a Sabbath and [will bring] rest in life everlasting’. For whoever makes mention of a Biblical verse at its proper time is regarded [by God] as though he had built a new altar and offered a sacrifice upon it.
On <i>Ḥanukkah</i> [the psalm is] <i>I will extol Thee, O Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 30.</i> On <i>Purim</i> it is <i>Shiggaion of David</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 7.</i> but others say it is <i>O God, keep not Thou silence</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 83. This is inserted by GRA but lacking in V, M and H.</i> On the first day of Passover it is, <i>Praise ye the name of the Lord</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 135. V, M and H quote as an alternative Ps. 83.</i> and the same [is said during] the intermediate days of the Festival; but on the first [two] days of Passover one must [also] say <i>May the glory of the Lord</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 104, 31. The reference is to the collection of verses beginning with these words (cf. P.B., p. 28).</i> while all the people stand. Then<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘Then’ to ‘for ever’ is the reading of GRA. The order of the words in V, M and H is confused.</i> they sit and say all the prescribed psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., pp. 20-33.</i> up to <i>And blessed be His glorious name for ever;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 72, 19; but the reference is to the doxology (P.B., p. 33) which concludes the morning Psalms.</i> but on the last day of Passover the Great <i>Hallel</i> is said. And what is the Great <i>Hallel?</i> [42<i>b</i>] <i>O give thanks unto the Lord, for He is good … O give thanks unto the God of gods</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 136.</i> The people have adopted the custom of saying the Great <i>Hallel</i>, although this is not the proper thing to do.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The proper time to say it is ‘when the appetite is satisfied and the stomach is full’ (Ta‘an. 26a, Sonc. ed., p. 135) and not in the morning service before food has been tasted.</i>
On the Feast of Weeks<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Pentecost.</i> [the psalm is] <i>Ascribe unto the Lord, O ye sons of might</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 29.</i> On the ninth day of <i>Ab</i> [the reading consists] of four verses of Jeremiah, <i>Hast Thou utterly rejected Judah?</i> to <i>For Thou hast made all these things</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 14, 19-22.</i> and the following two psalms, <i>O God, the heathen are come into Thine inheritance</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 79.</i> and <i>By the rivers of Babylon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 137.</i> Although it is usual<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in every place’.</i> to give precedence to the Holy Words<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘words of holiness’, the Hagiographa.</i> over the words of [prophetical] traditions,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Prophets forming the second division of the Scriptures.</i> in this case the latter<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Jeremiah, although it is included in the Prophets.</i> takes precedence over the former.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Psalms, which are included in the Hagiographa.</i>
Some [congregations] read the Book of Lamentations in the evening<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Of the Ninth of <i>Ab.</i></i> while others postpone it to the [following] morning after the reading of the Torah, when the reader<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">GRA.</i> stands up, his head covered with ashes, his clothes torn,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Like a mourner, the mourning being for the destruction of the Temples.</i> and reads it with weeping and lamentation. If he is able to translate it, well and good; but if he is unable he entrusts it to one who knows how to translate properly and [that person] does the translation, so that the rest of the people, the women and children may understand it; in that women are obliged to listen to the reading of the Book, how much more so does it apply to men.<br>Women have similarly the duty to read the <i>Shema‘</i>, the [<i>‘Amidah</i>] prayer and the Grace after meals, and to have a <i>mezuzah</i> [on their doors]. But if they are not acquainted with the holy tongue they are to be taught [to say them] in any language which they can understand and master. From this it was deduced that the man who recites the benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In his home.</i> must raise his voice for the benefit of his young sons, his wife and daughters. It is, therefore, a logical deduction that every section of the Torah and the Prophet [prescribed for the readings] of the Sabbath should be translated for the people, the women and the children after the reading of the Torah. This is why it was ruled: On the Sabbath the people come [to the Synagogue] early and depart late. They come early to read the <i>Shema‘</i>, like the pious men of old, with the first glimpse of the sun; and they depart late to listen to the interpretation of the weekly section. On a Festival, however, they come late, because they have to prepare the food for the day;<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Which is permitted on a Festival when not the Sabbath.</i> and they hasten to depart because it is unreasonable to interpret [the Torah or the Prophet] for them, it having been stated: Rab did not appoint an interpreter from [the beginning of the first day of] the Festival to the conclusion of the second day.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘its companion’. Rab’s reason was that, owing to the legitimate indulgence on the Festival days, his mind might not be clear to impart the proper instruction or to give the necessary guidance to the public through the interpreter. Similarly it is implied that, in order to enable the people to enjoy the festivities of the day, they are given the opportunity of departing at an earlier hour.</i>
There was likewise a beautiful custom in Jerusalem to train<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA, M and H.</i> their young sons and daughters to afflict themselves on a fast day:<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Although the duty of fasting the whole day did not apply to them.</i> at the age of eleven to the middle of the day, at the age of twelve the full day, and at the age of thirteen [the boy] was taken round<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘and after that he carried him’.</i> and presented to every elder to bless him and pray for him that he may be worthy to study the Torah and engage in good deeds. In like manner,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This is an insertion of GRA, omitted in V, M and H.</i> everyone who had a man superior to him in the town would rise from his place and go and bow down to him [requesting] that he pray on his behalf. This is meant to teach us that they<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The men of Jerusalem.</i> were splendid men and their deeds were splendid, and that their hearts [were directed] towards Heaven. Furthermore, they did not leave their young children behind but took them to the Synagogue to encourage them in the performance of the precepts.
On the day when R. Eleazar b. ‘Azariah was appointed head of the academy, he began his discourse by saying: <i>Ye are standing this day all of you … your little ones, your wives</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 29, 9f.</i> the men came to listen, the women came to receive a reward for their journey,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘steps’. Even if the women did not understand the discourse, they were entitled to reward for their attendance and bringing their children with them. The corresponding passage in Ḥag. 3a (Sonc. ed., p. 9) reads ‘men came to learn, women came to listen’.</i> but why did the little ones come? That a reward may be given to those who bring them. From this the young daughters of Israel learnt the practice of coming to the Synagogue so that a reward may be granted to those who bring them while they would [in due course]<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">When they reach womanhood.</i> also receive their reward.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">As the result of their early training and practice. In childhood one is subject neither to reward nor punishment.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This is the continuation of Rule 4 above.</i> The reader on the Ninth of <i>Ab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by M and H, omitted in V.</i> says,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Before the reading of the Book of Lamentations, or before the reading of the section of the Torah.</i> ‘Blessed … the true Judge’.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 292. This blessing is said on a calamity or hearing bad news.</i> Some place the Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">M reads ‘the case of the Torah’.</i> on the ground in a black wrapping and say, <i>The crown is fallen from our head</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Lam. 5, 16.</i> The reading and the lamentations are carried out as with a man whose dead lies before him. Some change their [customary] places [in the Synagogue]; others descend from their benches, and all of them roll in ashes and do not greet one another throughout the night and also throughout the day until the people have finished their lamentations. During the recital of the lamentations it is forbidden to speak [any irrelevant] word, or to go outside [the Synagogue], lest one’s mind be diverted from the mourning; and it is obvious that no conversation with a Gentile is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since the conversation would have no relevance to the destruction of the Temples.</i>
In the evening service<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">On the ninth of <i>Ab.</i></i> no one can hear the other, because all pray in a whisper;<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As a mark of mourning for the destruction.</i> therefore we say neither ‘Bless ye’<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">‘Bless ye the Lord Who is to be blessed’ which is addressed to the congregation at the beginning of the evening service (cf. P.B., p. 96).</i> nor ‘[Blessed] be the name’.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Which was exclaimed before ‘Bless ye’ but is not now said.</i>

Chapter 19

In the morning service,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Of the ninth of <i>Ab</i>, the services of which were treated in the previous chapter.</i> however,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The contrast is with the ruling in XVIII, 8 where it was stated that in the evening service ‘Bless ye’ is not said.</i> after the psalms and the Scriptural verses, [the reader] in the <i>Yoẓer</i> benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 37.</i> says ‘Bless ye’ in a low voice; but neither ‘Holy’<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ḳedushshah</i>, ‘Holy, holy’, etc. (P.B., p. 39).</i> nor <i>ḳaddish</i> is to be recited. In the afternoon prayer, however, both ‘Holy’<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 94<small>C</small>.</i> and <i>ḳaddish</i> are said.
<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following Rule continue the interrupted regulations on the selection of psalms for various occasions begun in XVIII, 2f.</i> And on the New Year we say, <i>O clap your hands, all ye peoples;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 47.</i> on the Day of Atonement <i>Bless the Lord, O my soul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 104.</i> and <i>Out of the depths have I called Thee, O Lord;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 130.</i> on the Festival of Tabernacles <i>In Judah is God known;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 76.</i> on the Eighth Day [of Solemn Assembly] <i>For the Leader … on the Sheminith</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 6.</i> If one is not conversant with these psalms, he says I <i>will give thanks unto the Lord with my whole heart</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 9.</i> and that suffices.
On Passover, both in the [<i>‘Amidah</i>] prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 228.</i> and over the cup of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Ḳiddush</i>, P.B., pp. 230f.</i> one must mention ‘On this Festival of holy convocation, this day of the Festival of Unleavened Bread’; and during the intermediate days of the Festival ‘This Festival of Unleavened Bread’.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. V and M read ‘this so and so Festival’.</i> On the seventh day one says<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>‘Amidah</i> and <i>Ḳiddush</i>.</i> ‘On the Seventh Day of Solemn Assembly’, but no mention is made of ‘Festival’ since it is not an independent Festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The seventh day is part of the Passover.</i>
On the Feast of Weeks<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Pentecost.</i> one says ‘On this Festival of holy convocation and this day of the Feast of Weeks’. The evening, morning and afternoon services consist of the same prayers.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As on Passover except for the change in the name of the Festival.</i>
On the New Moon of <i>Nisan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">And similarly on the other New Moons. <i>Nisan</i> is named because it is the first of the months of the year.</i> one must mention<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>‘Amidah</i> (P.B., p. 50).</i> ‘On this day of the New Moon’; and on the New Year ‘On this Festival of holy convocation,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">V, M and H add ‘and on this day of New Moon’. GRA omits because this addition is no longer customary.</i> this day of the New Year, this Day of Memorial, a day of blowing the <i>Shofar</i>’, concluding with<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The benediction (P.B., p. 242).</i> ‘King over all the earth, Who sanctifiest Israel and the Day of Memorial and the Seasons’.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. The reading in V is confused.</i>
On the Day of Atonement no mention is made<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>‘Amidah</i>.</i> of ‘on the Festival Day’, because there is no Festival on a fast day; but one says, ‘On this day of holy convocation, on this day of the Fast of the Tenth,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Day of Atonement is observed on the tenth of <i>Tishri</i>.</i> on this day of pardoning of iniquity’, concluding with<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 258.</i> ‘pardonest and forgivest our iniquities and the iniquities of Thy people Israel in mercy, and grantest remission for their transgressions, King over all the earth, Who sanctifiest Israel and the day of the Fast of Atonement, and the Seasons and the holy convocation’; but no mention is made of Festival and joy since there is no joy without par-taking of food.
As we laud [God] on the first day, the intermediate days and the last day<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Added by GRA; omitted in V and M. The reading of H is: ‘As we laud Him on the New Year and Day of Atonement, so we laud Him on the Festivals’.</i> of the Festival, so we laud Him [on the other] Festivals in the morning,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>Yoẓer</i> prayer which begins the morning benedictions before the <i>Shema‘</i>.</i> afternoon and evening prayers.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>‘Amidah</i>.</i> And how is He lauded! ‘Our God and the God of our fathers, do Thou make the glory of Thy kingdom manifest upon us’.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 234.</i> This is followed by, ‘We pray Thee, O Lord our God,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">So H. V reads ‘our God name’.</i> may … rise and come,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 228.</i> etc. Bestow upon us’;<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i>, p. 229.</i> and the conclusion of the prayer is, ‘Blessed art Thou Who sanctifiest Thy people Israel, the Festival of joy, the Seasons and the holy convocation’.
As the concluding words [of the benedictions] of the New Year and the Day of Atonement are different from those of other Festivals, so are the prayers of these days; and we do not include ‘remembrance’<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">‘Remember us unto life’, etc. (P.B., p. 239).</i> either in the first three<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">‘Blessed art Thou … the holy God’ (<i>ibid.</i> pp. 44f).</i> or last three<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">‘Accept O Lord … with peace’ (<i>ibid.</i> pp. 50-54).</i> [benedictions of the <i>‘Amidah</i>] except only on the two Festival days of the New Year and Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>ibid.</i> pp. 239, 256.</i> and, even with these, permission was given with difficulty.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Because, as a rule, no break is allowed in these benedictions.</i> If the New Year falls on a Sabbath, one does not say ‘A day of blowing the <i>Shofar</i>’ but ‘a day of remembrance of blowing the <i>Shofar</i>’, because the blowing of the <i>Shofar</i> does not take precedence of the Sabbath in country towns,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Outside Jerusalem. This was permitted in the Temple only (Rashi). According to Maimonides the permissibility extended to all Jerusalem.</i> this being a preventive measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">One might carry the <i>Shofar</i> on the Sabbath through a public domain to an instructor to take lessons in the art of blowing it.</i>
On the New Moon,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Which occurred on the 31st day of the preceding month. The New Moon was celebrated either on the 30th day of the preceding month (if witnesses testified in time that they saw the new moon in the evening sky) or on the 31st if no reliable witnesses were forthcoming.</i> the associations of elders, leaders and students were assembled in session<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">V inserts in brackets ‘on the first day’. They assembled in the academy where a ritual feast was held in honour of the declaration and sanctification of the New Moon.</i> from the time of the afternoon service onwards until the sun had set and the moon appeared in the night [sky].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H omit ‘and the moon … sky’.</i> It is necessary to say [after the ceremonial meal] in the benediction over the wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A cup of wine was used for the Grace after meals.</i> ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who createst the fruit of the vine. Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe, Who hast revealed the secret of the renewal of the moon, Who didst instruct and teach us<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘them’.</i> the [courses of] the seasons, New Moons and Festivals<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the version of GRA. The text in V is confused and varies with that of M and H which also disagree with each other.</i> and Who didst appoint men of understanding who determine the times [of the New Moons and Festivals]. Thou, our Rock, hast weighed and calculated the minutest divisions of time whereby those New Moons and Festivals are determined, as it is written, <i>Who appointedst the moon for seasons; the sun knoweth his going down</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 104, 19.</i> and it also states, <i>For as the new heavens and the new earth, which I will make</i>’;<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 66, 22.</i> and one concludes with ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who sanctifiest Israel and the New Moons’. [Then one says,] ‘O give thanks unto the Lord for He is good; on this day we all in Jerusalem rejoice and are happy in the All-present. May Elijah the prophet soon come to us; may the king Messiah cause to spring forth in our days the [happy] time like the years<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">So M; V wrongly ‘in the years’. H reads: ‘that he may appoint the days like the years’.</i> when the Temple will be rebuilt, and may joyous occasions multiply in Israel’; and the people answer and say ‘Amen’. [The prayer is then continued,] ‘May good tidings multiply in Israel, may happy days multiply in Israel, may disciples of the Torah multiply in Israel. Consecrated is the New Moon, consecrated at the beginning of the month, consecrated in its proper time, consecrated in its intercalation,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the addition of the 30th day to the preceding month by declaring the 31st day to be the New Moon and the first day of the new month.</i> consecrated according to<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">So M and H. V wrongly ‘in’.</i> the Torah, consecrated according to the <i>halakah</i>, consecrated in the celestial regions, consecrated in the terrestrial regions, consecrated in the land of Israel, consecrated in Zion, consecrated in Jerusalem, consecrated in all the habitations of Israel, consecrated by the order of our Rabbis, consecrated in the House of Assembly. O give thanks unto the Lord for He is good’. Then [the reader] says, ‘You are all blessed’. After every benediction, moreover, [the reader] says, ‘O give thanks unto the Lord for He is good’, except after the benedictions of the Levites.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Who omitted ‘for He is good’ (cf. its omission in 2 Chron. 20, 21). GRA reads ‘the mourners’ instead of ‘the Levites’ (cf. Rule 12 below). N.Y. also had this reading but considers the phrase ‘except after’, etc., superfluous.</i>
When he<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the one who presides over the assembly which is consecrating the new moon.</i> sings the praises of God he does so in the presence of the twelve notable men<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘good’.</i> of the town and twelve <i>ḥaberim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Members of an association scrupulously observant of the laws concerning ritual purity. The translation accords with the text of GRA and H.</i> corresponding to the twelve tribes, the twelve months and the twelve constellations.
Although [in the Grace after meals the prayer] ‘May … rise and come’<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">P.B., p. 282.</i> has been said in [the benediction concluding with] ‘rebuildest Jerusalem’ and all<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and he finishes’.</i> the benediction of <i>zimmun</i> has been said over the cup<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">So M and H. V reads ‘all the cup’.</i> [of wine], that cup being passed on to one’s wife<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">To partake of its contents.</i> to fulfil that which is stated, <i>To cause a blessing to rest on thy house</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Ez. 44, 30. Cf. ‘his house is his wife’ (Yoma I, 1, Sonc. ed., p. 1). The wife is the mainstay of the home.</i> nevertheless another cup of wine is brought over which is said the benediction ‘Who createst the fruit of the vine’ and afterwards ‘Who didst reveal’, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">As in Rule 9 above (GRA). V has the meaningless text: ‘which is a circle all of it’.</i> The same rule also applies<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and so you say’.</i> to the benediction of bridegrooms and the benediction of mourners,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">These benedictions, too, must be said over a cup of wine other than the one used for the Grace after meals.</i> because two sanctifications<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The benedictions whereby the sanctifications are declared: that for the Grace after meals, on the one hand, and the benedictions mentioned on the other.</i> must not be pronounced over the same cup. Our Masters have adopted the practice of saying the benediction of bridegrooms<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">V adds ‘and the benediction of mourners’, which is omitted by M and H. The benediction of mourners is dealt with in Rule 12 below.</i> in the morning over a cup of wine in the presence of ten men; and [in the presence of] new guests all the seven days;<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Of the wedding festivities. If no new guests arrive, the benedictions are said only on the first day.</i> and similarly in the evening after<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘before’.</i> the meal.
With regard to the benediction of mourners on the Sabbath, when mourning is suspended, it is said after the statutory service in the presence of the worshippers over a cup of wine; for R. Eliezer b. Hyrḳanos said:<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This follows GRA. V, M and H read: ‘in the evening after the prayer in the presence of the worshippers over a cup on the Sabbath when mourning in public is suspended according to R. Eliezer b. Hyrḳanos who said’. The text is clearly out of order.</i> Solomon saw the greatness<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘power’.</i> of those who bestow lovingkindness, and built two gates for Israel, one for bridegrooms and the other for mourners and excommunicated persons. On the Sabbath the inhabitants of Jerusalem used to congregate, ascend the Temple Mount and take their seats between these two gates to show kindness to these persons. Since the Temple was destroyed it was enacted that bridegrooms and mourners should go to the Synagogue so that kindness could be shown to them: to bridegrooms by recounting their praises and escorting them to their homes. To mourners [by comforting them as follows:] After the reader finishes the <i>musaf</i>, everyone goes behind the doors of the Synagogue, which are in front of<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V omits ‘everyone’ and inserts ‘or’ before ‘in front’. [H has the reading <i>pinnath</i>, ‘the corner of’.]</i> the Synagogue, and there meets the mourners and all their relatives. The benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For the mourners.</i> is said followed by <i>ḳaddish</i>. The version ‘In the world that will be renewed in the time to come’<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This is an enlarged <i>ḳaddish</i> which is now recited by sons after the interment of a parent (P.B., pp. 321f).</i> is only said after the study of<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘over’.</i> the Talmud and a Rabbinic discourse.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘exposition’.</i>

Chapter 20

The benediction over the moon must not be said except at the conclusion of the Sabbath when one is in an exhilarated mood<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">After the happiness of the Sabbath day.</i> and well dressed. [43<i>a</i>] He directs his eyes towards the moon, places his feet together and pronounces the benediction ‘Who<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Preceded, as all benedictions, by ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the universe’.</i> didst create the heavens by Thy word and all their hosts by the breath of Thy mouth. Thou didst set for them a fixed law and time that they should not change their ordained task. They are glad and rejoice to perform the will of their Possessor.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA and M; omitted by V. H adds it in the textual notes.</i> They act loyally, for their<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA, M and H. V has ‘for his’.</i> action is truth. To the moon Thou didst say that it should renew itself with a precious light and a diadem of beauty for those who have been sustained from the womb<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A reference to Israel (cf. Is. 46, 3).</i> who, like it, will be renewed in the future and glorify their Creator for the majesty of His kingdom. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who hallowest the New Moons’.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">H reads ‘hallowest Israel and the New Moons’; the reading of GRA is ‘Who renewest the months’.</i>
Then one says three times, ‘An auspicious omen, an auspicious omen, shall it be for all Israel. Blessed be He Who formed thee, blessed be Thy Maker, blessed be thy Possessor, blessed be thy Creator’. Turning in the direction of the moon,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘towards it’.</i> he jumps three times and exclaims three times, ‘As I jump towards thee but do not<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">M reads ‘and cannot’ which is the reading in the ritual.</i> touch thee, so if others should jump against me to hurt me may they not touch me. Let terror and dread fall upon them and may this be retrospective, Amen, Amen, Selah. Praise ye the Lord’. He then says ‘Peace’ three times to his fellow and goes home with a cheerful heart. That is [the conclusion of the regulations] concerning the interruptions for New Moons.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. XVII, 2f where the regulations for the New Moon began and, with many interruptions, insertions and amplifications, continued to the end of this Rule.</i>
What are the regulations respecting <i>Ḥanukkah?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘how do we interrupt for <i>Ḥanukkah</i>’, a reference to XVII, 2 where <i>Ḥanukkah</i> was first introduced in this set of regulations with the statement, ‘The sequence … is interrupted for … <i>Ḥanukkah’</i>.</i> The Rabbis said: On the twenty-fifth day of <i>Kislew</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The ninth Heb. month on the twenty-fifth of which the Festival begins.</i> the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp is kindled,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This takes place on the preceding evening, the night belonging to the following day.</i> and it is forbidden to use an old lamp;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If it is an earthen lamp, because the burnt oil renders it unsightly.</i> but if a person has only an old lamp, he must thoroughly heat it in fire;<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">To burn away the old oil, after which the lamp may be used.</i> nor may [the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp] be moved<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘lifted’.</i> from its place before it is extinguished.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Carrying the lamp about while it is burning might cause suspicion that one is making a profane use of it; but the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> light, commemorating the miracles which led up to the celebration (cf. Rule 5), is sacred, is only to be looked at and must not be used for any other purpose.</i>
The commandment to kindle it extends from sunset until the last person has left<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the foot ceases from’.</i> the street.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The light is intended to serve as a public demonstration, but at such a late hour there are no passers-by to see it.</i> And, although there is no actual proof,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">That the lighting must take place after sunset.</i> there is an allusion to it: <i>The pillar of cloud by day</i> [<i>and the pillar of fire by night</i>,] <i>departed not</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 13, 22, which indicates that the pillar of fire which, like the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp, was intended to shed light, appeared in the night only</i> If it was lit in daytime, one must derive no benefit from it, nor may the benediction be said over it; for so [the Rabbis] said: No benediction is to be pronounced over the lamp before one enjoys its light. There is no need, however, to be concerned about changing the wick before it has been used up.
It is a religious precept to place the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp by the door which is near to the public domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">So that the light is seen from the street.</i> in such a manner that the <i>mezuzah</i> should be on the right hand and the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp on the left, to fulfil what is stated, <i>How fair and how pleasant art thou</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 7, 7.</i>—<i>how fair</i> with the <i>mezuzah</i> and <i>how pleasant</i> with the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lamp. The number<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA, wanting in V, M and H.</i> of lights [for each night] is in accordance with the ruling of the School of Hillel,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">One light on the first night, two on the second, and so on, until eight lights burn on the eighth and last night of the Festival.</i> because in sacred matters one should proceed to a higher grade<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Adding lights on the succeeding nights.</i> but not descend to a lower one,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As would happen if the number were to be reduced from eight on the first night to one on the last in accordance with the view of Beth Shammai.</i> [and also because the number of lights should] correspond to the days [of the Festival] as they pass.
In what manner are the benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Over the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> lights.</i> said? On the first day, the person who kindles [the lamp] says three benedictions while the onlooker says two. The person who kindles says, ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Inserting after ‘Lord’, as in all benedictions, ‘our God, King of the universe’ (P.B., p. 274).</i> Who hast sanctified us by Thy commandments and hast given us command to kindle the light of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>’. Then<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">After the three benedictions: the one just given and the two mentioned below.</i> he says, ‘We kindle these lights on account of the deliverances and the miracles and the wonders which Thou didst work for our fathers, by means of Thy holy priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Maccabees who were a priestly family.</i> During all the eight days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> these lights are sacred, and it is not permitted to make any profane use of them but we are only to look at them, in order that we may give thanks unto Thy name for thy wonders, Thy miracles and Thy deliverances’. [The third benediction is] ‘Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who hast kept us in life’.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Concluding ‘and hast preserved us, and enabled us to reach this season’ (P.B., p. 274).</i> [Before this] he says, ‘Who wroughtest miracles for our fathers’.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Beginning, as usual, ‘Blessed art … universe’ and concluding ‘in the days of old at this season’ (P.B., p. 274).</i> These benedictions are for the person who kindles the lights, but the onlooker says on the first day only two benedictions: ‘Who hast kept us in life’ and ‘Who wroughtest miracles’. From that day onwards the person who kindles the lights says, ‘To kindle’ and ‘Who wroughtest’, while the onlooker only says ‘Who wroughtest miracles’.
The precentor must say, ‘Thou art holy’<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the <i>ḳedushshah</i>. It appears that, instead of beginning with ‘We will sanctify’ (P.B., p. 45) or ‘We will reverence’ (P.B., p. 160), there was a custom to begin with ‘Thou art holy’ on Sabbath and Festivals. Rule 7 extends this practice to some of the less important days as specified below. It has also been suggested to read, instead of <i>’attah ḳadosh</i> (‘Thou art holy’) <i>’eth haḳḳedushshah</i> (‘the <i>ḳedushshah</i>’).</i> on the eight days<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘in the eighteen [benedictions]’.</i> of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, and similarly on New Moons and on the intermediate days of a Festival,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">viz. Passover and Tabernacles.</i> because <i>beside</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA, omitted by V, M and H.</i> <i>the continual burnt-offering</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 15 (on the New Moon); <i>ibid.</i> 24 (on the intermediate days of Passover); and <i>ibid.</i> XXIX, 19, 22, 25, 28, 31, 34 (on those of Tabernacles).</i> is written in connection with them. And R. Hiyya learnt: On any day on which there is a musaf <i>ḳedushshah</i> is said except on <i>Ḥanukkah</i> on which <i>ḳedushshah</i> is said although there is no <i>musaf</i> on it, because <i>Hallel</i> is included [in the service]. Some maintain [that it is said] on <i>Purim</i> also, because the scroll of Esther is read on it.
In the benediction for Thanksgiving<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Beginning ‘We give thanks’ (P.B., p. 51).</i> we include [on <i>Ḥanukkah</i>] ‘and thanks for the wonders and salvation of Thy priests which Thou hast wrought in the days of Mattathias, son of Joḥanan, High Priest, and the Hasmoneans his sons. So also, O Lord our God and God of our fathers, perform for us miracles and wonders, and we will give thanks unto Thy name for ever. Blessed art Thou, O Lord, Who art all-good’.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The present version of this form of thanksgiving is longer and differs in wording (P.B., p. 52).</i> The miracles [of the days] of Mordecai and Esther are also mentioned [on <i>Purim</i>] in the benediction for Thanksgiving. Both<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Those for <i>Ḥanukkah</i> and <i>Purim</i>.</i> are likewise mentioned in the Grace after meals.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In the second benediction (P.B., p. 281).</i>
The whole <i>Hallel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Including Pss. CXV, 1-11 and CXVI, 1-11 (P.B., pp. 220f).</i> is recited during all the eight days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>. During [the recital of] the first two<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘three’.</i> chapters one may not return greetings, and there is no need to say that one must not give greetings. During [the recital of] the last two chapters one may give greetings out of fear;<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">To a person who can cause harm in his resentment at not having been greeted.</i> during the two middle chapters<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of GRA. V and M have ‘and return greetings for the sake of peace and out of respect’. H omits the text of V and GRA.</i> [greetings may be given] for the sake of peace or out of respect. The following are the first two chapters: <i>Praise, O ye servants of the Lord</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 113.</i> and <i>When Israel went forth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXIV. V, M and H adds Ps. 115 as the third.</i> The two middle ones are: <i>Not unto us</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXV; ‘the two middle … <i>us’</i> is the reading of GRA.</i> and <i>I love</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXVI.</i> The last two are: <i>O praise the Lord</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXVII.</i> and <i>O give thanks unto the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXVIII.</i> It is also necessary to say a benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">‘Blessed art Thou … to read the <i>Hallel</i>’ (P.B., p. 219).</i> before them and read them melodiously. For R. Simeon b. Jehoẓadaḳ said:<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, H and M read ‘learnt’ using a term which refers to a Mishnah, whereas the quotation is from the Gemara in Ta‘an. 28b (Sonc. ed., pp. 150f).</i> On eighteen days and one night the individual reads the whole <i>Hallel</i> and they are: the eight days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, the eight days of Tabernacles,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Omitted by V and M, but added by GRA and H in agreement with Ta‘an. <i>loc. cit.</i></i> the Festival of Solemn Assembly, the first Festival day of Passover and the night preceding it.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. Pes. V, 7 (Sonc. ed., p. 327) where it is described how the <i>Hallel</i> was recited during the sacrifice of the Paschal lamb on the eve of Passover.]</i> In the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. all countries outside the land of Israel where two days are observed as holy instead of the first day of Tabernacles, two days for the Festival of Solemn Assembly, and two days each for the first and seventh day of Passover.</i> [the whole <i>Hallel</i> is read on] twenty-one days and two nights. The most proper way of performing the commandment is to read the <i>Hallel</i> psalms on the two nights [of Passover] in the Diaspora in the Synagogue,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to reading them at home in the course of the <i>Seder</i> ritual. ‘In the Synagogue’ is inserted by GRA but omitted by V, M and H.</i> to say the benediction over them, and to recite them melodiously to fulfil what is stated, <i>And let us exalt His name together</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 34, 4.</i> When, however, one reads [the <i>Hallel</i>] at home,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">During the <i>Seder</i>.</i> there is no need to say the benediction because it has already been said in the congregational service.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘among many’. The Service in Synagogue precedes the <i>Seder</i> in the home.</i>
A scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">M and H read ‘a scroll of the Torah’.</i> is then taken out<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the Synagogue Service during <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> and we read in the section of the princes,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 7.</i> <i>And he that presented his offering the first day was;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 12-17.</i> and likewise for each day [the corresponding section of the offering] of that day,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and so for each day its day’, the number of the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> day corresponding to the respective number of the days of the offerings of the princes.</i> until the eighth day. On the Sabbath [during <i>Ḥanukkah</i>] one reads, <i>And it came to pass on the day that Moses made an end</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 7, 1.</i> up to <i>so he made the candlestick</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VIII, 4.</i> On the eighth day he reads from <i>On the eighth day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 54.</i> through all the sections<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Of the offerings of all the princes.</i> until <i>so he made the candlestick</i>. On the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V and M read: ‘and so on the eighth day until <i>And this was the work of the candlestick</i> (Num. 8, 4) all the verse’. H has a different text.</i> one also reads as the <i>hafṭarah, Thus all the work … was finished</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. in the passage 1 Kings 7, 51—VIII, 21.</i>
During the eight days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> we read the following one day earlier:<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘we advance to say’. To the prescribed reading of the day that of the following day is added.</i> <i>On the second day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 7, 18-23. This is added to the reading of the first day and then repeated on the second, and similarly with the rest.</i> <i>On the third day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 24-29.</i> <i>On the fourth day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 30-35.</i> <i>On the fifth day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 36-41.</i> <i>On the sixth day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 42-47.</i> <i>On the seventh day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 48-53.</i> <i>On the eighth day</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 54-59.</i> so as to complete ten verses.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Which is the minimum required for the reading of the three persons who are called to the Torah each day of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i>
When the New Moon of <i>Ṭebeth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Which always occurs during <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> falls on a Sunday, three persons read on the first day<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Of the New Moon, which is observed sometimes on one day and sometimes on two.</i> in the passage of the New Moon first, while the fourth reads the passage of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, because that which is more frequent takes precedence,<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The New Moon occurs every month, <i>Ḥanukkah</i> once a year.</i> and so [the reading for the New Moon] precedes the other. On the second day, however, the order is not the same, but three persons read the passage of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> while the fourth reads that of the New Moon, because on the previous day the section of the New Moon had been read first.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read: ‘because the reckoning of the month is from the second day only when the years are regular’.</i> [This is the procedure] because R. Isaac Seḥorah asked R. Isaac Nappaḥa, ‘Which do we read [first]<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the second and third scrolls of the Torah. In the first the regular portion of the week is read.</i> when the New Moon of <i>Ṭebeth</i> falls on a Sabbath?’ [He replied,] ‘In the section of <i>kalloth Mosheh</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">E.V. <i>Moses had made an end</i> (Num. 7, 1). The extent of the reading is given in Rule 10 above.</i> while the <i>mafṭir</i> reads<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the third scroll.</i> the passages relating to the Sabbath and the New Moon.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 9-15.</i> If it falls on a week-day, on the first day [of the New Moon] three persons read [the passage] for the New Moon, the fourth reading that of <i>Ḥanukkah;</i> because that which is more frequent takes precedence. As, however, the section of the New Moon was read first,<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">On the first day of New Moon.</i> three persons are to be called up on the second day for the reading of the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> section,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the first scroll. Only two scrolls are taken out on the week-days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.</i> while the fourth reads<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the second scroll.</i> the section of the New Moon, viz. <i>And in your new moons</i> to the end of the section.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 28, 11-15.</i> Why is this so?<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Why is the order of the reading reversed on the second day as compared with the first?</i> Because it is only on account of the <i>musaf</i> of New Moon<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">On the other week-days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i> there is no <i>musaf</i>. This argument cannot be applied to the first day of New Moon because it is the second day which begins the month, the first being the last day of the old month.</i> that a fourth person is added.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Rule 12 incorporates nearly the whole of XII, 7.</i> Behold [we have concluded the regulations respecting] the interruption which is made for the sake of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. XVII, 2f.</i>

Chapter 21

[Reverting to the sequence of the readings which] is interrupted for <i>Purim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. XVII, 2 where the subject was begun. Having been suspended in favour of other Festivals, it is here resumed.</i> It is<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA and H. V and M read ‘according to’.</i> the custom of our Rabbis in the West to afflict themselves by fasting the three days of the fast of Mordecai and Esther<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Esth. 4, 16.</i> intermittently, viz. after <i>Purim</i> on the Monday, Thursday and Monday.
And why do we not fast in the month of <i>Nisan?</i> Because on the first of <i>Nisan</i> the Tabernacle was erected<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ex. 40, 2.</i> and twelve princes offered their sacrifices during [the first] twelve days, a day for each tribe,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 7, 1ff.</i> and everyone held a festival on his day.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Between the twelfth of <i>Nisan</i> and the fifteenth, on which Passover begins, only two days intervene; therefore they, too, are regarded as festival days. The Passover ending on the twenty-first (in the land of Israel) or on the twenty-second (in the Diaspora), only eight or nine days of the month remain. As by far the greater part of the month was made up of festival days, the entire month is treated as a festival season in which fasting is prohibited.</i> Furthermore, in the time to come the Sanctuary will be rebuilt in <i>Nisan</i>, to confirm what is stated, <i>There is nothing new under the sun</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 1, 9.</i>
For this reason<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">That most of the month contained festival days.</i> no supplications<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This refers in particular to the prayer, ‘And He, being merciful’, etc. (P.B., pp. 57-65).</i> are offered on any of the days of <i>Nisan</i> and there is no fasting until <i>Nisan</i> has passed. The only exception is [the fast of] the firstborn who fast on the eve of Passover.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In thanksgiving for the deliverance of the Israelite firstborn on Passover eve when Egypt’s firstborn perished (Ex. 12, 29).</i> The very pious also fast on that day on account of the unleavened bread, in order that they shall begin to eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">On the evening preceding the fifteenth of <i>Nisan</i>. Lit. ‘that they shall enter it’.</i> with relish.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In honour and appreciation of the commandment.</i> Scholars fast on Mondays and Thursdays [throughout the year] on account of the desecration of the name of God<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">By the heathens who destroyed Jerusalem.</i> and for the honour of the Temple which was burnt.
To what does the foregoing apply? To fasting in private, but it is forbidden to proclaim a public fast until <i>Nisan</i> has passed.<br>On the first day of <i>Adar</i> a public proclamation is made regarding the shekels.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Every adult had the obligation to give one half-shekel as a contribution to the Temple.</i> Why on the first of <i>Adar?</i> Because it was foreseen, revealed and known before Him Who spoke and the world came into being that Haman would in time to come weigh [shekels] against Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Esth. 3, 9.</i> He, therefore, forestalled him and instructed Moses that the shekels of Israel should precede Haman’s. It is required that Israel shall give their shekels before [43<i>b</i>] the Sabbath of Remember;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Sabbath before <i>Purim</i> when the <i>mafṭir</i> reads the passage beginning <i>Remember</i> (Deut. 25, 17-19).</i> and it is forbidden to say of them<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The half-shekels.</i> that they are ‘for a ransom’ [but must be given] for a freewill-offering. From the proceeds water and food should be supplied for their poor brethren on account of a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <i>ḳorban</i> with GRA. V has ‘on account of complaints’ (<i>ḳoblin</i>). [M has <i>ḳislin</i> which Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>, p. 531, renders ‘feast’.]</i> and gifts to the poor.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Esth. 9, 22.</i> Some supply bread and wine, others supply bread and fish; in any event not less than two gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since Esth. <i>loc. cit.</i> speaks of <i>gifts</i>, in the plural, which indicates not less than two.</i> should be given, although they consist only of wheat and beans.
<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The translation of this Rule follows the reading of GRA. V, M and H read: ‘On the fourteenth and fifteenth of Adar, tables of <i>Ḥaberim</i> (“associates”, i.e. men who observed levitical purity) were set up (for changing foreign currency into half-shekels); in the villages on the fourteenth and in the walled cities on the fifteenth’.</i> In villages and towns [the <i>Megillah</i>] is read on the fourteenth [of <i>Adar</i>], and in walled cities<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Those that, like Jerusalem, had been walled in the days of Joshua the son of Nun (cf. Meg. I, 1).</i> on the fifteenth of the month.
We read<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">On <i>Purim</i> in the morning service.</i> from <i>Then came Amalek;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 17, 8-16.</i> and although it consists of only nine verses,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">While the general rule is that a reading must consist of not less than ten verses (cf. Rule 7).</i> we do not begin<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the third person called to the Torah.</i> <i>Now Jethro … heard</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 18, 1, etc., which follows the section beginning <i>Then came Amalek</i>.</i> but the first [to be called up] reads three verses, viz. <i>Then came Amalek … And Moses said … So Joshua did;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVII, 8-10.</i> the second reads <i>And it came to pass, when Moses held up his hand … But Moses’ hands … And Joshua discomfited;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 11-13.</i> the third reads <i>And the Lord said … And Moses built … And he said: The hand upon the throne of the Lord: the Lord will have war</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14-16.</i> The scroll of the Torah is then put back in its place, and <i>Blessed be the name of the Lord from this time forth and for ever</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 113, 2.</i> and <i>ḳaddish</i> are said;<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">V inserts here in parentheses, as also M and H, ‘For there is no reading [of the Torah] which requires <i>ḳaddish</i> until after he restores [the scroll in the ark] except that of the Sabbath alone on account of the <i>mafṭir</i> and over the Prophet. After the scroll of the Torah is put in its place one says <i>ṭaddish;</i> and why <i>ṭaddish?</i> Only to teach’. GRA transposes this to the end of the Rule in an emended version.</i> because neither on New Moons, nor fast days, nor Mondays and Thursdays, nor on the intermediate days of a Festival, nor the eight days of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, nor <i>Purim</i> is <i>ḳaddish</i> said until after the Torah scroll had been restored to its place, while the people stand and respond ‘Amen, let His great name’, etc.,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. P.B., p. 75.</i> with devotion and [still] standing. Furthermore, you have no reading that requires a <i>ḳaddish</i> before [the scroll] is put back except that of Sabbath and Festivals alone, [when it is said] before the <i>hafṭarah</i> is read in the Prophet.
R. Huna said: For three readings in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. when only three persons are called up to the reading of a section, viz. on a Monday or Thursday or at a Sabbath afternoon service.</i> not less than ten verses must be allotted.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Three verses for each of the first two and four for the third.</i> Hezekiah<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A Rabbi of that name.</i> explained [the reason:<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Why ten verses are required.</i> The number] corresponds to the Ten Commandments. But have we not learned in a Mishnah:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Ta‘an. IV, 3 (Sonc. ed., p. 137).</i> ‘On Sunday [the men of the <i>ma‘amad</i> read] <i>In the beginning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 1-4.</i> and <i>Let there be a firmament</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 5-8.</i> although they contain not more than eight verses?<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">How then could R. Huna rule that a minimum of ten verses was required?</i> R. Iddi said: Kahana and Ashë differ [on the method of procedure],<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As how to make ten verses out of eight.</i> one holding that [the reader] goes back, and the other maintaining that he cuts.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the continuation.</i> According to him who holds that ‘[the reader] goes back’, he repeats two verses;<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The second person repeats the last two of the three verses which the first had read. The two, together with the eight, make up the minimum of ten verses.</i> and according to him who maintains that ‘he cuts’, <i>And there was evening and there was morning</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The second part of each of verses 4 and 8 of Gen. 1.</i> is a verse on its own.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">So that verses 4 and 8 represent two verses each, bringing the number to ten.</i> But have we not learned in [the same] Mishnah: ‘On Monday [the reading of the <i>ma‘amad</i> was] <i>Let there be a firmament</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 6-8, three verses.</i> and <i>Let the waters … be gathered together’?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 9-13, five verses.</i> Now, according to him who holds that ‘[the reader] goes back’, he goes back over two verses, but according to him who maintains that ‘he cuts’, even if he does so, there are not ten<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Added by GRA, omitted by V, M and H.</i> verses!<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">There would only be nine, since <i>And there was evening</i>, etc., forms the whole of verse 13 and cannot be reckoned as two verses as in the case of verses 5 and 8.</i> R. Polippa b. R. Pariṭ raised an objection before R. Jonah,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">To R. Huna’s ruling.</i> ‘Behold the section of Amalek!’<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 17, 8-16, read on <i>Purim</i>, which consists only of nine verses.</i> ‘This is different’, he replied, ‘since<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA, M and H. V reads: ‘he said, No, it is taught, it’.</i> it is the prescribed portion for the day’.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This reply could also have been given to the preceding objection, since in Gen. 1, 6-13, if only verse 8 is divided into two, there would be nine verses, as in the section of Amalek.</i> R. Eliezer b. R. Maḳom raised an objection:<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">To R. Huna’s ruling.</i> [It has been taught,] ‘He who reads the <i>hafṭarah</i> from the Prophet shall read not less than twenty-one verses’,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Meg. 23a (Sonc. ed., p. 140). From ‘but [if R. Huna’s ruling … to the last one]’ is added by GRA. V omits it but inserts in brackets: ‘It is permitted to skip in a Prophet but not in the Torah, because if [a reader] skipped even one verse by not reading it, and restored the scroll [to the ark] and recited <i>ḳaddish</i>, he must unroll it again, say the benedictions, and read that verse and the two verses which follow it’. This is a repetition of XI, 2, 6.</i> but [if R. Huna’s ruling] is valid, twenty-three verses should be required, ten for the first three,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In accordance with R. Huna’s ruling above that ‘for three readings … no less than ten verses must be allotted’.</i> ten for the second three, and three for the last one!<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since a minimum of three verses must be read for each person who is called up. No reply is given to the objection, but it is possible to provide the following explanation: Only when no more than three persons are called up is it necessary to allot ten verses, but when the number of persons is more than three, as, e.g., on the Sabbath when not less than seven persons must be called to the Torah, three verses for each person or twenty-one in all are sufficient.</i>
Where the custom is to read the <i>Megillah</i> on two days<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The fourteenth and fifteenth of <i>Adar</i>.</i> it should be so read, [and where it is customary to read it] in the night and in the day, it should be so read; all depends on the practice of the country. Furthermore, all Israel must read it in the holy tongue.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In Hebrew.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">From this point to the end of the tractate is an aggadic addendum.</i> <i>The greatest man among the Anakim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 14, 15.</i><i>—among the Anakim</i> refers to our father Abraham whose height was equal to that of seventy-four men; his eating and drinking were of a similar proportion, equal to those of seventy-four men; so too his strength. What did he do?<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This is probably a reference to Gen. 25, 6, <i>But unto the sons of the concubines … Abraham gave gifts; and he sent them away … eastward, unto the east country</i>.</i> He removed the sixteen<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA in accordance with Gen. 25, 2-4. V, M and H incorrectly ‘seventeen’.</i> sons of Keturah, built for them a walled city of iron and settled them in it. The sun never penetrated into it because it was exceedingly high, so Abraham handed to them disks of precious stones and pearls of which use will be made<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read, ‘and they will be used’.</i> in the hereafter when the Holy One, blessed be He, will cause the sun and moon to be confounded, as it is written, <i>When the moon shall be confounded, and the sun ashamed</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 24, 23.</i> because use will be made of these [disks as luminaries].<br>Og<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">King of Bashan; Deut. 3, 11.</i> is identical with Eliezer,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Abraham’s servant; cf. Gen. 15, 2, XXIV, 2ff.</i> and [he was so huge that] he could hide Abraham’s feet in the palm of his hand. Once he was rebuked [by Abraham] and from fright his tooth fell out. Abraham picked it up and made ivory beds of it in which he slept. Others say that he made of it a chair which he used<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and sat in it’.</i> all his life. Who gave him to Abraham? Nimrod.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in Gen. 10, 8ff.</i> Og went and built sixty cities, the smallest of which was sixty miles high, as it is stated, <i>Threescore cities, all the region of Argob</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 3, 4.</i> And what did he eat? A thousand oxen and the same [number of] other animals, and his drink consisted of a thousand measures. A<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V, M and H read ‘and what was the’.</i> drop of his semen weighed thirty-six pounds.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">V and H add ‘and so for all generations’, which makes no sense.</i><br>It was taught: What did our father Jacob do when his sons brought him the coat [stained] with blood?<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Gen. 37, 31ff.</i> He did not believe them at all. Whence do we infer this? For it is written, <i>But he refused to be comforted</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 35.</i> because no consolations are acceptable for a living person. One, however, who is dead passes naturally from the mind,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he is forgotten from the heart of his own accord’.</i> as it is stated, <i>I am forgotten as a dead man out of mind</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 31, 13.</i> What then did he do? He proceeded to make a test with sheaves,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">GRA transposes the order of V, M and H by putting the test of the sheaves before that of the stones, and omits ‘according to the first opinion’.</i> writing upon them the respective names of the tribes, their constellations and the months, and said to them, ‘I order you to prostrate yourselves before Levi because he wears the Urim and Thummim’,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ex. 28, 30.</i> but they did not stand up. ‘Before Judah who is king’, but they did not stand up; but when he mentioned Joseph to them, they all stood up and bowed before Joseph. But it was not yet quite clear that he was alive. So Jacob went to the mountains, hewed twelve stones, arranged them in a row, and wrote on each the name of its tribe, the name of its constellation and the name of its month. On one stone he wrote ‘Reuben, lamb, <i>Nisan</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The name of the tribe, constellation and month.</i> and similarly on every stone. He began from Simeon and said to them, ‘I order you to stand up for Reuben’, but they did not stand up. ‘For Simeon’, but they did not stand up. ‘For every tribe’, but the stones did not stand up. As soon, however, as he mentioned the name of Joseph to them, they stood up at once and bowed before Joseph’s stone.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the text of GRA.</i> For this reason, all the tribes were written on Joseph’s stone. Similarly, all Israel are called by Joseph’s name, as it is stated, <i>Thou that leadest Joseph like a flock</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 80, 2 where <i>Joseph</i> is a synonym of all Israel.</i> So also all the heads of the families of the priests and Levites, [e.g.] <i>Eliashib</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Neh. 3, 1.</i> because of the phrase <i>lahashibo ’el ’abiw;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In the story of Joseph (Gen. 37, 22), E.V. <i>to restore him to his father</i>. The name Eliashib is broken up in three parts corresponding in sound and meaning to these three Heb. words. From ‘For this reason’ to <i>’abiw</i> is the reading of GRA. V and H have instead: ‘but from the <i>mishmaroth</i>, Eliashib the priest’.</i> <i>Elḳanah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Connected with <i>ḳanah</i>, ‘he bought’.</i> because Potiphar had bought him [as it is stated,] <i>And Joseph ms brought down to Egypt, and Potiphar … bought him</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 39, 1. V inserts in parentheses, <i>And Pharaoh called Joseph’s name Ẓaphenath, paneah</i>, etc. (<i>ibid.</i> XLI, 45), which has no relevance to the subject.</i><br>When Rebekah left her father’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 59ff.</i> she was three years old,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">V, M and H add ‘and three days’.</i> because it is customary among kings, when a daughter is born to them, to hear of it after three days;<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">H reads ‘after three years and three days’.</i> but as her father did not hear [of her birth] he did not defile her up to that time; and now a miracle happened to her in that her father died so that he should not defile her, as it is written, <i>Neither had any man known her</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 24, 16.</i> and by <i>man</i> only her father could be meant,<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘he did not’ to ‘meant’ is GRA’s reading. V, M and H read: ‘therefore a miracle happened to her that she should not be defiled’.</i> for such was the practice of the Arameans to lie with their virgin daughters after they were three years of age,<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">‘After … age’ is inserted by GRA; omitted in V, M and H.</i> and then to give them away in marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">V, M and H add: ‘on account of that which is written, <i>Neither had any man known her</i>, and by <i>man</i> only her father could be meant’.</i><br>Dinah was six years old when she bore Asenath from [her association with] Shechem,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Gen. 34.</i> corresponding to<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the number of’.</i> the six years which Jacob served Laban in payment for the flock,<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXI, 41.</i> thus completing<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘until’.</i> the twenty years of his service. [The Archangel] Michael then descended and took her away to the house of Potiphar.<br>From here onward let the man of understanding increase knowledge.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">From ‘completing the twenty years’ to ‘knowledge’ is GRA’s text. V and M have instead: ‘and he added twenty years, because he died. From this point onwards let the man of understanding increase knowledge. And Michael descended and led her to Potiphar’s house’.</i>
